The women-led investment teams banking on social returns

Amy Bell 
732 words
20 September 2018
04:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Backers of ventures that contribute to the social good often have a strong female contingent

As one of the first investors in Tesla Motors and NEXTracker, which was sold to electronics supplier Flextronics in 2015, Nancy Pfund has a record of spotting a promising trend. But when in early 2000 she saw the potential for investing in companies that combine financial returns with a positive social impact, the investors she was trying to convince were not so sure.

 We were pioneers,  says Ms Pfund, now managing partner of venture capital firm DBL Partners.  The typical reaction to our pitch was a negative one, we got a lot of doors slammed in our face,  she says.  People thought that by introducing something other than an economic driver into your investment decisions you would lose money. 

Today, demand for investments that incorporate environmental, social and governance standards, or impact investing, has grown substantially. In a recent study, Morgan Stanley compared the interest shown in sustainable investing by male and female clients. In 2017, 84 per cent of the women expressed interest, up from78 per cent in 2015; for men the figure was 67 per cent, up from 62 per cent.

Within this investment subcategory there is a relatively high representation of women compared with the broader VC field, says Ms Pfund, whether as managers or investors. At DBL, the two managing partners are one woman and one man; at general partner level, two of the five are women, including Ms Pfund.

As an emerging field of finance, there was an early opportunity for newcomers to make their way as practitioners, says Ms Pfund.  Women have not been as invested in the status quo as men 	. 	. 	. 	This, coupled with impact investing s reach into social and environmental domains, towards which many women gravitate anyway, has allowed the field to grow in a more diverse way from the get-go. 

DBL stands for  double bottom line : the aim is to achieve attractive financial returns for investors, while also delivering social, economic and environmental benefits to the sectors and regions in which they invest. One example is Farmers Business Network in the US, which uses big data to give farmers an insight into their supply chains and enables them to make changes that are both more sustainable and economical. Supporting farmers in this way helps rural Americans thrive on their farms, thus serving a social purpose, while the network has boosted employment.

Laurie Spengler, chief executive of Enclude, a UK-based specialist advisory intermediary in impact investing, says:  It s deeply satisfying to integrate what you care about with what you do professionally. 

An early adopter of impact investing, Ms Spengler was able to use her experience in development finance to move into the field quickly. In addition, as with any new movement, she says, the barriers to entry and career advancement for women are not so embedded.

The gender balance of staff at Enclude is just over 40 per cent female and, like MsPfund, she is struck by the relatively high number of women working in impact investment compared with other areas of investing.  We are intrigued by the role of finance in society and using it as a tool to create better outcomes,  she says.

Rachel Whittaker, a sustainable investment strategist at UBS, started her career as an analyst in an investment bank in 2000, where she was often the only woman in meetings.  When I made the move into sustainable investing it was a major change,  she says.

In the organisations she worked for over the past decade Ms Whittaker has noted the different gender splits in sustainable investment teams compared with traditional teams. Despite this, she sees the same trends as in other sectors, where the number of women drops the higher up the ranks you look.

Role models in the sector are important, says Ms Pfund.  We go out of our way to find women entrepreneurs,  she says.  We want to make the best investments, and if you leave out half the talent pool you re probably not going to do that. 

The firm has a strong deal flow coming from women, she says.  We don t really need to go finding them. They re finding us 	. 	. 	. 	because they know that some of the barriers that have existed have melted away.

Features
Women-led investment teams bank on social returns

Amy Bell 
739 words
20 September 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
5
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Impact investing Backers of ventures that contribute to the social good often have a strong female contingent, writes Amy Bell

As one of the first investors in Tesla Motors and NEXTracker, which was sold to electronics supplier Flextronics in 2015, Nancy Pfund has a record of spotting a promising trend. But when in early 2000 she saw the potential for investing in companies that combine financial returns with a positive social impact, the investors she was trying to convince were not so sure.

"We were pioneers," says Ms Pfund, now managing partner of venture capital firm DBL Partners. "The typical reaction to our pitch was a negative one, we got a lot of doors slammed in our face," she says. "People thought that by introducing something other than an economic driver into your investment decisions you would lose money."

Today, demand for investments that incorporate environmental, social and governance standards, or impact investing, has grown substantially. In a recent study, Morgan Stanley compared the interest shown in sustainable investing by male and female clients. In 2017, 84 per cent of the women expressed interest, up from 78 per cent in 2015; for men the figure was 67 per cent, up from 62 per cent.

Within this investment subcategory there is a relatively high representation of women compared with the broader VC field, says Ms Pfund, whether as managers or investors. At DBL, the two managing partners are one woman and one man; at general partner level, two of the five are women, including Ms Pfund.

As an emerging field of finance, there was an early opportunity for newcomers to make their way as practitioners, says Ms Pfund. "Women have not been as invested in the status quo as men ... This, coupled with impact investing's reach into social and environmental domains, towards which many women gravitate anyway, has allowed the field to grow in a more diverse way from the get-go."

DBL stands for "double bottom line": the aim is to achieve attractive financial returns for investors, while also delivering social, economic and environmental benefits to the sectors and regions in which they invest. One example is Farmers Business Network in the US, which uses big data to give farmers an insight into their supply chains and enables them to make changes that are both more sustainable and economical. Supporting farmers in this way helps rural Americans thrive on their farms, thus serving a social purpose, while the network has boosted employment.

Laurie Spengler, chief executive of Enclude, a UK-based specialist advisory intermediary in impact investing, says: "It's deeply satisfying to integrate what you care about with what you do professionally."

An early adopter of impact investing, Ms Spengler was able to use her experience in development finance to move into the field quickly. In addition, as with any new movement, she says, the barriers to entry and career advancement for women are not so embedded. The gender balance of staff at Enclude is just over 40 per cent female and, like Ms Pfund, she is struck by the relatively high number of women working in impact investment compared with other areas of investing. "We are intrigued by the role of finance in society and using it as a tool to create better outcomes," she says.

Rachel Whittaker, a sustainable investment strategist at UBS, started her career as an analyst in an investment bank in 2000, where she was often the only woman in meetings. "When I made the move into sustainable investing it was a major change," she says.

In the organisations she worked for over the past decade Ms Whittaker has noted the different gender splits in sustainable investment teams compared with traditional teams. Despite this, she sees the same trends as in other sectors, where the number of women drops the higher up the ranks you look.

Role models in the sector are important, says Ms Pfund. "We go out of our way to find women entrepreneurs," she says. "We want to make the best investments, and if you leave out half the talent pool you're probably not going to do that."

The firm has a strong deal flow coming from women, she says. "We don't really need to go finding them. They're finding us ... because they know that some of the barriers that have existed have melted away."

Good day. Today we have much more on the spinoff secondary fund that is buying out New Enterprise Associates' stakes in late-stage startups.

We also have more on Blumberg Capital expanding its team, particularly in Israel. And some VCs are targeting Medicare as a growth industry.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

A NewView for NEA: The NEA spinoff fund buying secondary stakes in NEA startups, called NewView, is seeking $1.37 billion for the fund. The fund, which has raised about $700 million so far, will invest in dozens of NEA's growth-stage companies, including 23andMe and Duolingo. Goldman Sachs and Hamilton Lane are among the investors in the fund. WSJ Pro's Yuliya Chernova has the exclusive.

The GitHub effect: GitLab Inc., which offers tools for software developers, is raising new funding and Iconiq Capital is expected to lead an investment that values the company at $1 billion or more, according to people familiar with the matter. The round was competitive, according to these people, in part because of the hope that GitLab could someday become an acquisition target. Earlier this year, Microsoft Corp. acquired a similar company, GitHub, for $7.5 billion. WSJ Pro's Katie Roof has the exclusive.

Blumberg's hires: San Francisco firm Blumberg Capital is boosting its Tel Aviv presence. Blumberg Capital is announcing six new members on its team, three of whom will help grow its presence in Tel Aviv. Ehud Schneorson, the former commander of Unit 8200, the intelligence unit of the Israel Defense Forces, is joining as a managing director. Also joining as a managing director is Yodfat Harel Buchris, who is also based in Tel Aviv. WSJ Pro's Katie Roof has the exclusive.

Mack's Take: Town Hall Ventures Raises $115 Million for Startups Tackling Underserved Populations

By Heather Mack

Few startups in the past have dared to target the giant insurer that is Medicaid and Medicare. But recently more startups are focusing on Medicaid or Medicare, and more venture capitalists are paying attention.

Town Hall Ventures, a Minnesota-based firm, has just closed on $115 million for its first fund specifically to back companies that help low-income or vulnerable patient populations. It's a rare move in health investing to focus exclusively on this population.

But the firm has expertise: one of the co-founders is Andy Slavitt, the former Medicare chief in the Obama administration.

More in Mack's Take.

Exits

Eventbrite Inc. priced its initial public offering at the high end of an elevated price range, giving the event ticketing company a market valuation of about $1.76 billion. The San Francisco company sold 10 million shares at $23 apiece, raising $230 million. Read more.

Amicus Therapeutics Inc. said it has agreed to acquire clinical-stage gene therapy company Celenex for $100 million in cash, giving the publicly traded biotechnology company entry into gene therapies. Rev1 Ventures was an investor in Celenex. Read more.

Total SA said Thursday that it has bought venture-backed electric-vehicle-charging company G2mobility, which manages almost 10,000 charging points through its services platform. Read more.

Gremlin Social, a provider of social-media marketing services for banks, has acquired VidVerify and Insight CRM. St. Louis-based Gremlin is backed by Cultivation Capital. VidVerify, also of St. Louis, is a video-based communication platform for mortgage lenders and is backed by SixThirty. Insight CRM provides social media management technology. Doug Wilber will join Gremlin as chief executive. Ben April, VidVerify's CEO, will become chief operating officer; while Josh Dennis, CEO of Insight CRM, will be the company's first chief technical officer.

New Money

Galera Therapeutics Inc. said it has raised $150 million through an equity funding round and a royalty purchase agreement to finance late-stage trials of its lead drug candidate. Read More

More funding deals:

Israel-based MeMed Diagnostics Ltd., which is developing a diagnostic blood test with the goal of reducing overuse of antibiotics, has raised $70 million in Series C financing. Ping An Global Voyager Fund, Foxconn, Caesara Medical Holdings, OurCrowd and other investors contributed. "http://www.me-med.com/"

Proterra Inc., a Burlingame, Calif.-based electric-bus maker, has raised $155 million in new funding. Daimler and Tao Capital Partners co-led the round, with participation from G2VP. "https://www.proterra.com"

Enigma Technologies Inc., a New York-based data analytics company, has secured a $95 million investment led by New Enterprise Associates. New investors including Capital One Growth Ventures and Glynn Capital participated in the round, along with previous backers Comcast Ventures, Crosslink Capital, Two Sigma Ventures and Partnership Fund for NYC. "https://www.enigma.com"

Livspace, an Indian home interiors and renovation startup, has completed a $70 million Series C round. TPG Growth and Goldman Sachs led the investment, which included participation from Jungle Ventures, Bessemer Venture Partners and Helion Ventures. "https://www.livspace.com/"

Silverback Therapeutics Inc., a Seattle-based startup developing treatments for cancer, fibrosis and infectious diseases, has closed a Series A extension financing, bringing the round total to $47.5 million. OrbiMed Advisors led the investment, with participation from Celgene Corp. and Alexandria Venture Investments. "https://silverbacktx.com"

YouDo.com, a Russian service marketplace for finding and hiring local contractors, has picked up a $17 million investment. Russian telecom operator PJSC MTS led the round, which included participation from current shareholders. Sistema Venture Capital, Flint Capital and United Capital Partner are among the company's backers. "https://youdo.com/"

ioTium, a Santa Clara, Calif.-based startup specializing in securing industrial internet-of-things devices or machinery, has wrapped up a $13.6 million Series B round. March Capital Partners led the funding, with participation from Honeywell Ventures, JC2 Ventures, Hanna Ventures, GE Ventures and Juniper Ventures. "https://www.iotium.io/"

Wove, a San Francisco-based marketing partnership software startup, has raised $9 million in new funding. August Capital led the round, and was joined by investors including Origin Ventures, Partech Partners, AngelPad and Tekton Ventures. Howard Hartenbaum, a general partner at August, has joined the company's board. "https://wove.com"

Empower, San Francisco-based mobile banking startup, has secured a $4.5 million funding round. Initialized Capital led the financing and was joined by Sequoia Capital. "https://empower.me/"

VC Funds

Early-stage investor Amplify Partners said it has raised $200 million for its third fund. The fund, Amplify Partners III LP, surpassed the size of its predecessor, which closed at $125 million in 2015. Read more.

Industry News

BuzzFeed is putting its podcasting ambitions on pause. The digital-media company is cutting its in-house podcast production team as it shifts resources away from audio and toward creating original shows similar to the ones it has produced for Netflix, Twitter and Facebook. Read more.

Neutron Holdings Inc., the dockless scooter startup better known as Lime, has challenged San Francisco's recent decision denying it a permit to operate in the city. In an appeal filed with the San Francisco Metropolitan Transportation Agency and the city attorney, Lime says agency officials were biased and didn't provide companies with evaluation criteria. In August, the city granted operating permits to only two scooter startups, Scoot Networks Inc. and Skip Scooters.

People

Atomico said it has hired Twitter Inc. executive Caroline Chayot as a partner, one of seven new appointments made by the European venture firm. Ms. Chayot was Twitter's head of people for Europe, the Middle East and Africa. Before that, she spent nine years working in human resources at Google. Read more.

Blumberg Capital is announcing six new members on its team, three of whom will help grow its presence in Tel Aviv.

Ehud Schneorson, the former commander of Unit 8200, the intelligence unit of the Israel Defense Forces, is joining as a managing director.

Also joining as a managing director is Yodfat Harel Buchris, who is also based in Tel Aviv. Ms. Buchris is an experienced investor who serves on the board of Israel Discount Bank Ltd., one of the country's largest banks. Idan Nurick will join as principal.

In San Francisco, Blumberg has hired Steve Gillan from Azure Capital Partners to be its chief operating officer and chief financial officer. Meanwhile, Andrew Van Nest is joining as an associate and Peter Cooney III as a data analyst.

Founder and managing partner David Blumberg said he is "pinching myself how happy I am that we've gotten these guys." He referred to his updated team as a "treasure trove of brain power."

Blumberg's fourth and most-recent fund is a $200 million vehicle. The firm writes early stage checks, typically between $500,000 and $5 million. Its investment categories include data, analytics and artificial intelligence.

Pro Cyber
Cyber Daily: Aflac's Patching Regime; Add Another 14M to the Breach Tally

3,016 words
19 September 2018
12:58
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Aflac's Weekly Patching Cadence Prioritizes Critical Vulnerabilities

By Jeff Stone

Patching is a basic part of cybersecurity hygiene, but the process is more complicated than it appears. Not patching security vulnerabilities, or doing it badly, can open the way for attacks.

Equifax Inc. revealed a breach in 2017 of data about 147.9 million people that happened, in part, when hackers took advantage of inadequate patching of the company's systems. Fifty-seven percent of companies that reported a data breach in the last two years said they hadn't installed a patch for a known vulnerability, according to an April survey of nearly 3,000 security professionals by the Ponemon Institute and the cloud-computing firm ServiceNow Inc.

Insurer Aflac Inc. takes patching seriously, going so far as to have some of its patching process approved by the board of directors, said DJ Goldsworthy, director of security operations and threat intelligence.

"When everyone knows the board and executives support and expect patching to occur, culture and approaches rapidly shift to support a streamlined process," he said in an email.

Monitoring software inventory. Aflac combs through its hundreds of applications with network-based vulnerability scanners, software that seeks out known vulnerabilities, and other third-party tools that check systems for security holes that need to be patched, said Mr. Goldsworthy. Aflac also has a four-person penetration testing team that uses the latest threat research from cybersecurity vendors and the U.S. Computer Emergency Readiness Team to try to infiltrate the company's systems. They seek vulnerabilities not found in the automated scans, such as application design flaws that could allow hackers to crack Aflac passwords.

Alerts about any potential vulnerabilities are sent to Aflac's security team in real-time via email.

Prioritize patch work. Security employees assign each vulnerability a priority level so the IT team understands the urgency with which they must administer a patch. Critical vulnerabilities, such as the 2014 Shellshock flaw in some widely used open-source software that could have allowed hackers to take control of web servers, must be fixed within seven days, said Mr. Goldsworthy said.

Aflac classifies vulnerabilities that could result in the theft of personal information or business secrets as high-severity. These must be fixed within 30 days, he said. Employees have up to 90 days to fix medium-severity issues, such as outdated encryption, he said.

Aflac resolves 98% of vulnerabilities within its deadlines, said Mr. Goldsworthy.

"It's important from a cultural standpoint for IT to embrace that as something that's inherent to their responsibilities, as opposed to something that security is dumping on them," he said.

Assign humans to oversee the process. Aflac assigns an individual or a team to oversee each patch. The overseer is accountable for delivery of the patch and coordinating the work of others who may need to be involved, said Mr. Goldsworthy.

After employees ensure patches are applied to the system, they oversee application and database teams that conduct tests to assess whether an updated application functions normally, he said.

Liaisons are assessed on their patching execution in performance reviews, he said.

"Security and IT are inexorably related, and its become increasingly important for the people to have those roles to be accountable for technology," he said.

Create a backup plan. It's not always feasible to fix a vulnerability within the prescribed time frame. Aflac this year missed its 30-day deadline to implement a patch for Meltdown and Spectre, flaws in Intel Corp. and other chips that power many computing devices.

Microsoft Corp. released a security upgrade for its systems quickly after Meltdown and Spectre were made public in January. But Aflac was unable to install the patch without it dramatically slowing down the company's systems, Mr. Goldsworthy said.

Microsoft warned at the time that some users of the Windows operating system would experience "significant slowdowns," and "a decrease in system performance" upon installing the patch.

It took roughly 45 days for Microsoft to release a patch Aflac could install without slowing down operations, Mr. Goldsworthy said.

While they waited, Aflac's security team studied the technical details of Meltdown and Spectre to assess how cybercriminals could exploit the bugs, he said. The team determined hackers were most likely to target employees with a phishing attack that directed the victim to a website containing malicious software. The team wrote code to disable malicious attachments in incoming email, said. Mr. Goldsworthy.

"We don't let ourselves off the hook because there's not a patch," he said. "A lot of times you can restrict access and take other steps to at least partially, if not fully, mitigate a vulnerability without a patch."

************************************

Hack Dissection: Basic Security Missteps at Government Payment Service

By Catherine Stupp and Jeff Stone

A data breach at Government Payment Service revealed personal information of 14 million customers of the online payment company, security blogger Brian Krebs reported Monday. Names, addresses, phone numbers and the last four digits of credit-card numbers dating back to 2012 were exposed through payment receipts on the website GovPayNow.com. Krebs reported that he informed the company of the breach last Friday. Government Payment Service said in a statement to WSJ Pro Cybersecurity that it changed its system so that users will only be able to view their own payment receipts.

- What was compromised: Information for about 14 million customers, including names, addresses, phone numbers and the last four digits of credit-card numbers, according to Krebs. A spokesman for Government Payment Service could not immediately confirm to WSJ Pro Cybersecurity the number of affected customers or the data that was exposed.

- How it happened: Krebs reported that the company used sequential customer records. He typed different digits into the web address and was able to access customers' payment receipts.

- What Government Payment Service says: A spokesman said in an email that the company "has addressed a potential issue with our online system that allows users to access copies of their receipts, but did not adequately restrict access only to authorized recipients. The company has no indication that any improperly accessed information was used to harm any customer, and receipts do not contain information that can be used to initiate a financial transaction."

The online payment service company is used by local and state governments around the country and was acquired in January by Securus Technologies, a company that provides telecommunications services to prisons. This spring, the New York Times reported that law enforcement authorities had improperly used Securus' systems to track crime suspects. Motherboard later reported about a breach of the company's database.

Jasson Casey, CTO & SVP Engineering at risk management company SecurityScorecard, and Chase Cunningham, principal analyst at Forrester Research Inc., told WSJ Pro Cybersecurity that the latest Government Payment Service breach is an example of basic security mistakes that companies could easily prevent.

Here are edited excerpts from the two separate conversations.

Q: This company has been hacked several times. Haven't they learned anything from the past?

A: Mr. Cunningham: The fact that this company was acquired by somebody else who also had data storage problems is a really good indication they're not focused on protecting data the way they should. It's like at daycare: If  & one [kid] is sick and then your kid gets sick and you get sick and everyone else does.

[In] the acquisition process, [Securus] should have asked [GovPayNet] if they've checked for vulnerabilities, if can they determine the controls based on the data [they're] required to store, do you have penetration testing and red teaming underway.

Mr. Casey: Application security is something that they still have a hard time with... They [the breaches] feel very similar, to be honest. It comes from a similar team, it comes from a similar mindset. I'm not sure what their focus is but it's probably not application security.

Q: Brian Krebs wrote that this kind of data exposure is easy to prevent, for example by encrypting some parts of the URL shown to users during payment. Is it really that easy?

A: Mr. Cunningham: That [encryption] is something that's done all the time. It's just another step in the process of when they were developing this application that they should have done. My guess is they didn't run through any tests that would find something more malicious in nature. A web application tester would have found that issue within 30 minutes.

I've seen people look for web application vulnerabilities by hiring penetration testing firms. People say it's too expensive, but they don't think about the expense of losing brand recognition and being known as a business that doesn't take security seriously.

Organizations need to just get moving and quit screwing around and actually understand you can't only use basic tests. You have to act and think like a malicious actor. If you're not finding problems, you're not doing the test right.

Mr. Casey: Yeah, this is really simple &This is just basic web development in terms of best common practices, to actually use HTTPs, to use secure sessions to actually have a session ID bound to your user. Good practice is to filter a view to where the user that's actually accessing the system is only getting data that is indeed bound for them.

Q: Some of the data that was exposed was from 2012. Six years seems like a long time to store this kind of data.

A: Mr. Cunningham: Obviously, there's a need to have data for some period of time for tracking. But to keep data for six years  & in today's day and age is ridiculous. All the data being stored there is a liability. In a perfect world, you keep the data for a foreseeable amount of time, like 18 months or two years. Then you store it in encrypted long-term storage, or you nuke it entirely.

Q: How easy was it for hackers to access so much data, and did they need to access the system for very long in order to expose so many millions of records?

A: Mr. Cunningham: The fact that Krebs is the one discovering it [is telling]. He's good but it's not like he was using a U.S. National Security Agency exploit. You just had to modify the URL and change a few things and you get all kinds of information. That's web security 101. You could have mandated security controls around URLs and nullified that risk. Ninety-nine percent of these breaches are simple failures, rather than something from a Jason Bourne movie.

Mr. Casey: The exploit actually looks like a fairly basic technique where the URL was basically being used as a way to craft delivery of any information. If you understand that a certain ID was a customer record locator or a payment record locator by manipulating that information and sending it back, you could then get access essentially to data that is not yours, receipts that are not yours. From a software development and security engineering perspective, that's certainly an interesting finding in terms of separation and partitioning of data visibility.

Q: What else strikes you about this hack?

A: Mr. Cunningham: Once they do forensic research, they will find all kinds of other threat activity over the years. Once [they] start digging, they'll find things from years ago, like open doors of data being exposed to the internet, that they didn't know before.

Mr. Casey: There are a lot of other questions that come up in this process that you can't really answer but they're certainly worth posing. If it's this easy to divulge the last six-plus years of receipts for any payment that's been processed through the company, what else is there? What does the inside look like?... There's no reason to do things like sequential IDs. You can do randomization of IDs ... Vulnerabilities aren't really the end all and be all, at the end of the day. It's really about the team and the process behind these companies and whether they have good teams, good processes, follow best common practices. That's a much stronger indicator of a company's posture and hygiene.

***************************************

Quotable:

"Trying to analyze that information is impossible for a human."

  Mathew Newfield, CISO at Unisys Corp., on the need for artificial intelligence to fight cybercrime.

***************************************

72 Hours to Report Your Hack

Global companies are tightening controls to more quickly detect and report data breaches, in part to avoid penalties under a sweeping set of regulations from the European Union. The magic number: 72 hours. The General Data Protection Regulation, which took effect in May, requires firms that collect data about European Union citizens to report a data breach to regulators within 72 hours of discovery. Fast turnaround is a challenge for some companies, writes WSJ Pro Cybersecurity's Jeff Stone.

Quick dialing and prewritten scripts. Companies are rehearsing their breach responses to identify who needs to be involved and what actions may need to be accelerated. Cisco Systems Inc. goes so far as to track how long it takes designated executives to dial in to a conference line to discuss a cybersecurity incident. It can take a few minutes or over an hour, depending on the number of people involved, Mr. Stone reports. If Options Clearing Corp. detects a breach originating outside the company, its crisis-communications team and customer-service group draw from a prewritten script to notify the external firm about the problem via email or phone calls.

*****************************************

How AI Can Help Stop Cyberattacks

Companies are using artificial intelligence in the fight against hackers, sorting through millions of malware files to identify characteristics that can indicate a new attack, writes WSJ Pro Cybersecurity's Adam Janofsky. More than 121.6 million new malware programs were discovered in 2017, according to German research institute AV-Test GmbH. That's about 231 new malware samples every minute. "Trying to analyze that information is impossible for a human," says Mathew Newfield, chief information security officer at Unisys Corp.

Parsing a barrage of alerts. About three years ago, International Business Machines Corp. started training its Watson AI system on cybersecurity, to help security teams manage the influx of threat information. The system combs through alerts, recognizes patterns and determines things such as whether a malware strain is related to previous attacks. Like most AI, the technology took years to develop and encountered bumps along the way. Watson at one point concluded that the word "it" was the name of the most dangerous malware, because it appeared so frequently in malware research, says Koos Lodewijkx, vice president and chief technology officer at IBM Security.

Tracking down enemies. A common struggle for data-breach victims is figuring out who attacked them. Security systems can analyze information on registries and online databases for clues about the infrastructure that criminals use to launch attacks, such as domain names of websites and IP addresses of devices. When hackers leave traces, "you create a behavior footprint that you leave behind that is unique," says Chris Bell, chief executive of Diskin Advanced Technologies. Customers in the aviation, utilities and financial services have used it to spot pending attacks and block IP addresses associated with criminal groups, according to Mr. Bell.

*****************************************

More Cyber News

Facebook sought data from financial companies. As recently as last year, Facebook Inc. pressed financial firms for the ability to use customer data flowing through its Messenger platform for a range of purposes, including advertising, the Journal's AnnaMaria Andriotis and Emily Glazer report. Facebook's negotiating stance with financial firms evolved over the years, ranging from asserting ownership of all data that passes through its servers to, later on, allowing financial firms to restrict its use of the information, WSJ reports. Facebook executives in 2016 told American Express Co. they wanted individualized cardholder spending data that passes through Facebook for a variety of purposes, according to WSJ. A Facebook spokeswoman told the Journal the company has not and does not use consumer financial data to target advertisements.

Mirai botnet authors go undercover. U.S. prosecutors this week asked a judge to sentence three men to work with the Federal Bureau of Investigation as part of a community service sentence for their role in a cybercriminal scheme, according to Wired magazine. Josiah White, Paras Jha and Dalton Norman pleaded guilty last December to creating the malicious software for the Mirai botnet, a network of hacked computers used to knock websites offline in 2016. The three men already have logged more than 1,000 hours of assistance in cybercrime investigations, Wired reported.

Debate over deterrence strategy unfolds. Cybersecurity experts and former prosecutors are divided over whether the U.S. Department of Justice's strategy of indicting hackers unlikely be tried in an American court is effective, CyberScoop reported. The U.S. this month indicted Park Jin Hyok, an accused North Korean hacker, for crimes related to the WannaCry ransomware attacks and other hacking campaigns. Mr. Park would be arrested if he was extradited, or if he was apprehended by another country, the site reported. A former U.S. National Security Agency analyst told CyberScoop the indictment "makes it look like the United States can't even bring people to justice that we charged." But a former federal prosecutor said the complaint reveals precious hacking techniques, forcing nation-state hackers to expend time and effort to develop new attacks, CyberScoop reported.

Cyberattack at U.K. airport. England's Bristol Airport was hit last weekend with a ransomware attack, according to BBC News. The airport pulled arrival and departure screens offline as a precautionary measure, instead using dry-erase boards to communicate information to travelers, the BBC reported. The attack didn't affect any flights or put passenger safety at risk, an airport spokesman told the BBC.

Facebook Sought Users' Financial Data for Years

By AnnaMaria Andriotis and Emily Glazer 
765 words
19 September 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Facebook Inc. had been haggling with financial firms over its access to users' sensitive financial information for years, well before the social-media company came under fire for its handling of personal data.

As recently as last year, the social-media company pressed financial firms for the ability to use customer data flowing through its Messenger platform for a range of purposes, including advertising, according to people familiar with the matter and documents reviewed by The Wall Street Journal. Concerned about privacy, several firms negotiated bespoke agreements that limited how Facebook could use any financial data that would pass through its servers.

The negotiations highlight a dilemma facing Facebook as it balances its need for detailed user data to better target advertisements and increase user engagement with concerns about how best to handle users' most sensitive personal information. It is an issue that has taken on new importance following the uproar over Facebook's ties to political analytics firm Cambridge Analytica, which accessed information on as many as 87 million users of the social-media network without their consent.

Facebook has in recent months taken steps to give users more control over data it collects. Earlier this year, the company changed its privacy policy to more clearly spell out how it handles advertising and user data. In April, before the European Union began enforcing its General Data Protection Regulation privacy law, Facebook asked users to review information about different types of advertising.

"Like many online companies, we partner with financial institutions to improve people's commerce experiences, like enabling better customer service, and people opt into these experiences," said Facebook spokeswoman Elisabeth Diana. "We've emphasized to partners that keeping people's information safe and secure is critical to these efforts. That has been and always will be our priority."

She said Facebook hasn't used and doesn't use consumer financial data for so-called ad targeting, or placing ads in front of specific audiences.

Facebook's negotiating stance with financial firms evolved over the years, ranging from asserting ownership of all data that passes through its servers to, later on, allowing financial firms to restrict its use of the information. Several of the deals were negotiated ahead of Facebook's 2017 developer conference, when over a dozen companies in multiple industries, including financial services, launched services on Messenger.

Prior to the launch, Facebook's privacy policy said "we use all of the information we have about you to show you relevant ads."

In conversations with American Express Co. in 2016, Facebook executives said they wanted to use individualized cardholder spending data that passes through Facebook, a person familiar with the matter said. That would have allowed them to use the data for ad targeting, the person said.

AmEx didn't want Facebook to use individualized data for anything besides servicing cardholders who opted into the chatbot, the person said, and instead agreed to provide aggregated transaction data for ads and other purposes.

After Bank of America Corp. reviewed Facebook's terms, it decided it would move its clients who engage in private messaging with the bank on Facebook off of the site so that Facebook wouldn't gain access to their financial data, according to a person familiar with the matter.

When Wells Fargo & Co. launched a chatbot pilot with Facebook in 2017, it informed customers that Facebook would have access to their Messenger conversations with bank representatives, per its data policy. "Customers were cautioned that they should not enter account numbers or other sensitive data in their chatbot conversations," a spokeswoman said. The 5,000-customer pilot ended in April.

PayPal Holdings Inc., which allows users of the payment service to send money through Messenger, negotiated a custom contract that prohibits Facebook from using PayPal customers' data for advertising or any of Facebook's own commercial purposes, the company said.

Western Union Co., which also launched a Messenger-based money-transfer service at the 2017 developer conference, has a custom deal that "reflects our privacy obligations to our customers," said spokeswoman Pia De Lima.

The deals predate a more recent Facebook effort, reported by The Wall Street Journal last month, to form partnerships with big banks to offer a range of customer services through Messenger, including fraud alerts. The company said it wouldn't use any information obtained through those services for ad-targeting purposes.

Banks have been hesitant to give too much control to Facebook.

---

Peter Rudegeair contributed to this article.

Investcorp to invest up to $150m in China tech fund

Javier Espinoza in London and Don Weinland in Hong Kong 
517 words
18 September 2018
04:54
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Bahrain-listed group joins global investors chasing fast-growing unicorns

Investcorp, a Bahrain-listed asset manager, will invest in a Chinese state-backed fund with holdings in some of the mainland s fastest-growing technology companies, joining a raft of global investors seeking to capitalise on the prospects for the sector in Asia.

The investment group has agreed to pour as much as $150m into the China Everbright LimitedNew Economy Fund, which is managed by China Everbright, a Hong Kong-listed financial conglomerate that is controlled by the Chinese state.

Investcorp, which is investing in China for the first time since the group was set up in 1982, could add an extra $100m of co-investment in the future in a portfolio of companies that includes Chinese artificial intelligence developer SenseTime and video streaming service iQiyi.

The decision to invest in China is partly  in recognition of the country having the world s highest number of internet users and being home to some of the fastest growing  unicorn  technology companies valued at over $1bn , Investcorp said in a statement to the Financial Times.

An official announcement is expected on Tuesday morning in London.

The Everbright fund has already received an aggregate commitment of $313m from other investors, and has made investments including Wish, a large, unlisted mobile ecommerce platform, and Yixia Technology, a short-form mobile video-sharing platform.

China has experienced a flurry of pre-initial public offering fundraising this year, with several large tech groups raising billions of dollars at high valuations. Ant Financial, the payments arm of Alibaba, for example, raised $10bn at a valuation of $150bn.

Investors from around the world have piled into these groups, drawn by the prospect of cashing out in an expected wave of upcoming IPOs. However, many of China s recent technology and consumer listings, such as ecommerce group Pinduoduo and electric carmaker Nio, have underperformed investor expectations.

In May, SenseTime, one of China s top AI start-ups, launched its second funding round in just two months, bringing in $1.2bn for the year and valuing the company at $4.5bn.

Investors in the Beijing-based group, whose facial recognition software is used by Chinese police, include Alibaba, Fidelity International, Hopu Capital, Silver Lake, Tiger Global, Singapore s state investment fund Temasek and Qualcomm.

Baidu-backed iQiyi, which has been compared with US video streaming company Netflix, filed for an IPO in the US this year and was valued by analysts at $15bn.

The Everbright fund is one of a number of investment vehicles launched this year to focus on technology in China. State-owned China Merchants Group launched the Rmb100bn ($15bn) China New Era Technology Fund, aimed at investing in technology, similar to SoftBank s $100bn Vision Fund.

China Everbright is a sprawling state-owned conglomerate whose holdings span banks, asset management and aircraft leasing, among others. In 2016 it bought a 65 per cent equity stake in Italian-owned media rights company MP & Silva.

Data specialist Enigma reels in investment group cash

Lindsay Fortado in New York 
438 words
18 September 2018
15:08
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Hedge funds and venture capital firms move into analytics businesses they use themselves

Hedge funds, banks and venture capital firms are piling into investments in data companies in the hope of cashing in on a business they are using a lot more themselves.

In recent years, there has been a proliferation of start-ups that trawl through reams of data and sell it to investment groups searching for an edge.

The latest to attract investor interest is Enigma, a New York-based start-up that received funding from sources including quant giant Two Sigma, activist hedge fund Third Point and venture capital firms NEA and Glynn Capital in a $95m capital raising announced on Tuesday.

Enigma collects a huge range of data including the contents of every container ship that arrives in US ports every week, aeroplane registrations, elevator inspection reports in California and data on amusement park rides in Arkansas.

Their clients include banks, who might use the information to decide whether to extend credit or as part of anti-money laundering compliance, insurance companies assessing risks and pharmaceutical companies looking at adverse patient reactions to drugs.

Two Sigma made an investment earlier this month in another data company, Crux Informatics, which cleans and structures data to make it easier to use. Goldman Sachs and Citigroup have already invested in the business.

Despite the interest from hedge fund investors, Enigma chief executive Hicham Oudghiri said the company will not focus on the sector to build its client base because hedge funds tend to want data for one-off investments, rather than providing a steady stream of business.  We re more interested in powering Fortune 500 companies,  he said.

Much of Enigma s data are gathered from Freedom of Information requests or direct inquiries to government agencies and the company is aiming to create one of the biggest single data resources, with multiple ways of analysing the information.

Greg Papadopoulos, a partner at NEA, which also led a previous round of funding for Enigma, said he thinks it could become a multibillion-dollar company. His firm reviews about 20,000 companies a year   a  good fraction  of which are now data companies   and will invest in around 30, he said.

Enigma stood out for the range of data it collects, he said.

 If you look at the way most people look at and use data, it s like using a spellchecker, but if you really want to find out more you should go to the dictionary,  he said.  Enigma has built the dictionary of data.

Good morning. Today's top stories may seem to highlight a distinctly masculine investment thesis, but headlines can be deceiving.

There's no mistaking the target market of startups like Hims and Roman Health Medical, which are raising hefty rounds in the name of men's health. You might have noticed a recent rash of ads for these platforms.

It's also true that NFL players are backing a startup that aims to speed up recovery from severe knee injuries, but they have a history of self-interested investments. And this orthopedics startup could help people off the field as well as on it.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Men's health is the new black: New York-based Roman Health Medical is the latest men's health startup to raise large venture backing. The company has raised $88 million in Series A financing led by FirstMark Capital, with SignalFire, Initialized Capital and General Catalyst participating. Roman Health focuses on online consulting and prescriptions for erectile dysfunction. Its West Coast rival, Hims Inc., also sells ED pills, in addition to skin care and hair-loss products. Launched out of Atomic Ventures, it has raised $97 million from 8VC, CAVU Venture Partners, Founders Fund and Forerunner. WSJ Pro's Heather Mack has more.

Now they are racing to one-up each other to appear to be the market leader. WSJ's Rolfe Winkler has more.

Block and tackle: Pro football players are financing a startup that could help athletes better recover from a devastating form of knee injury. The NFL Players Association, the union for the league's players, is joining a $22.5 million investment in startup Miach Orthopaedics Inc. after funding early research behind the company. The association has invested in a series of efforts related to health and safety, such as the Dementia Discovery Fund, a venture-capital fund investing in dementia treatments. WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley has more.

Chernova's Take: Why LPs Might Cheer a Leaner Kleiner Perkins

The departure of Mary Meeker's team from Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers surprised the industry.

Firm divorces are rare. Moreover, it's a contrarian status since most firms are adding more growth and sector funds under one roof.

Still, it may all work out for Kleiner Perkins.

Limited partners have been funding the more-is-better strategies of many venture firms, but many have done so reluctantly.

Many LPs regard the rise of sprawling VC firms as a benefit to GPs, which get to draw larger fees, rather than to the LP base. Managing multiple strategies can be distracting and rife with conflicts of interest, such detractors say.

While other firms get fatter, a Kleiner Perkins that's lean and focused seems an attractive alternative, said one fund-of-funds manager, who added that their firm is looking forward to evaluating a new fund from Kleiner Perkins with Mamoon Hamid as a key investor.

More in Chernova's Take.

Exits

Two private equity-backed businesses in the oil-and-gas software space have agreed to combine, continuing the spate of deals in the sector so far this year.

Drilling Info Holdings Inc., which gained a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/genstar-buying-majority-stake-in-drilling-info-from-insight-venture-1529435968?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=5"in Genstar Capital earlier this year, acquired Accel-KKR portfolio company Oildex.

Drilling Info Holdings, also known as Drillinginfo, is a software-as-a-service and data analytics provider to the energy industry. In August, Genstar closed a deal to acquire a majority stake in Austin, Texas-based Drillinginfo, with existing investor Insight Venture Partners retaining a minority stake.

Oildex provides software that automates invoices and payments for the oil-and-gas industry. The company had around $55 million in projected revenue for 2018, according to two people familiar with the deal.

Accel-KKR took a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFLBO0020140224ea2oi828r?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=2"in Oildex in 2014. The firm is reaping a return of more than four times its invested capital on the investment, according to a person familiar with the situation. As a result of the most recent deal, Accel-KKR will hold a minority equity stake in Drillinginfo, according to a release by the firm.

Over the course of Accel-KKR's owenrship, Oildex completed three add-on acquisitions, including the procure-to-pay business of Automatic Data Processing Inc. in 2015 and financial and accounting data exchange business of PDS Energy in July 2017. Most recently, it bought Phoenix DAS, a provider of field operations and management software for oilfield suppliers, in September 2017.

The Oildex deal is the latest in a string of private-equity investments in oil-and-gas software providers. Following a rebound in oil prices, the pace of deal-making in the sector has picked up. Most recently, Silver Lake agreed to sell its oil-and-gas software provider Quorum Business Solutions Inc., also known as Quorum Software, to Thoma Bravo.

San Francisco-based Genstar targets investments in the health-care, financial services, software and industrial technology industries. The firm is investing from its $3.3 billion Genstar Capital Partners VIII LP, which closed in 2017.

Accel-KKR, which has offices in Menlo Park, Calif., Atlanta and London, invests in information technology-enabled businesses and software. Earlier this month, it sold a majority stake in digital marketing company "https://www.wsj.com/articles/insight-venture-partners-to-back-marketing-technology-platform-episerver-1536224400?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1" to Insight Venture Partners, in a deal that valued the business at $1.16 billion.

Growth-equity investor Edison Partners led a $4 million financing round for Sigstr Inc., which operates a marketing platform that inserts advertisements into corporate email signatures.

The investment will be used in part to help Sigstr roll out a new product that uses artificial intelligence to analyze employee emails and calendars for information that can be used in sales and marketing efforts, according to a news release.

Sigstr's flagship product enables marketers to run campaigns that place ads in the email signatures of employees. The Indianapolis company works with 400 business-to-business companies, including AT&T Inc., Amazon.com Inc. and Yext Inc., according to the release.

Sigstr last year secured $5 million in a Series A funding round that was led by Hyde Park Venture Partners and included participation from Battery Ventures, marketing-technology company HubSpot Inc., Grand Ventures and High Alpha Capital.

Edison invests in financial-technology and health-care information-technology businesses as well as providers of enterprise products and services. The Princeton, N.J., firm targets companies that generate $5 million to $20 million in revenue.

The deal with Sigstr is Edison's second investment in an Indiana company in as many days. The firm on Monday said it led a $7.5 million investment in Emplify, a Fishers, Ind., maker of employee surveys.

The firm "https://www.wsj.com/articles/edison-partners-closes-257m-for-eighth-fund-1467804602?mod=article_inline" with $275 million in 2016.

Base10 fundraising is largest for a black-led venture capital fund

Richard Waters in San Francisco 
760 words
17 September 2018
16:15
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

San Francisco-based vehicle co-run by Nigeria-born Adeyemi Ajao raises $137m

A venture capital fund co-led by a black investor has raised more than $100m for the first time, in a sign of Silicon Valley s belated efforts to back more minority investors and entrepreneurs.

The new investment vehicle, managed by San Francisco-based Base10 Partners, follows a spate of smaller funds raised by black VCs in the US in recent months   though these have made only a very small dent in the racially skewed nature of start-up investing.

The huge racial gulf in Silicon Valley, both among investors and entrepreneurs, has received relatively little attention amid wider criticism recently of the tech world s significant gender disparities.

Only 4 per cent of US start-up investors are black or Hispanic, according to a study of 1,500 venture capitalists by Richard Kerby, a partner at the firm Equal Ventures. By comparison, about 13 per cent of the US population identify as black, and 16 per cent as Hispanic.

Base10 was co-founded by Adeyemi  Ade  Ajao, a serial entrepreneur and angel investor. He was born in Nigeria and brought up in southern Spain, where his first start-up, a social app called Tuenti, was bought by Telefnica. Like many successful European entrepreneurs he then set his sights on Silicon Valley, taking a place at Stanford University and starting another venture that was sold to business software company Workday.

Base10 said on Monday that it had closed its first fund after raising $137m, a sizeable amount for a so-called seed-stage fund that provides small amounts of capital in the very early stages of a start-up s life.

Mr Ajao, who grew up in a mainly white region, said he had little sensitivity to racial politics when he came to the US, but had since come to understand the significance of his position as a rare black figure in VC.

 I have an obligation to foster that network to make sure others succeed,  he said.  The more the story is told, the more we can create role models. 

Critics of Silicon Valley s start-up system say the lack of diversity among VCs has starved minority entrepreneurs of capital, in turn producing fewer black company founders who can later move into investing   a chicken-and-egg problem that perpetuates the imbalance.

 So much of venture investing at the early stage is about trust and judgment and that means you are likely to invest in people who are more like you,  said Charles Hudson, another black VC who raised a $15m venture fund in late 2016 and has since made 75 investments.

Silicon Valley s start-up world is also driven by a number of overlapping networks, making it hard for outsiders to break in. For instance, about 40 per cent of VCs came from just two universities   Harvard and Stanford   according to Mr Kerby s study.

 If you re plugged into one of these [university] networks, gender and race are still an issue, but you re afforded access others aren t,  said Mr Hudson, who also attended Stanford before taking a job at Google.

Among other black-led VC firms, Los Angeles-based Backstage Capital raised $36m in May, while 645 Ventures, a New York fund with two black partners, completed a $40m fund last year. Late last year, Unilever announced a $100m fund to back minority female entrepreneurs after buying Sundial Brands, a beauty products company whose goods are aimed at women of colour.

Investors such as Arlan Hamilton, founder of Backstage Capital, and Richelieu Dennis, the founder of Sundial Brands and the brains behind Unilever-backed New Voices Fund, aim to invest exclusively in minority-founded businesses. Others, such as Mr Ajao and Mr Hudson, have more general investment criteria, but say they hope their personal backgrounds and investment philosophies will lead them to a wider group of entrepreneurs.

Base10 is aiming to invest in start-ups that develop artificial intelligence and automation for industries in what Mr Ajao calls  the real economy , such as construction and transport.

 By definition, you end up working with people who aren t Silicon Valley,  he said, making it more likely the firm will back founders out of the Valley mainstream. The firm, which has already made 10 investments from its new fund, has yet to back a black entrepreneur, though its company founders include two Hispanics and two women.

Warner Music buys German merchandise retailer EMP for $180m

Anna Nicolaou in London 
401 words
17 September 2018
14:03
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

World s third largest record company seals its largest takeover in five years

Warner Music has acquired EMP Merchandising, the German retailer that sells T-shirts featuring rock bands like Nirvana, in its largest takeover in five years.

Sycamore Partners, the private equity group that has bought struggling retailers like Staples, agreed to sell EMP for $180m, according to people close to the deal.

Warner, the third largest record company and home to artists including Ed Sheeran, has made a string of deals in the past year as music revenues soared and streaming services like Spotify have grown. The company has aimed to diversify its business beyond selling music, as merchandising and touring account for a bigger slice of artists  income in the streaming era.

EMP, founded in 1986 as a mail order music store, sells merchandise from bands including Pink Floyd, Metallica and AC/DC.

As album sales shrink, artists have piled into merchandise as the primary physical purchase for music fans. Merchandise is  a big part of how music has visible and physical impact , said Max Lousada, Warner s chief executive for recorded music.

Previously a smaller part of a musician s income, merchandise sales have surged in recent years, bringing in millions for the biggest stars. In its most recent tour, US rock band Panic! At the Disco made 30 per cent of its profits from merchandise, according to a Rolling Stone report, while Kanye West claims to have sold $780,000 of merchandise at a single New York concert.

Globally, music merchandise revenues grew 9.4 per cent to $3.1bn in 2016, according to the Licensing Industry Merchandisers  Association.

Warner, owned by billionaire Len Blavatnik s Access Industries, has been in dealmaking mode under Mr Lousada, who was promoted to the company s top creative job last year after running the UK business.

A year ago Warner bought Dutch dance label Spinnin  Records for more than $100m. Other deals have looked to broaden the label s scope beyond recorded music. Warner last month bought Uproxx, the pop-culture website, and earlier this year acquired Sodatone, a machine learning start-up, in a bid to use data to find new artists.

Warner made $1.92bn in revenues in the first half of 2018, up 10 per cent from a year ago, amid an industry-wide recovery.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in theUK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a Company rather than a trust structure .

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors .

The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available .

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. News
Banks jump on the fintech bandwagon

Rana Foroohar 
864 words
17 September 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Europe
9
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

C all me a Luddite, but I have always agreed with former US Federal Reserve chairman Paul Volcker that the ATM has been the most useful innovation in finance of the past few decades.

So I will watch this week's Senate banking committee hearings on fintech with trepidation. The hearings will be the first chance to dig into the Treasury department's summer report on the sector, which talks approvingly of data sharing among technology companies and big banks to create efficiency, scale and lower consumer prices.

The report puts rather less focus on the systemic risk and predatory pricing that could emerge if the world's largest technology companies and the biggest banks on Wall Street share consumer data. (Just imagine if your Facebook page had a checking account attached. What could go wrong?) Given the deregulatory stance the Trump administration has taken so far, that's no surprise. But it is alarming, nonetheless. The Treasury paper's diagrams of the way platforms and banks might share a consumer's financial information, in order to offer "personalised" products and services, remind me of the complex illustrations of credit default swaps that triggered the 2008 financial crisis. Complexity of that sort always makes me nervous   it leaves so much room for the party with more information to obfuscate.

The blending of platform technologies, big data, artificial intelligence and consumer finance is only the latest and most turbo-charged combination of banking and commerce. Prior iterations, enabled by 1990s financial deregulation, did not end well.

Take Goldman Sachs's experiment with aluminium hoarding, that came to light in 2013. The bank had bought up thousands of tons of aluminium and was moving it between warehouses (which it had also bought) in order to control the release of commodity supply. According to some industry estimates, this added between $3.5bn and $5bn in costs to American shoppers over three years.

The Cornell University professor whose work first sparked serious media interest in the topic, Saule Omarova, will be speaking at the fintech hearings this week. Her latest research raises questions about whether the blending of big tech companies and finance is in the public interest.

"If Amazon can see your bank data and assets, [what is to stop them from] selling you a loan at the maximum price they know you are able to pay?" Professor Omarova asks.

It is a legitimate question, given the history of big banks, and more recently, the manner in which large tech companies use data to reap what many believe are unfair advantages. Amazon uses "dynamic pricing" to profit from public sector contracts, Uber employs "surge pricing" to set rates based on customers' willingness to pay, and Facebook compiles data about users that they have not themselves shared. Google has tracked users even if their privacy settings ought to prevent it.

Some people do not worry about any of this. They feel that it is a fair trade to exchange data for convenient goods and services. But it is impossible to know what is fair in these cases. None of us can see inside the algorithmic black boxes of the largest technology companies. It is one thing for a company to know my vacation shopping patterns or what media I like; it is another for them to access my entire financial history, including my investments.

Many people already lack confidence in making financial transactions and in personal wealth management. Why else would so many of them still be paying above average fees for such services? Imagine how vulnerable some consumers might be if their bank notices that they have $9,000 sitting in a savings account.

How many would respond to a pop-up ad urging them to move their money into a wonderful new higher yielding investment vehicle? Too bad the Trump administration has already gutted the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, which looks after retail customers who have disputes with banks. The Treasury's fintech proposals suggest they will have plenty of new issues to dig into.

Data portability   the ability of consumers to share their data with multiple private and public sector institutions   does have some advantages. But the accompanying risks, particularly when it comes to sensitive areas like financial or healthcare data, argue for a cautious approach.

The EU started allowing data sharing among big banks and tech companies earlier this year, but it required very clear opt-in and opt-out provisions to protect consumers.

So far very few European banks have actually used the new data sharing rules to sign deals with the biggest tech companies. That may be because the banks still remember lessons learnt during the financial crisis, about how complexity and opacity in what seem like everyday transactions can blow up wildly (who thought bad debt would ricochet from Iceland to Iowa?). But I doubt it.

Probably they are merely a few paces behind their US counterparts, many of whom are already jumping on the fintech bandwagon with all too much zeal.

rana.foroohar@ft.com

Just imagine if your Facebook page had a checking account attached. What could go wrong?

Alibaba's Jack Ma Says Government Should Stick to Governing; His comments come after he announced retirement plans to pursue other interests, and as China increases restrictions on internet companies

By Yoko Kubota 
761 words
17 September 2018
12:24
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

SHANGHAI Chinese e-commerce tycoon Jack Ma used a government-sponsored forum to suggest regulators take a lighter touch in dealing with technology companies, saying the market should be allowed to decide how new industries such as artificial intelligence develop.

"I personally think that the government has to do what the government should do, and the companies do what companies should do," Mr. Ma said at the World Artificial Intelligence Conference in Shanghai on Monday, recalling a conversation he said he had last year with U.S. Secretary of Transportation Elaine Chao about self-driving cars.

"Protecting the backward forces who are crying out loud will be the most important factor in destroying innovation," Mr. Ma said.

Mr. Ma's comments came a week after he announced "https://www.wsj.com/articles/daniel-zhang-to-succeed-jack-ma-as-alibaba-chairman-1536542559?mod=article_inline" in one year as executive chairman of Alibaba Group Holding Ltd., the internet giant with businesses in online retailing, cloud computing and mobile payments.

Mr. Ma said he wanted time "https://www.wsj.com/articles/alibabas-jack-ma-to-step-down-as-executive-chairman-1536381356?mod=article_inline", but some observers saw his decision as part of the fallout from China's authoritarian government increasing restrictions on internet companies.

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/tencent-foldsem-shutting-another-videogame-as-beijing-cracks-down-1536582807", Tencent Holdings Ltd.'s shares plunged as the government announced its plan to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinese-videogame-companies-shudder-as-government-tightens-grip-1535690686?mod=article_inline". Ride-hailing firm Didi Chuxing Technology Co. has come under government scrutiny after police said "https://www.wsj.com/articles/didi-halts-carpool-service-again-in-china-after-passenger-killed-1535269890", the second since May.

In January, Alibaba's mobile-payments affiliate Ant Financial Services Group became a target when Chinese internet regulators said the company"https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-swats-jack-mas-ant-over-customer-privacy-1515581339" on the protection of personal data.

Mr. Ma didn't directly refer to any of those events in his speech, which also included his views on how artificial intelligence could transform businesses and society. Mr. Ma urged traditional industries to embrace new technologies and spoke about how sectors such as manufacturing could be transformed through the use of data.

In his speech, Mr. Ma also referenced the recent crackdown on Chinese peer-to-peer lenders, which match small borrowers with ordinary investors. These internet lenders have been blamed for stranding investors when they fail.

Mr. Ma suggested many of those lenders operated illegally under the camouflage of internet-driven financial services. He said the risk with companies that use actual data and artificial intelligence to control risks is low.

Mr. Ma didn't mention Ant Financial by name, but the company has been hit by new rules introduced to regulate the internet-driven financial-services market this year.

The artificial-intelligence forum is a high-profile event, hosted by various government agencies including the Ministry of Industry and Information Technology. One of China's top officials, Vice Premier Liu He, also spoke.

Besides Mr. Ma, Tencent Chief Executive Ma Huateng, also known as Pony Ma, and Baidu Inc. Chief Executive Robin Li gave speeches.

Liu Qiangdong, the chairman of e-commerce company JD.com Inc., was originally scheduled to appear but a company spokeswoman said he isn't attending. Mr. Liu, also known as Richard Liu, was arrested by Minneapolis police "https://www.wsj.com/articles/jd-com-founder-gets-back-to-business-in-beijing-days-after-arrest-in-u-s-1536062833" Aug. 31, and then released the next day without bail.

Through his attorneys, Mr. Liu "https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinese-billionaire-arrested-on-sex-misconduct-allegation-unlikely-to-face-charges-lawyer-says-1535974482?mod=article_inline".

Among foreign companies represented at the event, Google has been noticeably present with two senior executives taking the stage: Vice President Jay Yagnik and Fei-Fei Li, the leader of Google Cloud AI who recently announced her plan to step down.

Their speeches come as the Alphabet Inc. unit doubles down on a China expansion strategy. The two didn't touch on Dragonfly, which, according to people familiar with the matter, is a project that includes testing a mobile version of Google's search engine that would "https://www.wsj.com/articles/google-considering-censored-search-engine-for-return-to-china-1533157785".

Ms. Li, in speaking about Google's approach to artificial intelligence, said it was vital to consider the ethical implications of artificial intelligence as the technology develops.

Yang Jie in Beijing and Liza Lin in Singapore contributed to this article.

Banks jump on to the fintech bandwagon

Rana Foroohar 
880 words
16 September 2018
15:20
Financial Times (FT.Com)

English

Data sharing between financial institutions and tech companies puts consumers at risk

Too bad the Trump administration has already gutted the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau, which looks after retail customers who have disputes with banks. The Treasury s fintech proposals suggest they will have plenty of new issues to dig into.

Data portability   the ability of consumers to share their data with multiple private and public sector institutions   does have some advantages. But the accompanying risks, particularly when it comes to sensitive areas like financial or healthcare data, argue for a cautious approach.

The EU started allowing data sharing among big banks and tech companies earlier this year, but it required very clear opt-in and opt-out provisions to protect consumers.

So far very few European banks have actually used the new data sharing rules to sign deals with the biggest tech companies. That may be because the banks still remember lessons learnt during the financial crisis, about how complexity and opacity in what seem like everyday transactions can blow up wildly (who thought bad debt would ricochet from Iceland to Iowa?). But I doubt it.

Probably they are merely a few paces behind their US counterparts, many of whom are already jumping on the fintech bandwagon with all too much zeal.

rana.foroohar@ft.com

Willingness rather than ability to pay sets prices / From Diego Zuluaga, Washington, DC, US
