Good morning, and welcome back to your new and improved newsletter.

In today's edition, we examine the rise of black investors leading their own venture firms. We also have an exclusive on the relocation of Mithril Capital, the firm led by Peter Thiel and Ajay Royan, plus a look at another batch of venture-backed companies moving to Wall Street.

A brief word about our breakfast briefing in San Francisco on Sept. 25: I'm pleased to announce that Trae Vassallo, co-founding partner of venture firm Defy, will join our panel on the disruption of the VC model, along with Bart Stephens of Blockchain Capital and Chris Farmer of SignalFire. More information.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Higher profile: For an industry whose investors are rarely people from under-represented backgrounds, a small but growing group of minorities suddenly are in a position to write checks for startups in Silicon Valley and beyond. Earlier this year Richard Kerby, a black investor, co-founded Equal Ventures, a seed-stage firm, after previously working at Venrock as a vice president and IVP as an associate. "Sometimes the easier path is to start your own fund," said Mr. Kerby, one of several black VCs who have started firms recently. Others include Charles Hudson of Precursor Ventures (formerly of SoftTech VC), Base10 Partners' Adeyemi Ajai (an entrepreneur and investor) and Ulu Ventures' Miriam Rivera. More from WSJ Pro's Katie Roof.

Peer-to-peer lender turns to the public: Funding Circle Holdings Ltd. Monday confirmed that it plans to go ahead with an initial public offering on the London Stock Exchange, and it expects to announce the indicative price range and issue its prospectus in the coming weeks. According to recent reports, the company is aiming for a valuation of up to 2 billion. That would make it potentially London's largest fintech IPO since payment processor Worldpay was valued at 4.8 billion in 2015. Venture investors have put at least $373 million behind Funding Circle, including a $100 million Series F round announced in early 2017. More from WSJ's Ian Walker.

Robotic retail: Standard Cognition, the artificial-intelligence startup we featured in August, opened a brick-and-mortar store Friday in San Francisco that shows off its automated checkout technology. After downloading the app and linking it to a credit card, shoppers can walk in, grab groceries and leave without stopping at a cashier kiosk. Using its cameras in the ceiling, the system automatically detects what people picked up and it charges them. But it's off to a slow start: On day one, the store only admitted one customer at a time to enter. The startup beat Amazon into San Francisco, though the e-commerce titan is planning to own its own store in town. Revisit the August story by WSJ Pro's Tomio Geron.

Davis's Take: Mithril Capital Moves Its Money, and Its Headquarters

By Alexander Davis

Mithril Capital, the late-stage firm co-founded in San Francisco by Ajay Royan and Peter Thiel, is pulling up stakes to start a new home--in Texas, WSJ Pro has learned.

This is a firm that makes its moves with deliberation, especially about what companies it wants to bankroll. Since it was founded in 2012, the firm in some years has only made a couple of investments and now has 31 companies in its portfolio.

So it was noteworthy when news came that Mr. Royan was joining the board of Singapore-based GreyOrange as a part of a $140 million Series C investment in the maker of robotic systems for warehouses. The company fits what Mr. Royan calls "archetype of the hidden champion."

Mithril's investing thesis hasn't changed. As for the future home of his firm, Mr. Royan laments that Silicon Valley no longer has the bold, experimental thinking that made it "one giant incubator." The idea that there are so many incubators springing up within the region, he says, is a sad commentary on what remains.

So he has settled on Austin as his firm's new home, for its lower costs and a thriving culture of creativity and tech innovation. If all goes well, he'll even uncover a hidden champion or two.

Exits

Eventbrite Inc. set estimated terms for its IPO, saying it plans to offer 10 million shares priced at $19 to $21 each. The company would raise $200 million at the midpoint of that range, and it would be valued at about $1.55 billion. Investors valued Eventbrite at more than $1 billion in a 2014 financing. Read more.

Boston Scientific Corp. has agreed to buy Augmenix Inc., whose hydrogel product helps reduce side effects from prostate cancer radiotherapy. The deal will include an up-front cash payment of $500 million, and up to $100 million for sales-based milestones. Augmenix's backers included Ascension Ventures, Catalyst Health Ventures, Excelestar Ventures, Sparta Group, Pinnacle Ventures and Versant Ventures. Read more.

Y-mAbs Therapeutics Inc. set terms for its IPO that would value the cancer drug developer at about $489.4 million. The company said it plans to sell about 5.3 million shares at $14 to $16. Read more.

Veterinary services provider Vetsource has acquired a majority share in VetSuccess. Terms weren't disclosed. Vetsource raised $50 million in funding led by Bain Capital Ventures in June. VetSuccess, of Toronto, provides veterinary data analytics, machine learning and business intelligence services. Read more.

New Money

TPG is leading an $80 million minority-stake investment in software developer Sonatype Inc. The investment round included participation from Accel, Goldman Sachs Group and Hummer Winblad Venture Partners. Read more.

Wunder Mobility, a Germany-based provider of carpooling, smart shuttle and fleet management technology, has raised $30 million in Series B funding. KCK Group led the round, and was joined by investors including Blumberg Capital.

Spyce Food Co., a Boston-based provider of robotic kitchens, has raised $21 million in Series A funding. Collaborative Fund and Maveron led the round, with participation from investors including Khosla Ventures.

ParkWhiz, a Chicago-based parking app, has secured $20 million in Series D funding. NewSpring led the investment, with General Partner Glenn Rieger joining the company's board. Baird Capital, Jump Capital and Beringea also invested.

Blue Medora, a Grand Rapids, Mich.-based provider of an IT monitoring integration service, has picked up a $10 million investment. Edison Partners led the round, and General Partner Lenard Marcus will join the board.

Ordermark, a Santa Monica, Calif.-based startup that provides an online ordering management service for restaurants, has completed a $9.5 million Series A round led by Nosara Capital. New investors including Vertical Venture Partners, Techstars Ventures and Riverpark Ventures participated, along with previous backers including TenOneTen and Mucker Capital.

CloudCherry, a Salt Lake City-based customer experience management startup, has landed a $9 million Series A round led by Pelion Venture Partners. Cisco Investments, Vertex Ventures and IDG Ventures India also participated. Cisco's Donald Tucker and Brett Wingo will join the company's board.

MarketDial Inc., a Salt Lake City-based company that answers critical questions about retailers' businesses via customized tests, has wrapped up a $7.5 million Series A round. Crosslink Capital led the funding, with participation from Kickstart Seed Fund, Peterson Ventures and Silicon Valley Bank.

Alaia Capital, a Darien, Conn.-based provider of alternative investment services, has picked up $5.5 million in funding. Centana Growth Partners led the round, which included participation from ThirdStream Partners.

Industry News

Hoffman's highs and lows: Reid Hoffman gets hit up for money a lot. His inbox, he says, is flooded with between 100 and 200 cold pitches every week. Part of his job is seeing good ideas in bad pitches. Mr. Hoffman, now a partner at Greylock Partners and author of "Blitzscaling: The Lightning-Fast Path to Building Massively Valuable Companies," recalls two other bets: one he made, and one he should have made. More from WSJ's Chris Kornelis.

How to pick a successful stockpicker

Chris Flood 
1,262 words
10 September 2018
05:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Serious horseracing fans make a careful study of runners and riders  form before placing a bet. Recent form, sadly, provides little guide to finding a successful fund manager as yesterday s winners turn all too frequently into today s also-rans.

Understanding why some managers consistently produce alpha   market-beating returns   requires a different approach, according to Rick Di Mascio, a former chief investment officer at the British Coal pension fund.

He describes historic performance records as  blunt and misleading  measures to evaluate whether an equity fund manager is a skilled stockpicker or not.

Inalytics, a consultancy founded by Mr Di Mascio in 1998, analyses every single trade carried out by its clients, forensically examining 400 portfolios each month.

This database, which covers about 40 active managers, has registered about 290 basis points of outperformance annually (after fees), compared with the respective portfolios  benchmarks over the past five years. About four in 10 of this group of managers consistently outperform their benchmark by 400 basis points or more a year, net of fees.

 I am strongly of the opinion that skill does exist in active management. There is an elite that do a fantastic job. In sports like tennis the average player is mediocre at best, but what about those that compete at Wimbledon? Just because the average fund manager loses money, net of fees, does not mean that there is no skill across the industry,  says Mr Di Mascio.

Inalytics  process focuses on three building blocks. The first is evaluating the contribution of research in helping to identify and add winners as measured by the impact and performance of  first buys , when new picks are added to a portfolio.

Another is analysing  sizing decisions , as large high conviction positions are the main drivers of outperformance in equity portfolios.

The final block is examining the timing and effectiveness of buying and selling decisions. The latter can be particularly problematic due to the human reluctance to admit defeat on an idea that has failed.

Combining these three scores shows that even highly skilled managers deliver alpha in just 57 per cent of the months examined.

 The margin between success and failure is very small,  says Mr Di Mascio.

He adds that asset owners or investors increasingly want managers that can deploy skill in high conviction, highly concentrated portfolios holding as few as 15 stocks in a global equity portfolio.

 It has taken 10 years for this approach to become an accepted norm. This is the response required of active managers to the rise of passives. It is how the Darwinian forces are playing out,  he says.

Some leading hedge fund managers, the most dynamic traders globally, employ in-house teams to analyse their trading strategies in a similar fashion to Inalytics.

 We really analyse the skills of the investment teams, looking at the equity research, the monetisation of their ideas, the timing of their positions: are they too early, too aggressive,  says Gerald Beeson, chief operating officer of Citadel, the $36bn Chicago-based hedge fund manager.

The increasing availability of low-cost smart beta strategies that provide exposure to known sources of higher returns, such as smaller companies or low volatility stocks, has increased the pressure on hedge fund managers to demonstrate that they can deliver true alpha   genuine skill in picking winners.

Teun Johnston, the chief executive of Man GLG, the discretionary arm, says having the correct data to evaluate the performance of portfolio managers is vital.

 Where appropriate, we want to isolate security selection skills,  he says, adding that Man Group is  investing heavily  to help its portfolio managers marry their expertise in stockpicking with quantitative techniques, for example, to improve sizing decisions.

The Church Commissioners, the steward of the Church of England s 8.3bn investment portfolio, uses Inalytics  services along with a suite of other tools to assess external money managers.

 A successful manager selection process can add value for an asset owner. Track records are poor indicators of future performance, but a trade-by-trade analysis helps us to understand if a portfolio manager s decisions are consistent with their investment philosophy and processes,  says Tom Joy, director of investments for the Church Commissioners.

Many pension schemes still decide to pick asset managers from a so-called  beauty parade  organised by an investment consultant.

 This is not a process that is likely to lead to success as it depends who performs well on the day,  says Mr Joy.

Vanguard, the world s second largest fund house, has a long history of hiring third-party providers to run active equity strategies, which it oversees via a dedicated manager research team.

Matt Piro, head of product in Europe for Vanguard, says data about individual portfolio managers, teams and entire organisations are being scrutinised in ways that were near impossible even as recently as five years ago, helping to inform more nuanced views.

 There can be fundamental differences in the way that different portfolio managers think about holding periods. Portfolio managers also tend to think differently about sizing decisions. So it is important to customise each assessment to identify what makes that portfolio manager special,  says Mr Piro.

Malcolm Smith, head of international equities at JPMorgan Asset Management, says that the best fund managers all share the same qualities   self-awareness, a drive to constantly learn, including from their mistakes, and an appetite to improve   which can be enhanced with the help of modern portfolio analysis techniques.

 We now have high quality data to help us understand what provides the edge and how we can make improvements. Do we focus on our forecasting skills, breadth of research, portfolio construction or other process issues,  says Mr Smith, who leads a 149-strong team responsible for $130bn in client assets.

Demands for greater transparency and more portfolio-level data have increased from clients. This has in turn encouraged the development of a more structured analysis of the drivers of portfolio returns for JPMorgan AM.

 We want to make sure that where we do deliver alpha that we have a high degree of confidence that we can continue to achieve that outperformance and also to improve in the future,  says Mr Smith.

A stark warning for investors who still look at past performance

Star fund managers that can deliver consistently superior investment returns are a rare species. Managers that work hard to gain prominence as skilled stockpickers often find their reputation in tatters when previously successful trading strategies no longer prove effective.

Even the very best can struggle.

Warren Buffett, who is widely regarded as the world s most successful investor, has experienced multiple periods of underperformance over his long decades as chairman of Berkshire Hathaway.

S&P Global, the indices and analytics provider, regularly publishes a  persistence scorecard  that examines the ability of managers to deliver market-beating returns over multiple periods.

The report provides a stark warning for any investor that thinks they will be able to identify a winning manager from their historic performance record.

S&P examined the performance of 1,455 US active equity funds over the five years since March 2013. Fewer than one in five funds that started in the best-performing quartile in March 2013 remained in the top group five years later.

More than a quarter (25.8 per cent) of the best performers in March 2013 dropped into the worst-performing group over the same period. Similar trends were found when the funds were divided into size categories and over one and three-year time periods.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in theUK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors .

Single price: Based onamid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available .

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge fromfund managers/operators.

Mark Wiseman:  If you're unwilling to evolve you're likely to get eaten 

Robin Wigglesworth 
1,304 words
9 September 2018
05:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

About two decades ago Mark Wiseman had two landlines installed at his house in Toronto, and the BlackRock executive is still paying for them despite their utter disuse. The money management industry is similar, he argues.

 The erosion of the telephone landline has been far, far, far slower than anybody predicted a decade ago,  the gregarious, bespectacled Canadian points out. But like the old landline, the old way of investing is slowly but surely becoming obsolete, he predicts.

 If you re a traditional portfolio manager it s a challenging time because there s so much change happening around you,  Mr Wiseman says.  If you re unwilling to evolve you re likely going to get eaten alive. 

The hockey-loving Canadian has a good vantage point to judge. Two years ago he stepped down as chief executive of the Canada Pension Plan Investment Board, one of the biggest and most sophisticated pension schemes in the world, to take a position as BlackRock s senior managing director. He is global head of active equities, chairman of BlackRock Alternative Investors, and commands a prized slot on the asset manager s global executive committee.

His first job was to shake up the $304bnactive equities unit   essentially all the stock investing that does not fall under the aegis of Mark Wiedman, global head of the iShares exchange-traded fund empire. A round of previous restructurings had failed to turn around the long-disappointing stockpicking business, leading to the hiring of Mr Wiseman in 2016 and to another restructuring he oversaw last year.

The latest reorganisation, given the internal code name  Project Monarch , involved a cull of traditional mutual fund managers, shifting billions of dollars to BlackRock s San Francisco-based quantitative investment arm Systematic Active Equities, and attempting to reinvigorate the broader unit with SAE s knowhow.

The move amounted to a big bet that many investors will eventually ditch their old-school landlines   in other words, traditional human stockpickers that pore through corporate reports and economic data   for what he argues is the smartphone equivalent of investing: more sophisticated, computer-aided, big data-powered strategies.

This restructuring is now largely complete and sets up BlackRock as a more able competitor in an industry that will continue to see mounting pressures, Mr Wiseman argues. Money is already gushing out from expensive fund managers that in practice do little more than track an index, and the Ontario native predicts that the next phase will be outflows from asset managers that simply tap into one of the common investment  factors  identified by academics over the years   like value, momentum or growth   which can also be easily replicated by a cheap ETF.

 It s just the next innovation,  he says.  The same way that we learned that being an index-hugger you won t have a long career, the next step is that if you re just a factor-hugger you also won t have a long career as you can be replaced by a low-cost alternative. 

If Mr Wiseman does succeed in turning around BlackRock s equities unit it would put the former Fulbright scholar in a good position to be a contender to succeed the company s founder Larry Fink as chief executive.

Abruptly relinquishing the top job at the CPPIB surprised some observers, but it was seen as Mr Wiseman switching from being a big fish in a small pond to a smaller fish in a bigger pond   indeed the biggest pond in the asset management industry. While Mr Fink shows little sign of slowing down, he is now 65 and analysts say the poaching of Mr Wiseman shows that he was keen to inject some external competition into the mix of who might succeed him.

Although Mr Wiseman has only worked at BlackRock for two years his links to it are deeper than his tenure suggests. While at the CPPIB he had worked closely with Mr Fink on the advisory board of Focusing Capital on the Long Term   a think-tank set up by the BlackRock founder aimed at encouraging a longer time horizon among corporate executives and investors   and Mr Wiseman s wife Marcia Moffat had a year earlier been hired to lead BlackRock s Canadian business.

Mr Wiseman insists, with a studied nonchalance that brings to mind Jim Hacker s  I have no ambitions in that direction  in Yes Minister, that he is happy with what he describes as a  great job  and is not looking for another one.  I ve been a CEO, I know all that goes with it; I m in no rush to do it again,  he says.

Instead, he is now focused on ramping up BlackRock s investment capabilities in  private markets , given his chairmanship of BlackRock Alternative Investors and his longstanding interest in the field (he initially joined CPPIB in 2005 as senior vice-president for private investments).

Private markets, broadly speaking, are anything that are not traded on an exchange, such as real estate, infrastructure, direct loans or shares in non-public companies. Given how the private debt market has exploded in size and the public equities universe is now shrinking due to sluggish listings and vast buyback programmes, this is a huge opportunity for BlackRock, Mr Wiseman argues. But he laments the publicly listed company s declining importance.

 We should pay close attention to it,  he says.  The joint stock company is a fantastic innovation 	. 	. 	. 	but when public markets become too complicated, too regulated, too expensive, too intermediated, too volatile 	. 	. 	. 	what happens to that source of efficient financing to fuel economic growth? Have we got, or are we getting to a point, where the public company is no longer able to do that? 

The mounting complexity of markets is another topic on Mr Wiseman s mind. BlackRock s increasing emphasis on computer-powered, systematic strategies and enhancing traditional fund managers with their tools is very much part of the investment industry s broader zeitgeist. But there are a number of analysts, fund managers and even regulators that fret that the speed and complexity of modern algorithmic markets have made them more fragile.

Mr Wiseman argues that leverage is still the ultimate poison for the financial system, and remained contained by post-crisis regulations. But he concedes that  operational  dangers are on the rise, using his cars   a modern, high-tech Audi and a vintage 1965 Mustang   as a metaphor to explain his view.

When he opens the hood of the Mustang he can figure out how it works, and with the help of some YouTube videos fix most things that break. But he admits he recently struggled to even pop the hood of his Audi when the oil needed changing.

 My Audi never breaks down, but when it does, I m calling roadside assistance. There s no way I m going to figure out how to fix that thing,  he admits.  It s not just that markets are becoming more complex. It s their interconnectedness, the reliance on servers and data, and the potential for some unrelated entity in the ecosystem to simply break down   either through malicious action or simply by accident   that we should pay attention to.

WSJ Pro VC Newsletter for Friday, Sept. 7, 2018; Boldstart's Fundraising; Saudis' VC Plan; PagerDuty Goes  Unicorn'

Welcome to a new, mobile-friendly look for your WSJ Pro Venture Capital newsletter. We hope it's familiar but also fresh. What hasn't changed is our commitment to bringing you an information-packed daily briefing that gives you a competitive advantage.

In today's edition: Venture investors have shaken off the quiet of summer's doldrums and are picking up the deal-flow pace. We have an exclusive report on early-stage investor Boldstart's newest fund. Plus, Saudi Arabia has dreams of becoming a player in venture investing, and PageDuty has joined the Billion Dollar Startup Club.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Boldstart starts fresh: New York-based firm Boldstart is in the market to raise $75 million for a new fund, according to a person familiar with the matter. The firm, founded by Ed Sims and Eliot Durbin in 2010, raised $47 million for its previous fund in 2015. Boldstart's strategy centers on providing seed capital to technical founders often at a point when they have an idea but no product yet. The firm then uses relationships with corporations to help the startup sign customer deals. WSJ Pro's Yuliya Chernova has the exclusive.

Saudis plot fund strategy: Saudi Arabia's national oil company wants to get in on the mega-fund game. Better known as Aramco, the company is considering a $1 billion VC fund to invest in international technology companies, WSJ reports, as the kingdom deepens efforts to diversify its economy. Investments would complement the oil titan's operations, and the company may plant a flag in Silicon Valley or elsewhere in the U.S. to generate deals. WSJ's Rory Jones & Summer Said have the exclusive.

PagerDuty joins the club: PagerDuty Inc., which oversees enterprises' digital operations, said it has closed a $90 million financing round that valued the company at $1.3 billion. T. Rowe Price led the Series D, with participation from existing investors including Accel, Andreessen Horowitz and Bessemer Venture Partners. The company, headed by CEO Jennifer Tejada, said it has raised a total of $173 million. More from WSJ Pro's Katie Roof.

Gormley's Take: Gluing Medical Data Together

By Brian Gormley

Startups are vying to be the glue that brings electronic medical record systems together.

Digitized patient-data use is widespread thanks to a 2009 law that employed financial rewards and penalties to spur adoption. Part of the vision was that electronic records would enable easy exchange of health information.

Progress has been made in getting various digital systems to communicate, but work remains to be done. Startups like Bridge Connector and Sansoro Health are working with venture capitalists to close the gaps that remain in this digital divide of the health-care sector. More in Gormley's Take.

VC Funds

Oversubscribed Oberland: Oberland Capital Management said it has raised $800 million for a new fund focused on health-care royalties and debt. The final closing of Oberland Capital Healthcare II LP came in ahead of the fund's $650 million target and is nearly double the size of Oberland's first institutional fund, which wrapped up with $425 million in 2015.

Grayscale Investments LLC, a unit of Digital Currency Group, has launched its eighth single-asset token fund, this one focused solely on ZEN, a privacy-focused token based on the Horizen platform (formerly known as ZenCash). The fund has started with $6.3 million, which was seeded by DCG. In the first six months of 2018, Grayscale overall raised close to $250 million across its funds, which also include a diversified token fund, the company said. DCG, which owns and invests in blockchain startups, is itself backed by Social Capital, RRE Ventures and others.

MissionOG, a Philadelphia-based venture firm investing in enterprise startups, has raised about $61 million toward a $100 million fund, according to a regulatory filing.

Exits

Meituan advances listing: Meituan Dianping on Friday launched its much-anticipated initial public offering, which values the online retail services firm at close to $55 billion. The company expects to list in Hong Kong on Sept. 20, it said.

Shazam deal clears hurdle: Apple Inc. received the European Union's approval Thursday to acquire Shazam Entertainment Ltd., allowing the technology giant to close one of its most prominent deals in years. Shazam's backers included Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers, Institutional Venture Partners, DN Capital and Acacia Capital Partners.

New Money

GreyOrange, a Singapore-based robotics company used by Flipkart and others in supply-chain work, raised a $140 million Series C round, led by Mithril Capital in what the company called the largest industrial-robotics funding deal. Mithril's Ajay Royan will join GreyOrange's board.

MasterClass, a San Francisco-based online education service, has gathered $80 million in Series D funding. IVP led the round, and was joined by New Enterprise Associates, Javelin Ventures, Advancit Capital, Atomico and Evolution Media. Roseanne Wincek of IVP will join the company's board.

Twentyeight-Seven Therapeutics, a Cambridge, Mass.-based startup focused on modulating non-coding RNA biology to develop treatments for cancer and other diseases, has closed a $65 million Series A round. MPM Capital and Novartis Venture Fund co-led the financing, and were joined by investors including Johnson & Johnson Innovation   JJDC Inc. and Vertex Ventures HC. Carolyn Ng of Vertex, Michal Silverberg of Novartis, along with Luke Evnin and Briggs Morrison from MPM, will join the company's board.

Yoco, the South African provider of a card reader that connects with a merchant's smartphone or tablet, has collected $16 million in Series B funding. Partech led the round, which included participation from Orange Digital Ventures, FMO, Quona Capital and Velocity Capital. Cyril Collon, a general partner at Partech, will join the company's board.

Drinks Holdings Inc., an online wine purchasing and delivery service, has raised $15 million in Series B financing, led by Beverly Pacific and Shea Ventures.

Custora Inc., a New York-based provider of customer analytics software for retail, has raised $13.75 million in Series B funding. General Catalyst Partners led the investment, with participation from Foundation Capital and Greycroft Partners.

Black Bear, a Dutch company that transforms used tires into sustainable products, has raised  11 million in growth funding. Investors including ING Sustainable Investments, 5square, Social Impact Ventures and Chemelot Ventures participated.

People

Sanjay Keswani was named chief executive of Rheos Medicines Inc., an immune-mediated diseases startup backed by Third Rock Ventures and others. Mr. Keswani was most recently a senior vice president at Roche. Before that, he was with Bristol-Myers Squibb.

Dan Boneh has been named a research partner at Andreessen Horowitz's a16z crypto fund. Mr. Boneh is a Stanford University professor who leads the Applied Cryptography group, co-leads the Computer Security Lab, and teaches various crypto-related courses.

BUYOUTS

Argonaut Private Equity, Tulsa, Okla.

Argonaut Private Equity held the first closing of its latest fund at more than $230 million. Argonaut said it expects additional commitments from several limited partners. The fund's predecessor closed at $600 million in 2016. Argonaut invests in energy-services, industrial and manufacturing businesses.

Carlyle Group LP, Washington

Carlyle Group LP collected $18.5 billion for its newest U.S. buyout fund. The final tally for Carlyle Partners VII LP exceeded the fund's $15 billion goal. Carlyle closed on $13 billion for its previous U.S. buyout fund in 2014. The firm, its employees and other professionals contributed $1 billion of the new fund's total. Carlyle plans to use the fund to take majority and strategic minority stakes primarily in U.S. companies. Carlyle also has raised at least $773.4 million so far for Carlyle Global Financial Services Partners III LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm's global financial-services buyout strategy focuses on buyouts, growth-capital deals and minority investments in financial-services companies. The fund's predecessor wrapped up with $1 billion in 2014.

Life-settlements investor Corry Capital Advisors raised $301 million for a new fund, said a spokeswoman for the firm. The amount raised for the vehicle, CCA Life Settlements Fund FCPSIF, slightly exceeded a $300 million target.

H.I.G. Capital raised at least $2.39 billion so far for H.I.G. Advantage Buyout Fund LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm has revised its initial goal for the fund and is seeking to raise $2.75 billion, according to the SEC filing. H.I.G. indicated it was looking to gather $2.25 billion for the vehicle in a July 2017 regulatory filing. H.I.G.'s private-equity arm typically invests in small and midsize companies valued at $50 million to $500 million.

ICV Partners closed a new fund with $585 million. Investors that have disclosed commitments to the fund include the Illinois Municipal Retirement Fund and the New York State Common Retirement Fund. ICV invests in companies that generate $25 million to $250 million of revenue and $5 million to $30 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. ICV targets transactions in the manufacturing, business-services, consumer, commercial-services and health-care sectors.

Jordan Co. is approaching the $3.2 billion fundraising goal for its latest fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The vehicle, Resolute Fund IV LP, has gathered at least $3.17 billion so far, the filing states. One investor that has backed the fund is the Nebraska Investment Council, which committed $50 million, according to a pension document. Jordan is looking to match the $3.2 billion it raised for the vehicle's predecessor, Resolute Fund III LP, which closed in 2014. The firm wrapped up Resolute Fund II LP with $3.6 billion in 2007. Jordan invests in companies mostly located in North America that are valued at $50 million to $2 billion, according to another SEC filing.

A Credit Suisse Group AG affiliate is betting there is money to be made in backing technology companies that serve the financial-services industry. NEXT Investors, a growth-equity unit of Credit Suisse's asset management division, collected about $261 million for its second fund focused on investments in the sector. The fund, NEXT Investors II LP, held a $110 million initial closing in March 2017, said a Credit Suisse spokeswoman. The vehicle is the first in which Credit Suisse isn't an investor, although the fund works with the bank on a number of areas, including sourcing deal flow. NEXT secured $404 million in total commitments for its first fund in 2014. That vehicle included $135 million for new investments and $269 million for the purchase of a portfolio that Credit Suisse transferred to the firm.

Patriot Financial Partners raised at least $374.1 million so far for Patriot Financial Partners III LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The vehicle has a $400 million offering amount, according to the filing. That goal is higher than a $300 million amount the firm indicated it was seeking for the fund in 2017 filings. Patriot closed on at least $293 million for its previous pool of capital in 2015, according to SEC filings, although the fund ultimately may have raised more money. The firm takes noncontrolling stakes in community banks, thrifts and financial-services companies in the U.S.

Private Advisors, Richmond, Va.

Private Advisors is making progress on its sophomore real-assets fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The vehicle, Private Advisors Real Assets Fund II LP, so far has collected at least $165.3 million, according to the filing. The fund's predecessor closed on $205 million last year. Private Advisors is majority owned by New York Life Insurance Co.'s investment-management group. The firm manages $4.8 billion in total across public- and private-market strategies.

Quantum Energy Partners, Houston

Quantum Energy Partners has amassed nearly $5.6 billion for a new fund, a record for the private-equity firm as it pours more money into the oil patch. The energy investor closed Quantum Energy Partners VII LP and its affiliated vehicles with $5.58 billion in capital commitments, above the pool's $5.4 billion hard cap, said Wil VanLoh, Quantum's founder and chief executive. The total amount raised includes a $750 million co-investment pool and commitments from the general partners, he said. The firm so far has committed the fund to deals in various sectors of the energy industry, including upstream and midstream oil and gas, oil-field services and renewable energy, Mr. VanLoh said.

Ridgemont Equity Partners, Charlotte, N.C.

Ridgemont Equity Partners so far has collected at least $1.25 billion for its third flagship fund since spinning out of Bank of America Merrill Lynch in 2010, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm is seeking $1.5 billion for Ridgemont Equity Partners III LP, according to the filing. The amount recorded so far surpasses the $995 million the firm raised for its previous flagship fund, which closed in 2015. Ridgemont makes growth and buyout investments, generally deploying $25 million to $125 million in companies with $5 million to $35 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. The firm focuses on four main industry categories: health care; energy; technology and telecommunications; and business and industrial services.

Soundcore Capital Partners, New York

Soundcore Capital Partners raised $350 million for its first institutional fund to buy family-owned companies in the lower middle market. The vehicle, Soundcore Capital Partners Fund II LP, exceeded its $300 million target. Soundcore backs North American companies that generate up to $10 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. The firm, which invests $20 million to $70 million of equity per deal, targets the business-services, specialty distribution and manufacturing sectors.

Summit Park, Charlotte, N.C.

Summit Park closed a new fund with $245 million in commitments. Summit Park III LP exceeded its original $200 million target and the $118.5 million its predecessor closed on in 2015. The latest fund will back companies in the eastern half of the U.S. with $4 million to $15 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization. Summit Park targets companies in the business-services, consumer-services, manufacturing and distribution sectors.

Sycamore Partners, New York

Sycamore Partners closed its third fund, Sycamore Partners III LP, with $4.75 billion of capital commitments from limited partners. The firm raised $2.5 billion of committed capital for its second fund in 2014. Sycamore specializes in consumer, distribution and retail-related investments.

Tailwater Capital, Dallas

Tailwater Capital has attracted $1 billion for its latest fund. The energy investor closed Tailwater Energy Fund III LP at the fund's $900 million hard cap and received an additional $100 million co-investment commitment for one of the vehicle's portfolio companies. One investor that disclosed a commitment to the new fund is the Montgomery County Employees' Retirement System, according to one of the pension system's quarterly reports. The fund targets deals in the midstream oil-and-gas sector. Tailwater's previous vehicle in the series closed at $650 million in 2014.

Vista Equity Partners, San Francisco

Technology investor Vista Equity Partners could raise nearly $14 billion for its seventh flagship fund, according to a set of pension fund materials. A document published by Oregon State Treasury said the fund is expected to be 20% to 25% larger than its predecessor, which closed on more than $11 billion in 2017. Private Equity Analyst reported in May that Vista was seeking to raise $12 billion or more for the new fund. Vista's latest buyout fund will seek to take majority stakes in 15 software and data companies valued at $750 million to $10 billion, the document shows. The fund has yet to set a hard cap despite returning to market earlier this year, according to the document.

Welsh Carson Anderson & Stowe, New York

Welsh Carson Anderson & Stowe gathered $2.82 billion so far for WCAS XIII LP and its parallel and co-investment vehicles, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm set a $4 billion hard cap for the fund, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. If WCAS XIII hits its cap, the fund would be the largest the firm has raised to date, exceeding a previous pool of capital that closed on $3.33 billion in 2016. One investor that has backed the new vehicle is the Minnesota State Board of Investment, according to a pension document. Welsh Carson mostly makes growth investments in health care and technology.

DISTRESSED/TURNAROUND

Apollo Global Management LLC, New York

Apollo Global Management LLC held a $2.2 billion first closing for its hybrid-value fund in the second quarter, said Apollo Chairman and Chief Executive Leon Black during the firm's second-quarter earnings call. The fund is targeting $3 billion and is expected to invest in a mix of private equity and illiquid credit. It could make structured-equity investments as well as take noncontrolling positions in financially strapped and distressed companies.

Oaktree Capital Group LLC, Los Angeles

Oaktree Capital Group LLC closed on $1.1 billion for Oaktree Transportation Infrastructure Fund LP and $700 million for Oaktree Special Situations Fund II LP in the second quarter, said Oaktree Chief Executive Jay Wintrob during the firm's recent earnings call. He said the firm is expected to near the special situations fund's $1.75 billion target by year-end. Through its special situations strategy, Oaktree targets control-oriented debt and equity investments in companies that "have an element of distress, dislocation or dysfunction," according to the firm's website. Mr. Wintrob said Oaktree also has begun pitching Oaktree Power Opportunities Fund V LP, which has a $1.25 billion target, and has secured about $720 million for a new midmarket lending fund.

FUNDS OF FUNDS

RCP Advisors, Chicago

RCP Advisors raised at least $148.7 million so far for its latest fund, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. The firm is targeting $250 million for RCP Fund XIII LP, according to one filing. The vehicle and RCP Fund XIII Cayman Feeder LP, a feeder fund of RCP Fund XIII, have a total offering amount of $400 million. The firm's previous pool raised at least $382.1 million, according to an April SEC filing. Through its fund-of-funds strategy, RCP typically backs North American buyout managers with $200 million to $1 billion in committed capital.

MEZZANINE & OTHER DEBT

Abry Partners, Boston

Abry Partners is pitching its fourth senior debt fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm is seeking $1.5 billion for ABRY Advanced Securities Fund IV LP, a goal that would put the fund on par with the $1.5 billion Abry raised for its third senior debt fund in 2014. The firm typically invests its debt funds in the senior debt of businesses operating in the same sectors the firm targets through its buyout funds. Those sectors include advertising, broadcasting, content distribution, digital media, information services, information security, mobile communications and telecommunications, among others, according to the firm's registered investment adviser filing.

Thoma Bravo, Chicago

Thoma Bravo is nearly two thirds of the way to the fundraising goal for its first credit vehicle. The software and technology-enabled services investor so far has collected at least $499.4 million of the $750 million it is seeking for Thoma Bravo Credit Fund I LP and related vehicles, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm is in the midst of raising its latest flagship fund as well, aiming to gather $11.5 billion for Thoma Bravo Fund XIII LP, Private Equity Analyst previously reported.

VENTURE CAPITAL

Aldrich Capital Partners, Vienna, Va.

Growth-equity firm Aldrich Capital Partners is closing in on the goal for its first fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The vehicle, Aldrich Capital Partners Fund LP, has collected at least $246.4 million so far and has a $250 million offering amount, according to the filing, which doesn't indicate whether the fund has closed. The Connecticut Retirement Plans and Trust Funds committed to the vehicle, according to a pension document. Aldrich invests in companies focused on financial technology, health-care technology, software and technology-enabled services, aiming to back businesses with revenue of at least $10 million.

August Capital, Menlo Park, Calif.

August Capital is in the market to raise $250 million for its eighth fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The goal for August Capital VIII LP is smaller than the $450 million the firm secured for its previous fund in 2015. A person familiar with the matter said this is because August is shifting away from late-stage investing. The person said two partners from the firm are retiring.

Benhamou Global Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

Enterprise-technology investor Benhamou Global Ventures held a first closing on more than $40 million for BGV Opportunity Fund LP to invest in early-stage companies. The fund is the firm's fourth pool of capital. Benhamou said the vehicle is slated for follow-on investments in portfolio companies from its previous two funds. Benhamou closed its third fund with $80 million last year and its second fund with $72 million in 2015.

Berkeley SkyDeck, Berkeley, Calif.

Berkeley SkyDeck, a startup accelerator and incubator affiliated with the University of California, Berkeley, raised $24 million for a dedicated venture fund. Berkeley SkyDeck Fund will invest $100,000 in each startup that participates in the accelerator program. Half of the fund's profits will be shared with the university.

Cowboy Ventures, Palo Alto, Calif.

Cowboy Ventures closed on $95 million for its largest fund. The vehicle, Cowboy Ventures Fund III LLC, targets U.S. seed-stage technology startups. Cowboy previously closed on $57 million for its second fund in 2014.

Felicis Ventures, Menlo Park, Calif.

Felicis Ventures raised $270 million for its sixth fund. Felicis was targeting $240 million for the vehicle, Private Equity Analyst previously reported. "This [fund size] is the largest number we can implement to be able to lead and co-lead rounds but not so large that we have to drastically change," said Aydin Senkut, founder and managing director at Felicis. The firm, originally formed as a seed fund in 2006, has expanded and now does Series B and Series C deals in addition to seed and Series A rounds. The fund is a step up from the firm's previous investment pool, which closed with $200 million in 2016.

Flare Capital Partners, Boston

Flare Capital Partners is in the market to raise $200 million for Flare Capital Partners II LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm didn't record any commitments to the vehicle in the filing. Flare invests in health-care technology companies in the U.S.

Foundation Capital, Palo Alto, Calif.

Foundation Capital is seeking $350 million for Foundation Capital IX LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm didn't record any commitments to the fund in the filing. Foundation closed its previous fund with $325 million in 2015 to invest in early-stage companies.

Foundry Group, Boulder, Colo.

Foundry Group closed on $750 million for its seventh fund. In a blog post, the firm said the vehicle will combine strategies followed by previous funds including early-stage, growth and funds-of-funds strategies into a single fund. Founded in 2007, Foundry has invested in startups such as 3-D printer company Formlabs Inc. and dog-walking service Rover Inc. The firm also invests in seed funds directly and names Forerunner Ventures, Bonfire Ventures and Founder Collective among its partner funds.

Fuel Capital, Burlingame, Calif.

Fuel Capital has set out on its first fundraising effort since bringing on TaskRabbit Inc. co-founder Leah Busque as its second general partner. The early-stage investor is seeking $75 million for Fuel Capital III LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. No commitments were recorded for the fund in the filing. Fuel hired Ms. Busque last summer, shortly after she stepped down as chief executive of TaskRabbit. The firm closed on at least $46.2 million for its previous fund in 2016, according to another SEC filing.

The Fund, New York

The Big Apple has a new venture-capital firm run by people who disavow the term "venture capitalist." The Fund, as it is called, collected $3.2 million from about 75 startup founders and operators for a new fund, with the aim of investing in the next generation of New York entrepreneurs. No professional venture capitalists were allowed into the Fund as investors, said Matthew Brimer, one of the firm's co-founders. Mr. Brimer said the Fund is writing checks starting at $50,000.

GGV Capital, Menlo Park, Calif.

GGV Capital is seeking $1.88 billion across several new funds, according to Securities and Exchange Commission filings. The amount is listed for seven investment vehicles, including GGV Capital VII LP and GGV Discovery II LP. The firm previously closed on $1.2 billion across several funds in 2016. GGV is a multistage venture investor that backs companies in the U.S. and China.

Mission Bay Capital, San Francisco

Mission Bay Capital, a backer of early-stage bioscience startups, is seeking to raise a new $85 million fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm so far has secured at least $26.3 million for Mission Bay Capital III LP. Mission Bay's previous fund closed with $25 million in 2015. The firm was co-founded by Regis Kelly and Douglas Crawford, leaders of the QB3 incubator at the University of California, San Francisco. Mission Bay is affiliated with QB3 but is an independent entity. The new fund will operate slightly differently than Mission Bay's previous funds, Mr. Kelly wrote in an email. QB3 won't receive a percentage of the management fees or carried interest from Mission Bay's latest fund, as it did with previous funds.

Noro-Moseley Partners, Atlanta

Noro-Moseley Partners has received commitments of at least $56.9 million so far toward its latest venture-capital fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund, Noro-Moseley Partners VIII LP, is aiming to raise $150 million, according to the filing. Noro- Moseley invests in "early growth" information-technology and health-care companies. The firm closed on $117 million for its previous fund in 2015.

Polaris Partners, Boston

Polaris Partners held a first and final closing for Polaris Growth Fund I LP at the vehicle's $175 million hard cap. The fund will target investments of $10 million to $40 million in North American technology companies, including software, internet and technology-enabled services businesses.

Ridge Ventures, San Francisco

Ridge Ventures closed its latest early-stage venture-capital fund at $130 million, its first new fund since the firm changed its name from IDG Ventures USA last year. The firm said it would invest in enterprise-software businesses, with a focus on sectors such as artificial intelligence, analytics, software as a service, infrastructure, marketing technology and human resources. Ridge also backs select consumer companies.

Root Ventures, San Francisco

Root Ventures has raised $76.7269 million for its second seed-stage, hardware-focused "deep technology" fund. The firm previously raised $31,415,926.53 a reference to the first 10 digits of Pi for its first fund in 2015. The new fund's amount refers to the speed of sound in miles an hour. Root invests in startups with experienced technical founders developing new technology in areas such as hardware or software to manage hardware. A number of its startups are robotics companies developing automation technology.

Ten Eleven Ventures, Burlingame, Calif.

Cybersecurity-focused Ten Eleven Ventures closed its second growth fund at $140 million. The fund, Ten Eleven Growth Fund II LP, will write checks of $10 million to $15 million, targeting investments at the Series C stage and later. Ten Eleven now has $250 million under management across three funds.

Thrive Capital, New York

Joshua Kushner's Thrive Capital has started raising $1 billion for its largest fund to date, said a person familiar with the situation. About $400 million of the firm's sixth fund will go to early-stage deals, and $600 million will be dedicated to growth, the person said. Founded in 2009, the firm has raised $1.3 billion to date. It collected $700 million for its most recent fund in 2016.

EUROPE

BUYOUTS

Graphite Capital, London

Graphite Capital raised more than 470 million ($616.8 million) for its latest U.K. midmarket buyout fund. The firm will have slightly less money to spend than it did when it wrapped up its previous offering five years ago. Graphite in 2013 said it raised 450 million for its eighth fund and a further 50 million for a top-up fund. Private Equity Analyst reported in the summer of 2017 the firm was looking to raise more than 500 million for its latest pool after beginning talks with investors. Graphite said it likely would use the new money to make eight to 12 investments. The firm primarily plans to invest in companies valued at 30 million to 150 million but will support the expansion of some smaller companies.

MEZZANINE & OTHER DEBT

Ares Management LP, Los Angeles

Ares Management LP collected  6.5 billion ($7.62 billion) for its latest European direct-lending fund. The fund, Ares Capital Europe IV, exceeded its initial  4.5 billion target. The vehicle is larger than its predecessor, which closed with  2.5 billion in 2016. Ares expects to have about  10 billion available to deploy once leverage is included. It has made several commitments from the new fund.

Intermediate Capital Group PLC, London

Alternative asset manager Intermediate Capital Group PLC collected  3.7 billion ($4.33 billion) for its latest European corporate debt fund. A spokesman for the firm said the fund hasn't yet closed. The pool of capital, ICG Europe Fund VII SCSp, has a  4 billion target, ICG previously said. It is the successor to a  3 billion fund the firm closed in 2015. The fund is expected to make 15 to 25 investments in European companies valued at  100 million to  1.5 billion, according to a document from the Pennsylvania Public School Employees' Retirement System, which committed to the fund. The vehicle will invest in a range of situations, from management-led restructurings to sponsor-backed buyouts, the document shows.

Three Hills Capital Partners, London

Three Hills Capital Partners is in early-stage talks with investors to raise up to roughly  400 million ($466.2 million) for its third fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The firm has yet to start formally fundraising for the private-equity and debt vehicle, the person said. If it hits its target, the fund would be double the size of a roughly  200 million predecessor the firm closed more than two years ago. Three Hills targets investments in European companies, with a focus on the retail, consumer, leisure, manufacturing and services sectors. The firm typically seeks to invest  20 million to  60 million of debt or equity in midmarket companies.

ASIA

VENTURE CAPITAL

Long Hill Capital, Shanghai

Long Hill Capital raised $265 million for its second fund for investments in China-based health-care and consumer-focused startups. Limited partners that committed to Long Hill Capital Venture Partners 2 LP include global pension funds, endowments, foundations, financial institutions, family offices and funds of funds. Long Hill was launched by Xiaodong Jiang, a former managing director with New Enterprise Associates, in 2016. The firm's first fund closed at $125 million that year.

Yunfeng Capital, Hong Kong

Yunfeng Capital, the private-equity firm co-founded by Chinese billionaire Jack Ma, raised at least $2.5 billion for its latest investment pool, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The amount raised for Yunfeng Fund III LP and a companion feeder fund matches the listed offering amount. The filing doesn't indicate whether the fund has held a final closing. Yunfeng was established in 2010 by Mr. Ma, the founder of Chinese online-commerce giant Alibaba Group Holding Ltd., and David Feng, who founded Chinese display advertising company Shanghai Target Media Co. Yunfeng makes venture-capital, growth-equity and buyout investments in a range of sectors, from consumer goods to logistics. As well as backing Chinese companies, the firm invests in foreign businesses that have the potential to profit by expanding in China.

This column appeared in the September 2018 issue of Private Equity Analyst, a monthly magazine published by WSJ Pro.

Pro Cyber
One Year After Equifax Breach: Criminal Charges, New State Laws and Lost Chances; One year after credit reporting firm Equifax Inc. announced a major data breach, security and policy experts said it has largely been a missed opportunity.

By Adam Janofsky 
1,333 words
7 September 2018
16:34
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

In the world of cybersecurity, a crisis can be a great motivator. But one year after credit reporting firm Equifax Inc. announced a major data breach, security and policy experts said it has largely been a missed opportunity.

There has been modest progress: A handful of states have passed tougher data breach notification laws and some corporate boards and executives have reexamined their cybersecurity budgets and potential risks. But the push for systemic changes in the way businesses handle data has faded, said Steven Weber, a professor at the University of California, Berkeley School of Information.

"Anyone who was expecting there to be a big dramatic change is going to be disappointed," said Dr. Weber, who also serves as the faculty director for UC Berkeley's Center for Long-Term Cybersecurity.

On September 7, 2017, Equifax said hackers compromised the information of about 143 million U.S. consumers. The company, which collects and sells data on hundreds of millions of consumers and businesses worldwide, revised that number to 145.5 million in October and to 147.9 million in March 2018. The breach was particularly significant because of the type of data Equifax collects -- names, dates of birth, Social Security numbers, driver's license numbers, credit card numbers, phone numbers and email addresses were involved in the breach.

The company and several of its leaders suffered consequences. Chief Executive Officer Richard Smith, Chief Information Officer David Webb and Chief Security Officer Susan Mauldin stepped down following the incident. Additionally, the U.S. Department of Justice filed charges against Jun Ying, a former chief information officer, and Sudhakar Reddy Bonthu, a former software development manager, for insider trading related to the data breach.

A spokeswoman for Equifax said in an email that the firm has increased its security and technology budget by more than $200 million this year and hired a new chief information security officer, Jamil Farshchi, and chief technology officer, Bryson Koehler.

"In the past year we have undertaken a host of security, operational, and technological improvements, including hardening our data security perimeter to dramatically improve detection and response rates, and strengthening our data security protocols and controls," she said in the statement.

But politicians also pushed for bigger changes in the way companies handle consumer data.

Congress called on Mr. Smith to testify about the incident and explain what went wrong. Lawmakers introduced legislation that would, among other things, establish mandatory penalties for breaches of consumer data, require compensation of consumers and create an Office of Cybersecurity that would annually inspect credit reporting firms. The bills have not passed.

"I actually think we can make a difference here &," Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.) told "https://www.marketplace.org/2018/02/28/tech/sen-elizabeth-warren-equifax-may-actually-make-money-breach?mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy" in February 2018, describing how the breach affected both Republicans and Democrats. "It is now up to the federal government to put some rules in place so this doesn't happen again."

Those efforts have largely fizzled out, cybersecurity experts said.

"Do you remember all those great hearings? I testified, and Congress critters on both sides of the aisle asked very angry questions and demanded answers, and nothing happened," said Bruce Schneier, a fellow and lecturer at the Harvard Kennedy School of Government and the Berkman Klein Center for Internet and Society.

States Hold Equifax Accountable

States and companies have made more changes than federal bodies.

In March, Alabama and South Dakota passed data breach notification laws, becoming the last two states to do so. Under South Dakota's law, companies must notify consumers of a breach within 60 days of discovering one or face fines of up to $10,000 per day per violation. Alabama's bill has a 45 day notification requirement, and allows for fines of up to $5,000 per day.

Other states, including Arizona, Colorado, Louisiana and Oregon, have amended their laws to include provisions about credit freezes, expand the definition of personal information and shorten the time to notify victims.

"In the absence of federal legislation and federal initiative, states are becoming the incubators for these laws," said David Stauss, a partner at the law firm Ballard Spahr LLP.

State regulators have also been eager to hold Equifax accountable for the breach.

Massachusetts' attorney general "https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-says-data-breach-possibly-affected-100-000-canadian-consumers-1505834728?mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy" for allegedly violating the state's consumer protection and data privacy laws, and several other state attorneys general pushed the company to explain why it took so long to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/states-push-equifax-to-explain-why-it-took-6-weeks-to-disclose-hack-1509196933?mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy".

In June, eight state regulators, including those from Texas, California and New York, issued a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/equifax-ordered-by-eight-states-to-beef-up-cybersecurity-1530131194?mod=djemCIO_h&mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy&mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy" requiring Equifax to strengthen its cybersecurity defenses and create an annual internal audit program.

The company also faces hundreds of ongoing lawsuits filed by consumers, small businesses, financial institutions and cities.

Trying to Avoid the Same Fate

Corporate boards and executives have reexamined how they handle cybersecurity, hoping that they won't become the next Equifax.

Doug Clare, vice president of cybersecurity at the data analytics and credit scoring company Fair Isaac Corp., commonly referred to as FICO, said there was a "mad dash" to make sure the organization didn't have the same Apache Struts software vulnerability that led to Equifax's breach.

"There was definitely a big scramble & people wanted to make sure they understood their whole network," he said.

Less than a week after the Equifax breach was announced, credit reporting firm TransUnion Corp.'s chief financial officer said at a financial industry conference that the firm uses some of the same software as Equifax, and "made certain that all of the versions of this software are up-to-date with the current patches," The Wall Street Journal "https://www.wsj.com/articles/transunion-ramps-up-response-to-handle-equifax-data-breach-fallout-1505245009?mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy".

Corporate boards have also eyed more long-term changes, said Dr. Weber, who advises several boards in the financial and insurance industries.

The Securities and Exchange Commission in February issued updated guidance asking companies for more detailed descriptions of how boards oversee cyber risk. The agency said it expects companies to disclose details such as how they determine the severity of incidents and how senior executives and directors communicate about cybersecurity.

"Cybersecurity has risen to the top of the board's agenda," said Dr. Weber, adding that many of his clients have increased the time they spend with CISOs and have inquired whether they're spending their budget on the right tools.

At Express Scripts Holding Co., a large shareholder -- the New York State Common Retirement Fund -- proposed shortly after the Equifax breach that the pharmacy benefits company reveal more information about how it manages cyber risk.

Express Scripts tried to prevent the proposal from going to shareholders but was overruled by the SEC. The proposal failed in a shareholder vote.

Equifax itself has also made a number of notable changes in the way it handles cybersecurity. Mr. Farshchi, the company's new CISO, told WSJ Pro Cybersecurity in April that he planned to create a so-called "https://www.wsj.com/articles/new-equifax-ciso-integrations-physical-and-cyber-security-in-post-breach-overhaul-1524774047?mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy" with 30 to 40 employees to improve breach response times. He also plans to bolster the roles of "https://www.wsj.com/articles/new-equifax-ciso-tightens-structure-post-breach-1521235788?mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy" throughout the firm, who will help promote security in day-to-day operations.

Mr. Clare at Fair Isaac says there are also signs that the Equifax incident prompted companies to pay more attention to the third parties with whom they work.

Equifax supplies financial and personal data, credit scoring and services to a large number of other companies, he noted. "One good thing that's come out of it is that organizations are paying more attention to their supply chain security," he said, adding that FICO has seen an uptick in financial and health care firms subscribing to its third-party risk assessment tools.

"Before Equifax, this was a nascent space," said Mr. Clare. "Equifax opened a lot of people's eyes."

Kim S. Nash contributed to this report.

Cyber Daily: Missed Lessons of Equifax

One Year After Equifax Breach: Criminal Charges, New State Laws and Lost Chances

By Adam Janofsky

In the world of cybersecurity, a crisis can be a great motivator. But one year after credit reporting firm Equifax Inc. announced a major data breach, security and policy experts said it has largely been a missed opportunity.

There has been modest progress: A handful of states have passed tougher data breach notification laws and some corporate boards and executives have reexamined their cybersecurity budgets and potential risks. But the push for systemic changes in the way businesses handle data has faded, said Steven Weber, a professor at the University of California, Berkeley School of Information.

"Anyone who was expecting there to be a big dramatic change is going to be disappointed," said Dr. Weber, who also serves as the faculty director for UC Berkeley's Center for Long-Term Cybersecurity.

On September 7, 2017, Equifax said hackers compromised the information of about 143 million U.S. consumers. The company, which collects and sells data on hundreds of millions of consumers and businesses worldwide, revised that number to 145.5 million in October and to 147.9 million in March 2018. The breach was particularly significant because of the type of data Equifax collects -- names, dates of birth, Social Security numbers, driver's license numbers, credit card numbers, phone numbers and email addresses were involved in the breach.

The company and several of its leaders suffered consequences. Chief Executive Officer Richard Smith, Chief Information Officer David Webb and Chief Security Officer Susan Mauldin stepped down following the incident. Additionally, the U.S. Department of Justice filed charges against Jun Ying, a former chief information officer, and Sudhakar Reddy Bonthu, a former software development manager, for insider trading related to the data breach.

A spokeswoman for Equifax said in an email that the firm has increased its security and technology budget by more than $200 million this year and hired a new chief information security officer, Jamil Farshchi, and chief technology officer, Bryson Koehler.

"In the past year we have undertaken a host of security, operational, and technological improvements, including hardening our data security perimeter to dramatically improve detection and response rates, and strengthening our data security protocols and controls," she said in the statement.

Lawmakers introduced legislation that would, among other things, establish mandatory penalties for breaches of consumer data, require compensation of consumers and create an Office of Cybersecurity that would annually inspect credit reporting firms. The bills have not passed.

"I actually think we can make a difference here &," Sen. Elizabeth Warren (D., Mass.) told Marketplace in February 2018, describing how the breach affected both Republicans and Democrats. "It is now up to the federal government to put some rules in place so this doesn't happen again."

Those efforts have largely fizzled out, cybersecurity experts said.

I testified, and Congress critters on both sides of the aisle asked very angry questions and demanded answers, and nothing happened," said Bruce Schneier, a fellow and lecturer at the Harvard Kennedy School of Government and the Berkman Klein Center for Internet and Society.
