Alabama's bill has a 45 day notification requirement, and allows for fines of up to $5,000 per day.

Other states, including Arizona, Colorado, Louisiana and Oregon, have amended their laws to include provisions about credit freezes, expand the definition of personal information and shorten the time to notify victims.

"In the absence of federal legislation and federal initiative, states are becoming the incubators for these laws," said David Stauss, a partner at the law firm Ballard Spahr LLP.

State regulators have also been eager to hold Equifax accountable for the breach.

Massachusetts' attorney general sued Equifax for allegedly violating the state's consumer protection and data privacy laws, and several other state attorneys general pushed the company to explain why it took so long to report the breach.

In June, eight state regulators, including those from Texas, California and New York, issued a consent order requiring Equifax to strengthen its cybersecurity defenses and create an annual internal audit program.

The company also faces hundreds of ongoing lawsuits filed by consumers, small businesses, financial institutions and cities.

Trying to Avoid the Same Fate

Corporate boards and executives have reexamined how they handle cybersecurity, hoping that they won't become the next Equifax.

Doug Clare, vice president of cybersecurity at the data analytics and credit scoring company Fair Isaac Corp., commonly referred to as FICO, said there was a "mad dash" to make sure the organization didn't have the same Apache Struts software vulnerability that led to Equifax's breach.

"There was definitely a big scramble & people wanted to make sure they understood their whole network," he said.

Less than a week after the Equifax breach was announced, credit reporting firm TransUnion Corp.'s chief financial officer said at a financial industry conference that the firm uses some of the same software as Equifax, and "made certain that all of the versions of this software are up-to-date with the current patches," The Wall Street Journal reported.

Corporate boards have also eyed more long-term changes, said Dr. Weber, who advises several boards in the financial and insurance industries.

The Securities and Exchange Commission in February issued updated guidance asking companies for more detailed descriptions of how boards oversee cyber risk. The agency said it expects companies to disclose details such as how they determine the severity of incidents and how senior executives and directors communicate about cybersecurity.

"Cybersecurity has risen to the top of the board's agenda," said Dr. Weber, adding that many of his clients have increased the time they spend with CISOs and have inquired whether they're spending their budget on the right tools.

At Express Scripts Holding Co., a large shareholder -- the New York State Common Retirement Fund -- proposed shortly after the Equifax breach that the pharmacy benefits company reveal more information about how it manages cyber risk.

Express Scripts tried to prevent the proposal from going to shareholders but was overruled by the SEC. The proposal failed in a shareholder vote.

Equifax itself has also made a number of notable changes in the way it handles cybersecurity. Mr. Farshchi, the company's new CISO, told WSJ Pro Cybersecurity in April that he planned to create a so-called fusion center with 30 to 40 employees to improve breach response times. He also plans to bolster the roles of business information security officers throughout the firm, who will help promote security in day-to-day operations.

Mr. Clare at Fair Isaac says there are also signs that the Equifax incident prompted companies to pay more attention to the third parties with whom they work.

Equifax supplies financial and personal data, credit scoring and services to a large number of other companies, he noted. "One good thing that's come out of it is that organizations are paying more attention to their supply chain security," he said, adding that FICO has seen an uptick in financial and health care firms subscribing to its third-party risk assessment tools.

"Before Equifax, this was a nascent space," said Mr. Clare. "Equifax opened a lot of people's eyes."

Kim S. Nash contributed to this report.

*******************************

Federal CISO Calls For More Cyber-Savvy C-Suites

By Catherine Stupp

WASHINGTON -- Educating corporate leaders will be a crucial component of the federal government's new "cybersecurity moonshot" program, a Trump administration initiative that aims to raise awareness and coordinate a nationwide response to cyber challenges.

"It's about making sure that people at the C-Suite understand what cybersecurity is really about and have the level of sophistication," Grant Schneider, the federal chief information security officer, said during a panel discussion at the Billington Cybersecurity Summit here Thursday.

"If our CEOs can only go to our CIOs or CISOs and say,  are we secure yet?', either the CISO is going to say,  no' and get fired right then, or they're going to say  yes' and get fired whenever an incident happens," Mr. Schneider said.

"We need a far more sophisticated conversation where our leadership at the C-Suite are asking questions about what steps we are taking and what is our level of insecurity," he added.

Mr. Schneider was named federal government CISO in July and also serves as senior director for cybersecurity on the National Security Council. He was the federal government's acting CISO before his appointment this summer.

Cybersecurity as a Selling Point

The federal government announced its cybersecurity moonshot program in February and tasked the president's advisory group, known as the National Security Telecommunications Advisory Committee, to identify a "whole-of-nation" cybersecurity strategy to include steps related to education as well as better cooperation between industry and government. The group of 19 executives from telecoms and technology companies is expected to present its strategy to the president in November.

A growing number of companies now sees cybersecurity as a business opportunity, Mr. Schneider said. "We're starting to see technology companies who aren't necessarily cybersecurity companies tout their security level."

Gregory Touhill, who preceded Mr. Schneider as federal CISO, said the moonshot program needs to spell out a role for industry cooperation.

"We had the race to the moon, we had the mission statement that the president [Kennedy] put out where the goal was to take a person to the moon and return them safely. We had a discrete objective. What's our objective here with the cyber moonshot?" Mr. Touhill said in an interview with WSJ Pro Cybersecurity. He left the government CISO position last year.

Companies Can Lead the Way to Better Security

"The government doesn't manufacture anything except for policies and laws, so industry is going to have to manufacture the capabilities that lead to a successful cyber moonshot," he added.

The program should outline better developed cybersecurity education programs and prioritize cooperation with like-minded nations to hold other countries accountable if they sponsor cyber attacks, said Mr. Schneider and Jeanette Manfra, the assistant secretary for cybersecurity and communications at the Department of Homeland Security, speaking on the conference panel.

"If we're still hoping and praying users will just stop clicking on links as our solution, then I think we've failed," Ms. Manfra said. Corporations and government officials should have a more advanced understanding of cybersecurity within the next 10 years, she said.

Both Mr. Schneider and Ms. Manfra acknowledged that the moonshot analogy, which refers to the U.S. government's drive to develop space exploration in the 1960s, isn't directly comparable to the more incremental cybersecurity goals that corporations and government agencies must set to protect against growing cyber threats.

"There isn't going to be an instant where we say we've achieved success in cybersecurity. We're not blasting anyone into cyberspace. It's a different set of challenges," Mr. Schneider said.

*********************************

U.S. Indicts Suspected North Korean Hacker for Sony, Other Attacks

The U.S. unveiled charges Thursday against a North Korean operative in connection with a series of cyber attacks, including the 2014 Sony Pictures hack and a global ransomware attack that infected hundreds of thousands of computers last year, the Journal's Aruna Viswanatha and Dustin Volz report.

A hand in many crimes. The U.S. Department of Justice accused Park Jin Hyok of committing a series of cybercrimes at the North Korean government's direction between 2014 through this year. Along with his alleged role in the Sony breach, Mr. Park allegedly was involved in last year's WannaCry ransomware attack, which infected more than 300,000 computers in more than 150 countries. Prosecutors say Mr. Park also played a part in 2016 theft of $81 million from Bangladesh's account at the Federal Reserve Bank of New York, the largest ever cybertheft from a financial institution.

Details about Pyongyang's hacker army. Roughly 7,000 hackers and support staffers work at the direction of the North Korean government, the Journal reports. Mr. Park had been living in China and working for Chosun Expo, a North Korean front company affiliated with a government hacking organization, according to WSJ. The Justice Department gathered evidence against Mr. Park by executing about 100 search warrants to access more than 1,000 email and social media accounts.

U.S. approach remains constant. The Justice Department previously has identified by name suspected hackers from China, Russia and Iran. Most of those defendants remain overseas but officials have said the so-called name-and-shame strategy is meant to expose hackers and discourage future attacks. The Department of Treasury also enacted financial sanctions on Mr. Park and Chosun Expo as punishment for their alleged cyber activities.

********************************

More Cyber News

Botnet relies on Latvian routers. Cybersecurity researchers from the Chinese firm Netlab 360 have determined that hackers have compromised thousands of internet routers manufactured by the Latvian company MikroTik, Ars Technica reported. The attack exploits a vulnerability in the routers revealed when WikiLeaks published a trove of Central Intelligence Agency hacking tools. MikroTik in April released a patch for the problem but Netlab 360 said 370,000 connected devices still are vulnerable, according to Ars Technica.

Cybersecurity bills move forward. The U.S. House of Representatives passed two bills this week that would give the Department of Homeland Security more cybersecurity authority, CyberScoop reported. The Securing the Homeland Security Supply Chain Act of 2018 would authorize the DHS secretary to stop from contracting with DHS technology vendors that could pose a risk, according to CyberScoop. Another bill would codify into law the DHS' existing Continuous Diagnostics and Mitigation program, which governs how the department detects security threats on federal networks, according to CyberScoop.

Browser extension compromised. The file-sharing site Mega.nz is warning that hackers took control of its browser extension for Alphabet Inc.'s Google Chrome and directed usernames and passwords to a server in Ukraine, according to KrebsOnSecurity. The breach occurred after cybercriminals hacked someone with legitimate rights to alter the extension code, KrebsOnSecurity reported.

A hack targets U.K. festival. A hacker accessed the badges of attendees of Electromagnetic Field, a U.K. technology festival held from Aug. 31 through Sept. 2 for researchers and scientists, Vice Motherboard reported. The electronic badges, which could also function as smartphones, were tricked into revealing device information through a so-called international mobile subscriber identity-catcher, Vice Motherboard reported. The tool mimics signals put out by cell phone towers.

Pro Private Markets
Cooper's Take: Can Private Equity Hedge Its Bets With Asia Deals?

502 words
7 September 2018
12:30
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Upcoming Pro Private Equity Event

Join the WSJ Pro Private Equity community in Boston on Oct. 2 for two in-depth discussions with some of the industry's leading limited partners, including Singapore state investment firm Temasek. WSJ Pro Private Equity members can register for the event "https://conferences.dowjones.com/profile/form/index.cfm?PKformID=0x36310abcd" (registration is free).

Can Private Equity Hedge Its Bets With Asia Deals?

Many private-equity investors cite the heightened risks associated with investing in Asia as reason to avoid the region. But when it comes to margin improvement, Asia offers more opportunities than many firms acknowledge, according to one data provider.

A recent analysis of global private equity-backed deals by analytics company Cepres GmbH found that revenue growth and margin improvement yielded more value on a percentage basis among private-equity deals in Asia than they did with deals in the U.S. and Europe.

Daniel Schmidt, chief executive of Cepres, said there is a greater supply of undermanaged companies in Asia that could benefit financially from private-equity investment. In contrast, many companies in the U.S. and Europe have been sponsor-backed and, thus, already have realized initial growth and cost savings. He added Asian companies tend to be less heavily levered than their U.S. and European peers, which could help them better withstand an economic downturn.

Plenty of private-equity firms see opportunities to capitalize on these dynamics.

Funds focused on emerging Asian countries drove much of the growth among emerging markets fundraising during the first half of the year, according to data from the Emerging Markets Private Equity Association. Funds focused on the region collected $28 billion through June 30, up from $22 billion during the first half of 2017, Empea data show. Excluding emerging Asia, however, overall emerging markets fundraising declined to $3.7 billion during the first half from $7 billion in the year-earlier period, according to Empea.

Despite the opportunities, Asia isn't the easiest market for Western private-equity firms to invest in. Every region has its own culture, language and other barriers these firms face. Currency volatility also presents challenges for firms with dollar- or euro-denominated funds.

But for firms that can successfully manage these challenges, the growth profile of Asian companies could one day offer a hedge against uncertain economic times.

News
China's state investors face higher barriers to US tech

DON WEINLAND 
986 words
6 September 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
13
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Critics say new powers to block deals on security grounds will hinder sector

It took Massachusetts-based Nantero nearly two decades to bring its carbon nanotube memory chips to the market. And when it did, Chinese state investors were quick to grab a small slice of the start-up.

In a $30m funding round in April, the privately held company took in capital from China's largest semiconductor maker, SMIC, and the China Integrated Circuit Fund, a $15bn state investor tasked with helping China's chip industry outpace the US over the next decade. The investment in Nantero was part of a record flow of Chinese venture capital into US technology, an important source of funding for the sector this year.

But national security researchers fear such deals also are being used by staterun Chinese companies to gain access to some of the world's most valuable earlystage technologies   a linchpin issue in the escalating trade and technology war between the US and China.

The Foreign Investment Risk Review Modernisation Act, which took effect in August, pushes back against that perceived threat by empowering the US government to scrutinise and block even small minority foreign investments into sensitive technologies. "The government is concerned that the minority stakes are a backdoor to the technology," said Aaron Cutler, a partner at law firm Hogan Lovells in Washington and a former senior staffer at the US House of Representatives.

The changes reflect a vast expansion in powers of the Committee on Foreign Investment in the US, the body responsible for reviewing inbound investments for national security threats. Critics have said the changes will curb the healthy flow of capital into the US tech sector.

In the past, Cfius reviewed only inbound acquisitions in which control of companies changed hands. The new rules give it authority to screen any deal involving "critical technologies", such as biotech, artificial intelligence and semiconductors.

In doing so, the definition of national security has been broadened to cover the US's greater interests in remaining competitive in these fields against rivals such as China, which is seeking to supplant America as a world leader in many high-tech areas by 2025.

Lawmakers are concerned that small venture investments might open a window through which Chinese companies can acquire valuable technologies, through board appointments, contact with management and other privileges commonly awarded to investors.

"Even without access to any proprietary [intellectual property], Chinese venture investors can use this leverage to promote technology transfer, for example, by encouraging a start-up to establish a subsidiary or joint venture in China where knowhow is likely to diffuse to Chinese employees and partners," said Adam Lysenko, a senior analyst at research house Rhodium Group. Greg Schmergel, Nantero's cofounder and chief executive, said the Chinese infusion was "a no-stringsattached financial investment that does not grant any access to IP or technology".

The deal is one of hundreds of similar transactions in recent years. Chinese venture capital investment in the US between January and May this year hit $2.4bn and is on track to double the record set last year, according to Rhodium Group. State-backed venture capital investment, while still just a quarter of total investment, is also on track to double.

The new regulations are not expected to be retroactive and the Nantero deal has closed, but it is an example of what an emboldened Cfius will be hunting for.

Nantero's fundraising caught the attention of national security researchers largely because the groups the company now counts as investors are those seeking to beat the US on semiconductor technologies over the coming decade.

When Nantero set up a wholly owned foreign company in China in September last year, it chose a sprawling complex that is home to many of the country's top semiconductor start-ups. That development area is a project of Beijing E-Town, a state-run company that has invested heavily in semiconductor assets around the world.

Notably, in its corporate registration in China, Nantero stated that the scope of its business includes "technology transfer"   a primary target of the new Cfius legislation. Nantero said this was a boilerplate phrase added to the registration document.

Months after the China office was established, investors connected to E-Town joined Nantero's fundraising, just as the company's technology was going commercial following 18 years of development.

Those Chinese investors were Shanghai-based China Fortune-Tech Capital and the $15bn China Integrated Circuit Fund.

Fortune-Tech is controlled by flagship state-owned semiconductor maker SMIC, while the IC fund is controlled by China's Ministry of Industry and IT.

Both are connected to E-Town through investments and are part of a state strategy to wean China off reliance on foreign-made chips.

SMIC, E-Town and the $15bn semiconductor fund have not been accused of any wrongdoing. None of the three responded to requests for comment on the matter.

Some experts say the new US law is unnecessary. "Start-ups have strong incentives to protect their technology, even to the point of denying it to their venture capital investors," said John Reynolds, a partner specialising in economic sanctions and national security at law firm Davis Polk.

But regardless of the intentions of Chinese investors, future ventures are expected to face much higher barriers in the US.

"Essentially, an investment will be deemed to be passive only if the investor can prove that it does not result in any exploitable privileged access," said Shawn Cooley, an antitrust lawyer at Freshfields Bruckhaus Deringer and a former director at the US Department of Homeland Security.

Deals that once connected state investors such as SMIC to top US tech such as Nantero are expected to become a thing of the past.

'The government is concerned that minority stakes are a backdoor to the technology'

Metro Money: Wanted: Workers With Autism

By Anne Kadet 
705 words
5 September 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
A10B
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Alexis Prendergast, a 25-year-old Brooklyn woman who has autism spectrum disorder, is the first to admit there are skills she hasn't mastered. "Mostly it's social stuff. I miss some cues," she says. Her wiring also lends unusual strengths, like an ability to focus for long stretches on tasks requiring great attention to detail. Happily, she found an employer looking for someone just like her.

Daivergent, a Manhattan tech startup, provides autistic contractors to clients seeking high accuracy and speed on tasks including data entry and online research. Most people find these jobs tedious and tiring; they get sloppy after an hour, says co-founder and chief executive officer Byran Dai. But Daivergent's autistic workers "can do it for the day, can do it for the week, can do it month after month," he says.

Mr. Dai, who launched his company in February with $50,000 in savings and $100,000 in funding from the Entrepreneurs Roundtable Accelerator, manages a team of four autistic employees at his headquarters and a pool of some 200 contractors around the country who work from home.

In a project Mr. Dai describes as typical, workers digitally labeled items such as doors and windows in blueprints for a company making construction cost-estimate software. The tagged images are used to train an artificial-intelligence system to recognize such objects on its own.

Daivergent's remote workers are paid by the project. They might earn a set amount for tagging a certain number of images, for example. Pay typically works out to $12 to $20 an hour, Mr. Dai says. Employees and contractors can choose their own schedules, and most work between five and 30 hours a week. Daivergent offers no benefits but plans to do so in the future.

For Mr. Dai, who is 28 and grew up in New York City before studying data science and epidemiology at Harvard University and Johns Hopkins Bloomberg School of Public Health, the motivation to start Daivergent was personal. His 19-year-old brother, Brandon, is on the autism spectrum. Four years ago, before their mother died of cancer-related complications, she asked Mr. Dai to look after him.

People on the spectrum often struggle with job interviews and navigating the social aspects of work. An estimated 50% to 75% of people on the autism spectrum experience long-term periods of unemployment. "I want him to have a meaningful life," Mr. Dai says of his brother.

Autistic workers can face other challenges in the workforce once employed. Many people with autism think very literally and may struggle with ambiguity. So Mr. Dai developed his own digital work platform to accommodate Daivergent's workers. It breaks projects into small chunks so workers can easily track their own progress, and the system relies heavily on video instruction.

Employees such as Leon Campbell, a Bronx 23-year-old who recently graduated from Hunter College, say they appreciate this structure. "I was afraid my first job would be overwhelming and give me more stress than even school did," he says. "But this is simpler."

Mr. Dai consulted with employment-services experts at the Arc New York, an advocacy group, while developing the work platform. To refine it further, Daivergent is also consulting with a licensed clinical social worker specializing in young adults on the autism spectrum.

Because the spectrum includes such a range of conditions, Mr. Dai says he doesn't require any specific diagnosis to hire. He typically recruits from organizations that provide support services to people on the spectrum.

Some small outfits already employ workers on the spectrum for remote work, says Dave Kearon, director of adult services for advocacy organization Autism Speaks, but Daivergent stands out for the training it provides. "He's offering not just technical skills but social and communication skills-training."

The Points Guy, a website for consumers aiming to maximize their travel rewards, retained Daivergent to help build the database behind its new app. "It's super complicated, and it's tedious work," says director of product management Alon Hartuv. "But they provided the goods."

Funding Circle s IPO, LendInvest 150m boost, Wonga s woes

A clutch of hopeful start-ups lined up this weekto pitch to one of the toughest audiences around   insurance underwriters and brokers.

The twenty companies were aiming to get intoLloyd s Lab, the latest stab at innovation from Lloyd s of London, the 330-year old insurance market.

After the pitches, the insurers and brokers voted on which ones will get into the Lab, a ten week project during which the start-ups will have the chance to work with people at Lloyd s to develop new products and services.

There might even be some investment at the end of it from either Lloyd s itself or the companies that operate there.

Inga Beale, chief executive of Lloyd s, said:  The essence is not for Lloyd s corporation to end up owning these businesses. We may invest or the [insurers] may invest 	. 	. 	. 	 about access to the market and access to the expertise. 

The 10successful start-ups includedLayr, an artificial intelligence insurance buying platform;CargoSnap, a mobile transportation inspection app;DropIn, a video inspection platform;Insurercore, a digital-based risk appetite directory;BelMead Tech, a blockchain-based claims support platform;Parsyl, a claims system that uses data and predictive analytics;Geollect, creators of proprietary intelligence software for businesses;ZASTI, an AI technology platform;Qnity, a digital design-your own insurance service;iCede, a cloud-based platform for insurers to arrange cross-border cover.

The Lab has been in the works since November last year, and has been developed by Lloyd s along with Boston Consulting Group and L Marks, which specialises in connecting start-ups to established companies.

It has been given a chunk of space on the fourth floor of Lloyd s Richard Rogers-designed London HQ. That s now been turned into a WeWork-esque office, where the underwriters and start-ups can learn to speak each others  languages over a strong cup of coffee.

There will be a lot to learn. The insurers and brokers working at Lloyd s of London do not have a great reputation when it comes to enthusiastically adopting new technology. A marketwide effort to automate some of the existing paper based processes initially met with stiff resistance, and only really took off when Lloyd s decided to force insurers to use the new system.

Ms Beale thinks that the Lab will enthuse the market.  This isn t about removing jobs, it s about changing jobs,  she said.  People will be able to pop in [to the lab] and the idea is that you get people excited. 

The start-ups see plenty of potential at Lloyd s, which insurers everything from ships and buildings to cyber risks and sports events. The early years of insurtech tended to focus on consumer insurance, but lately commercial and specialist cover has attracted interest from innovators and VCs. The Lab had 200 applications from 36 countries.

One of those that gained access to the Lab isDropIn, a Los Angeles-based venture which uses video technology to enable insurers to see the properties they are insuring without having to physically send their own people there.

Gilad Shai, a DropIn advisory board member, says that he hopes the Lab will enable his company to take its offering  to the next level  by working with several companies who insure the same properties.  It is all about developing a service that will meet the needs of Lloyd s,  he said.

Lloyd s sees the Lab as a long-term commitment. After the first 10-week project there will be three more, giving a much wider range of start-ups access to Lloyd s underwriters and brokers.

Ms Beale hopes that the Lab will be able to give some sort of coherence to the innovation efforts being made by the various underwriters and brokers at Lloyd s.  The challenge is to piece it all together so that the market doesn t become chaotic,  she said.

Further fintech fascination

Funding Circle s IPO.Funding Circle is not one to be put off by events outside its control. The peer to peer lender has announced plans to raise 300m in an IPO which could value the company at 1.5bn. Bears will have plenty to say   shares in US listed peers Lending Club and OnDeck have fallen since their IPOs and UK regulators are proposing to make it more difficult for many retail investorsto invest in peer to peer loans. But Anders Povlsen, a businessman and investor, has agreed to back the IPO and valuations of European fintech companies have been rising.

Brick by brickNomura, the Japanese bank, and Magnetar, the US fixed income investor, have committed to invest 150m throughLendInvest, the British peer-to-peer lender to property developers. The duo join a number of existing institutional investors in the platform, including Citigroup and the Merseyside Pension Fund and take the total capital available for loans to about 1bn. Since its launch five years ago, LendInvest has funded more than 1.5bn of loans for small-scale property development projects across the UK.

South Korean start-ups.Fintechs in South Korea should start sharpening their pitching skills. The government has announced that it is to start investing a $9bn fund designed to boost start-ups in areas including fintech, artificial intelligence and drone technology. South Korea s Financial Services Commission and the Ministry of SMEs and Startups will give out $2.8bn this year and $3.3bn in each of the next two years.

Wonga s woes.Payday lender Wonga used technology to push a well-worn business model   high interest loans to people who might struggle to get credit elsewhere. The tech was not enough to save it from an old fashioned collapse though. The company called in the administrators late last week after a surge in compensation claims from disgruntled customers.

Fintech views in the FT Banking podcast

Bits and bobs

Zurich calling.Lloyd s of London is not the only insurance organisation trying to attract start-ups. Last week Zurich launched an  Innovation World Championship  in which start-ups will compete against each other for the right to offer their services to Zurich s customers. The Swiss group is looking for companies with established businesses in areas such as mobility, smart homes and buildings, and financial planning.

Robo-advice reversal.As one Swiss company looks to advance in technology-enabled financial planning, another is beating a retreat. UBS said that week that it was closing its UK robo-advice service less than two years after launch, having concluded that the near term potential of the scheme was limited. Unlike some rival services, UBS s SmartWealth offered a mix of passive and active investment funds, which pushed its costs up.

Singapore/Dubai tie-up.The Monetary Authority of Singapore and the Dubai Financial Services Authority have signed an agreement that allows them to refer fintech companies to each other and share information about financial innovation,reports The Straits Times. They will also work on joint projects in areas such as digital payments, blockchain and big data.

Wealth Management (A Special Report): --- Year-by-Year College Education in Credit Scores: How students can use their four years at school to build a good credit rating -- and to put themselves in a better position postgraduation

By Demetria Gallegos 
1,059 words
4 September 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
R6
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Establishing a credit history and building a good credit score can be a bit of a Catch-22 for young adults.

Take two of my daughters. Just as one was wrapping up her freshman year of college, she applied for a credit card and was denied because she has student loans (even though no payments are due until after she graduates). Her older sister, who was about to graduate without any student loans, was denied because she had no credit record at all.

It used to be that card applications were thrust into the hands of college students during freshman orientation. But it became harder for young adults to get a card following passage of the Credit CARD Act of 2009, which requires those under age 21 to have a cosigner or proof of independent income.

So what is a young adult to do? Here are some steps to consider, year by year, to help students begin building a strong credit profile.

Freshman Year

Parents can start by adding their college freshman as an authorized user on one of their credit cards -- but should do so only if they have good credit scores themselves, says Nancy Bistritz-Balkan, vice president of consumer education and advocacy at credit-reporting firm Equifax Inc. If they don't, it could hurt the student right out of the gate. A good parental score is in the high 600s or above, says Ethan Dornhelm, vice president of scores and analytics at FICO, which created the credit-scoring model most lenders see.

Another good early option is a secured credit card, says Bruce McClary, vice president of communications at the National Foundation for Credit Counseling, a nonprofit in Washington, D.C. No credit check is required since the card is funded by depositing cash. The consumer essentially spends his or her own money and replenishes it each month by paying the bill.

At age 18, students can begin requesting free credit reports each year from the big three credit-reporting companies, Experian PLC, TransUnion and Equifax via annualcreditreport.com. This allows them to check for fraudulent activity and begin to understand how their profile is built. The reports won't include a FICO score, however. Students may be able to get that free through their bank or by using Discover Credit Scorecard, an online tool. Free scores also are available through financial apps such as Mint and Credit Karma, but they use a model known as VantageScore 3.0, which can vary from the FICO score.

Sophomore Year

Some students have a car by now, subsidized by parents. Parents may be making the car payments, while the child covers gas and insurance costs. One way to help the student start building a credit history is to flip that arrangement: Have the student cosign the auto loan and make the payments, while the parents cover insurance and other costs. One caveat: The debt associated with that loan could hurt a child on future credit applications.

Experts say paying utility, cellphone and subscription bills on time, as well as responsible debit-card use, won't bolster a student's credit history. But students who live off campus and make reliable monthly rent payments can use services such as Rental Kharma or ClearNow to have those payments reported to credit bureaus, says Jeff Richardson, vice president of marketing for VantageScore Solutions. Many of these services require landlords to be registered and charge monthly fees.

For the first solo, unsecured credit application, a gas or retail credit card is a good way to go, says Mrs. Bistritz-Balkan. The amounts approved are often low, so even a part-time job can provide enough income to qualify. Use it regularly but never let your balance grow to more than 30% of the available credit line. And pay it off in full each month.

Junior Year

If they can swing it, students may want to begin making small payments on student debt -- even if they anticipate taking out more loans for the final two years of college. The lender will report payments to credit agencies, even for amounts much smaller than the full payments due after graduation. If students make the payments with an automatic debit plan, they may qualify for a small interest-rate reduction. Students should first research whether there are drawbacks to making payments before the loan is due, as might be the case for those entering fields that include loan-forgiveness plans.

In the case of my younger child, while her student loans initially led to a denial, she was ultimately able to be approved for a student credit card (with a $500 limit) because when she appealed she was able to demonstrate that her remaining college fund was worth more than the loans that had sunk her. So students may want to apply for credit before the full four years of student loans have been issued, and while college savings can still be leveraged.

Students who have had a secured credit card for a year or two with a perfect payment history should be able to graduate to an unsecured one now. They should compare deals from various card issuers, including their current lender.

Senior Year

Students who still don't have a credit card of any kind should consider applying for a student card before graduation. These have relatively low credit lines, but less stringent underwriting so they aren't as dependent on income.

Those who have had a student card for a while with a stellar record should ask their lender for more favorable terms, such as lower rates and lower -- or no -- annual fees.

"If you've have several years of no missed payments and your balances are relatively low, then there's no reason your FICO score wouldn't be in the high 600s or above. . .a pretty solid score that could qualify you for mainstream credit," says Mr. Dornhelm.

---

Ms. Gallegos is a news editor for The Wall Street Journal in New York. She can be reached at demetria.gallegos@wsj.com.

News
The robots are retreating but will have their revenge

Tom Braithwaite 
623 words
1 September 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
9
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Ted Dimon still turned up for work at JPMorgan in Manhattan until shortly before he died two years ago at 85.

Starting in 1953, Mr Dimon had worked as a broker at a variety of leading Wall Street institutions, managing investment portfolios. That longevity is impressive in any career, particularly one whose success is influenced heavily by moody markets. It was also accomplished despite technological change.

Throughout his life Mr Dimon dealt with a raft of new electronic tools. They did not "disrupt" him. He chose which to adopt and which to ignore. He used a computer, for example, but insisted it displayed an old-style ticker tape.

Towards the end of his career came the rise of digital platforms, including "robo-advisers" that cut out humans like Mr Dimon in favour of algorithms. It is unlikely he was too troubled.

The robo-advisers accumulated more hype than assets. The largest, Betterment, announced last year that it had surpassed $10bn of assets under management, which sounds impressive until you recall that Bank of America's wealth arm is 300 times bigger.

This week came a sign of robot retreat: UBS said it was scrapping an electronic-investment platform. That should be no surprise. As another elder statesman of finance, Paul Volcker, likes to say, the last useful innovation in the sector was the cash machine.

Yet, as The Terminator teaches us, the rise of machines can be insidious. One day you are tinkering with artificial intelligence; the next you are in a fight to the death with shape-shifting genocidal androids.

One factor obscuring the upheaval is monetary policy. Goldman Sachs dived into consumer banking two years ago, with a sleek online product, "Marcus", that outshines the established banks and is about to launch in the UK. Crucially, it offers much higher rates on current accounts: 1.85 per cent in the US compared with a paltry 0.01 per cent at Bank of America.

After inflation, though, customers are still making a slightly negative return on their cash and Goldman is not vacuuming as many deposits as it otherwise might. As policymakers continue to hike rates, that will change. More people will shop around for a low-cost solution paying real returns and the suction should be significant.

Generational change will also play a role. A few years ago one bank chief executive, with a big wealth management business, told me that he was not worried what young people wanted: they didn't have any money.

Yet the oldest millennials are now in their mid-30s. Not all of them spend their entire salary on avocado toast. Some have real assets to invest. They have got in the habit of using low-cost, no-frills platforms and are less likely to be as willing as their parents to pay large fees to human advisers.

Traditional institutions are wrestling with the innovator's dilemma: throw everything at satisfying the demand for easy, low-cost technology or try to defend a high-margin business that has proven profitable for decades.

Ted Dimon's son has made his choice. Jamie, chief executive at JPMorgan, this week revealed You Invest, a new service that offers free trading and stock research   both of which used to cost a fortune. The younger Mr Dimon has likened the effort to Amazon Prime, where a single fee comes with an ever-increasing array of services.

Nobody starting as a broker today will be doing the same job in 2081.

Tom Braithwaite is the FT companies editor

Risk taking in US commercial property market runs hot

Joe Rennison in New York 
607 words
31 August 2018
04:01
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Investors accept smaller premium for taking on risk in bonds backed by commercial mortgages

Investors are stomaching the lowest premium in more than a decade for taking on more risk in the US commercial property market, as a humming American economy encourages money managers to reach for higher returns.

The difference between the return on the safest slices of commercial mortgage-backed securities   a pool of mortgages bundled into a bond   and the riskier slices has dropped to its lowest level since the build-up to the financial crisis, according to data from Trepp, a data analytics company.

A combination of an expanding US economy and the ongoing hunt for yield is driving investors  willingness to assume more exposure to potential losses without greater compensation. For some it is another sign that a nearly decade-old credit cycle maybe approaching a turning point.

 As you get toward the latter innings of the credit cycle, people have money they need to put to work and they take on more risk for less return,  said Alan Todd, a CMBS analyst at Bank of America Merrill Lynch.

CMBS bundle pools of mortgages on commercial buildings such as offices and malls into a single bond, with the interest paid by the mortgage repayments of borrowers. The debt is sliced into tranches, with higher returns offered to those prepared to absorb potential losses on the underlying mortgages first.

Demand has waned for senior, or  triple-A , tranches of new CMBS deals, while it has remained for riskier, or  triple-B minus , slices, narrowing the gap between the two to the lowest level since May 2007, according to Trepp.

As the Federal Reserve has tightened monetary policy, higher quality fixed-rate investments such as the triple-A tranches of CMBS have fallen in price, prompting investors to seek out floating-rate assets like company loans, or take more credit risk to boost returns.

 You are probably not getting paid for the risk you are taking and that definitely concerns us,  said Dushyant Mehra, co-chief investment officer at Hildene.  We are cautious about this environment. 

There has also been a deluge of supply in the CMBS market, with $49bn of new issuance between January and July, topping the $45bn sold in the same period in 2017.

It has contributed to the credit premium between triple-A rated tranches and triple-B minus tranches   which sit one rating above junk   shrinking to 2.1 per cent in August from 2.2 per cent in July and below its previous post-crisis low of 2.3 per cent touched in July 2014, according to the Trepp data. While it marks the lowest premium for investing in the lower tranches of CMBS securities since May 2007, it is still far from pre-crisis lows of 67 basis points hit at the beginning of that year.

 It is something everyone frets over,  Gunter Seeger, a portfolio manager at fund manager PineBridge, said of the evaporating premium investors are demanding.  You are always concerned that the pendulum swings too far but the reach for yield is still there. 

Concerns over the presence of mortgages tied to retail buildings in CMBS have also abated, pushing prices higher for lower-rated tranches. Given the strength of the US economy, many investors remain sanguine about the risk of an imminent drop in prices for US corporate debt. But others point out that the robust appetite for risk echoes the period before the financial crisis, raising concern over the scale of the fallout when the economy slows down.

Pro Cyber
Cyber Daily: Get Time On Your Side When Fighting Hackers

1,845 words
30 August 2018
12:47
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Aflac Wants to Tempt Hackers into Opening the Wrong Door

By Jeff Stone

Insurer Aflac Inc. nearly three years ago began inserting into its corporate network digital tripwires to trick hackers into revealing their presence inside the company's systems.

Cybercriminals who click on links disguised as employee credentials or sensitive human resources data in fact are raising a red flag for the security team. Many thousands, perhaps millions, of so-called honeypots now exist in Aflac's remote desktop software, in file-transfer tools, near sensitive databases and on employee machines, said DJ Goldsworthy, director of security operations and threat intelligence.

Aflac uses this deceptive technology as a last line of defense against hackers and rogue insiders who sneak around the company's other security protocols, said Mr. Goldsworthy.

"Think about your house: If you had some precious jewelry you wanted to protect, deception would be like having multiple doors that lead to nowhere," he said. "And behind every door there are multiple safes, where if you touch the wrong safe it alerts the police to your presence. To steal the jewelry, thieves would have to know which safe to open without getting caught."

Honeypots can consist of compact bits of code, and can be implemented throughout an organization with automation tools.

Mr. Goldsworthy in an interview with WSJ Pro Cybersecurity explained why the security team has used honeypots as a failsafe technique. Here are edited excerpts:

Q: How do you use honeypots?

A: We're conducting weekly scans with a standard vulnerability scanner. We have a tool that has an inventory of all our applications in our system, and it lets us know if any vulnerabilities are identified in our environment in real-time. We're doing state and dynamic code analysis for all the code we write. We're also using penetration testing constantly, and have four individuals slated for our red team who are doing manual testing all the time.

And then we have this [honeypot] strategy to compensate for anything that's not resolved by any of those controls. Say we did a really bad job patching, and someone uses one of those holes to get in and move around the network. They would bump into one of our decoys...and alert us to their presence. It's to reduce the risk of any residual vulnerabilities.

Q: How often do hackers actually trigger these alerts and what do you learn?

A: Not often. We've had some penetration testers be detected and we learned they can't tell if they're interacting with a fake system, so they spend a lot of time trying to get into that. That gives us more time to respond, and we get a good forensic view that tells us all their techniques, tactics and procedures.

We've also been able to identify third parties trying to access things they really had no legitimate business use for. One example was a developer attempting to get access to something that didn't exist. We looked at the restrictions on their account and made sure they only had the access required for code development. Then we worked with the vendor to reiterate the security elements of our agreement, and I think they gave that person a warning.

There was no evidence to indicate the person was after something with malicious intent, but it was serious enough to be a concern.

************************************

What Businesses Can Learn From NIST's Advice for Securing Medical Devices

By Adam Janofsky

As organizations across industries confront the risks introduced by the Internet of Things, one government agency is sounding the alarm on a connected device that has potentially deadly consequences.

The National Institute of Standards and Technology's National Cybersecurity Center of Excellence has spent more than two years studying threats posed by wireless infusion pumps, which are widely used by healthcare organizations to deliver fluid to patients in a controlled manner. The agency last week offered a number of suggestions for securing these devices against threats such as denial-of-service attacks that can stop a pump from functioning.

Although the guidance covers a specific device, many of the recommendations apply to connected equipment that may be unrelated to health care, said NCCE deputy director Tim McBride. These measures, such as installing firewalls to limit a device's access to the rest of a corporate network and block it from the internet, can help prevent malware infections, data loss and misuse of IoT devices, he said.

Divide your network. NIST recommends a multilayered approach for protecting infusion pumps and other connected devices, but one of the most critical measures is splitting an organization's network into smaller and more manageable parts that aren't integrated.

In the guidance, NIST describes what it calls a zone-based security approach, which involves using different local networks for designated purposes. In this scenario, a hospital's system might be divided into a dozen networks, each protected by different safeguards depending on the sensitivity of the information and devices they contain.

For example, a guest network that allows visitors and patients to access the internet might need fewer security controls than a database server that contains personal health information regulated by the Health Insurance Portability and Accountability Act. For infusion pumps, there might be a biomedical engineering zone that contains pump-management servers, a medical device zone that contains the infusion pump system itself, and a remote access server for external vendor support.

NIST recommends that a medical device zone use firewalls and other security controls to block access to the internet and other parts of the network, which would help prevent hackers from compromising the devices.

"We found that a lot of health care and hospital networks are rather flat, so by segmenting the network like this we can eliminate [wireless infusion pumps] from being a big security problem," said Mr. McBride.

Fatal consequences. Wireless infusion pumps and connected medical devices in general are similar technologically to many IoT devices in other industries, such as smart refrigerators and connected sensors, said Sue Wang, a cybersecurity engineer at the non-profit Mitre Corp. These devices often have limited computing power, weak default passcodes and can be challenging to patch, she said.

But unlike many other IoT devices, infusion pumps face cyberthreats that can be fatal. For example, NIST's report highlights malware infections that can cause changes to prescribed dosages, denial-of-service attacks that interfere with the pump's functioning and targeted attacks that can lead to unauthorized access to patient health information.

The guidance was published about two weeks after security researchers at Black Hat USA 2018 in Las Vegas demonstrated how to take over an insulin pump using radio signals. The researchers, speaking to thousands of cybersecurity professionals, used what they described as a cheap radio device to instruct a pump to withhold a dose of insulin.

"Whatever the remote programmer can do, we can do that," said Jonathan Butts, founder of QED Secure Solutions, referring to the programming devices health care professionals use to monitor and adjust the devices.

NIST, a division of the U.S. Department of Commerce, publishes a cybersecurity framework that is widely used by government agencies and private companies. The framework describes best practices for securing data, such as how to encrypt sensitive information and install critical updates.

Kids Are Getting Hit in Identity Fraud Schemes

New legislation to take effect Sept. 21 will give parents the ability to check and freeze credit files in their children's names at no cost, the Journal reports. The law is part of a broader banking law that legislators passed in response to a data breach at credit reporting company Equifax Inc. last year.

ID theft via stolen Social Security numbers. One in 50 credit applications that were filed last year under new Social Security numbers were connected to false identities, according to an estimate from ID Analytics, an identity-fraud prevention unit of Symantec Corp. About 147.9 million U.S. consumers' data was exposed in the Equifax breach, including Social Security numbers.

Children targeted by fraudsters. Experts say children are increasingly becoming victims of identity theft because they have clean credit histories and parents rarely check their files. Children usually do not have credit files unless their parents or criminals have opened them in their names. But ID Analytics recommends that parents should create credit files for their children, since the credit-reporting system recognizes the first person to claim an identity as that person.

More Cyber News

Air Canada confirms breach. Air Canada said in an email to customers that a breach of its mobile app may affect 20,000 people, TechCrunch reported Wednesday. Cybercriminals may have accessed customer names, passport numbers, passport country of issuance and other personal information, the airline said. It wasn't clear whether hackers breached Air Canada's systems, or reused username and password credentials stolen from other sites to access Air Canada's app, according to TechCrunch.

Old protocol renders new phones vulnerable. Cybersecurity researchers told Wired magazine they found a new way to bypass security mechanisms and unlock screens on Android phones. The technique uses Attention, or AT, commands, a 1980s-era protocol companies used to instruct phones to dial or hang up a call, according to Wired. AT commands now are used for SMS testing and functions, such as activating a camera. Numerous phone manufacturers leave AT commands open to anyone with a USB port, meaning attackers could inject malicious commands through a USB cable, Wired reported.

Iranian propaganda campaign larger than reported. An Iranian influence operation involved a large number of anonymous websites and social media accounts in 11 languages, according to Reuters. Facebook Inc. last week said it removed pages that were spreading messages from Iranian state media, but a Reuters analysis identified 10 more websites and social media accounts on Facebook's Instagram, Twitter Inc., and Alphabet Inc.'s YouTube. "The Iranian operation relied on quantity, not quality, but it stayed undetected for years," Ben Nimmo, a senior fellow at the Atlantic Council's Digital Forensic Research Lab, told Reuters.

Ransomware techniques evolve. Cybercriminals are threatening to use fake negative reviews and complaints on social media about CheapAir.com if the flight comparison website doesn't pay a ransom, Vice Motherboard reported. CheapAir's chief executive told Motherboard the company will not pay the extortionists' demand of 1.5 bitcoin, or about $10,500. Ransomware attackers typically use malicious software to encrypt an organization's sensitive files, saying the materials will be unlocked only after the firm pays up.

U.S. News: Now Even Children Face ID Theft

By Yuka Hayashi 
768 words
29 August 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
A2
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON -- Parents have a new item to add to their financial to-do list: check their child's credit history.

A new federal law going into effect in September will make it easier for families to combat the growing problem of identity fraud of minors, allowing them to make inquiries about credit files in their child's name and freeze a file at no cost.

Data-security experts say children are increasingly targeted by thieves who steal their Social Security numbers to create fake or "synthetic" identities, then open credit cards, take out loans or apply for public assistance. One credit-reporting company, Experian, estimates such identity theft will affect one in four children before they become an adult.

The ability to check children's credit reports is part of a broader banking law that also allowed unlimited, free credit freezes for adults, a response to a massive data breach at Equifax Inc. last September.

Criminals see the clean credit histories of children as particularly attractive, in part because parents rarely check on them. That often allows theft to go unnoticed for years, until the victims reach college age and start applying for credit cards and student loans. Also, a 2011 change in how the government assigns Social Security numbers to babies and new immigrants made it easier to steal numbers without being discovered, experts said.

Young children don't have credit files unless parents or guardians open them -- or someone committing fraud did.

Last year, the Federal Trade Commission received 14,000 complaints involving identity theft targeting people 19 and younger, about 3.7% of complaints for all age groups. But experts say those numbers may understate the problem because parents are often unaware of children being targeted.

"We have found children are increasingly victims of identity theft," said Suzanne Barber, director of the Center for Identity at the University of Texas at Austin. "If I were going to patchwork an identity, whose would I want to use? I'd probably use the identity that is checked least often."

Al Pascual, an analyst specializing in fraud and security at Javelin Strategy & Research, said a child's personal information is more valuable on the dark web than an adult's, with a full set of personal information known as "fullz" -- name, date of birth, address and Social Security number -- "going for 10 times, 20 times more" than an adult file, which typically fetches $5 per person.

ID Analytics, an identity-fraud prevention unit of Symantec Corp., estimates 2.2% -- or one in every 50 -- of credit applications filed under newly issued Social Security numbers carried fake identities last year, up from 1.2% in 2013. Many of those numbers are believed to belong to young children.

Once a child's personal information is stolen, cleaning up the aftermath can be difficult.

Jill Carlon, a Peoria, Ariz., mother of three, said she spent seven years fixing her daughter's credit file after receiving a call in 2011 about a new J.C. Penney Co. credit card opened using her then 7-year-old daughter's Social Security number. More accounts followed, including at Dillards Inc., Macy's Inc. and Fry's Electronics Inc. Then collection notices came from a local hotel.

"As a mother, I am supposed to be there to protect her and do everything I can," said Ms. Carlon, a 46-year-old nurse. "It was out of my control."

Ms. Carlon tried to close the accounts, but was turned away because the name on the accounts didn't match her daughter's, Avery. She called banks, credit-reporting companies, the police and lawyers, then turned to politicians for help.

The Carlons changed their daughter's first and middle names in an attempt to get her a new Social Security number. Finally, earlier this year, she was issued a new number.

Once the provision in the new law goes into effect Sept. 21, parents should contact the three main credit-reporting companies -- Experian, Equifax and TransUnion -- to check on their children's credit files, experts say. Most young children shouldn't have credit files, though some experts recommend parents create a file for their child, then freeze it.

Ken Meiser, chief compliance officer at ID Analytics, says the credit-reporting system is built on the idea that the first person to claim an identity is that person. "It makes sense for a parent to make sure that they have created that file and protected it," he said.

Paul Murphy

Chief executive and financier in tit-for-tat allegations over company s bankruptcy

A veteran corporate financier who previously worked for Blackstone has been accused of orchestrating the fraudulent bankruptcy of a company he had bought so that he could repurchase it on the cheap.

Chinh Chu, who established his investment firm CC Capital after a 25-year stint working on multibillion-dollar private equity deals for Blackstone, bought US healthcare software company Constellation Healthcare Technologies last year. At the time Constellation was listed on London s Aim.

Within months of the acquisition, Constellation s founding chief executive Paul Parmar had departed, Mr Chu claimed he had been subject to a  complex fraud  and both the US Securities and Exchange Commission and the US Department of Justice had launched actions against Mr Parmar.

Constellation filed for bankruptcy in March, claiming that some of the businesses it had purchased while Mr Parmar was in charge were fictitious. Investors and banks such as Bank of America Merrill Lynch, who financed the takeover have allegedly been left with uncollectable debts.

Mr Parmar s attorneys openly admit that there were irregularities at the time that Constellation was de-listed from Aim, but claim that Mr Chu had full access to the real information and went ahead with the transaction regardless.

Mr Parmar claims that Mr Chu let Constellation slide into bankruptcy and then rigged the subsequent auction of assets to his benefit.

His legal team is trying to get the charges against him dropped, claiming that Mr Chu made false representations to the authorities in a  brazen move  to stop Mr Parmar  derailing this elaborate plan  to repurchase Constellation after bankruptcy proceedings, according to court documents.

 It is utterly implausible that such a highly sophisticated investor could spend $7m on due diligence and then make an investment decision based on a few inaccurate statements made very early in the process by defendant Parmar, rather than the mountains of data that were subsequently provided,  said court documents filed by Mr Parmar s legal team.

CC Capital said:  We deny the false and outrageous allegations from Mr Parmar, who stands accused by the US Department of Justice and the US Securities and Exchange Commission of perpetrating a brazen and elaborate fraud involving false statements, fictitious documents and sham companies. We will continue to co-operate fully with the law enforcement agencies that are investigating his extensive fraud. 

The court documents refer to a series of questions that were originally raised by the FT s Alphaville finance blog before the takeover about a string of supposed bolt-on acquisitions made by Constellation before its go-private discussions with CC Capital had been made public.

 While such an article would normally cause a deal like this to be derailed or, at a minimum, be delayed to perform additional due diligence, BofA requested none and Chinh Chu incredibly had the opposite reaction, demanding to immediately close the deal,  state the documents submitted by Mr Parmar s legal team.

In the court filings, Mr Parmar insisted that Mr Chu was aware the bolt-on acquisitions identified by FT Alphaville were in fact empty corporate shells, despite Constellation having raised scores of millions of pounds via Aim share placements to buy them.

FBI agents arrested Mr Parmar in May along with two of his former colleagues, Sotirios  Sam  Zaharis and Ravi Chivukula, and charged the men with securities fraud. The SEC has launched a parallel civil action.

Mr Chu targeted Constellation as his first post-Blackstone deal, worth $309m.

Associates of Mr Chu say he is mortified that the transaction has collapsed amid a welter of legal claims, but point to the fact that he is still pursuing other deals, with CC Capital recently leading a $6.9bn move to take data analytics firm Dun & Bradstreet private.

Insurance Vision Fund injects $100m with a view to tapping expertise for investments

SoftBank's Vision Fund is to make a $100m investment to expand the technology business of Chinese online insurer ZhongAn.

The $100bn investment fund, which has shaken up technology investing over the past three years, will own a majority stake in the business, with plans to use ZhongAn's technology in a number of its investments, such as Uber and its rival Didi Chuxing.

ZhongAn, which will also invest $100m in the joint venture, was launched in 2013 by China's Ant Financial, Tencent and Ping An Insurance. It attracted early attention by offering unusual products such as a policy to cover customers who got alcohol poisoning during the World Cup. More recently, it has diversified into technology. with a large business focused on blockchain, artificial intelligence and other developments.

It has started a project that uses blockchain to track farm-raised chickens.

The tech business employs more than 1,500 engineers and technicians   more than half of ZhongAn's total staff. Until now, its operations have been limited mainly to business within China.

One aim of the SoftBank deal is to export ZhongAn tech to overseas insurance companies, financial technology groups and healthcare operators.

But the first focus would be on how other companies that the Vision Fund has invested in can use ZhongAn technology, said Francis Tang, the company's chief financial officer. "They use their network; we use our technology," Mr Tang said. "The natural [strategy] was to go to SoftBank's portfolio. Working on that will help us take the company global."

The Vision Fund, which has backers including Saudi Arabia's Public Investment Fund, Apple and Qualcomm, has invested tens of billions of dollars in companies such as WeWork, Paytm and Uber. SoftBank was also a cornerstone investor in ZhongAn's $1.5bn IPO in 2017.

ZhongAn provided insurance to more than 300m customers in the first half of 2018. Its insurance premium income during that time increased by 107 per cent to Rmb5.2bn ($761m) compared with the same period last year, it said yesterday in its interim report.

It is lossmaking, however, and its margins remain thin: its most recent premium contribution was just Rmb17 per person. In the first six months of the year, net losses expanded by more than 130 per cent to Rmb666.8m. Part of the losses derive from fees ZhongAn pays to other internet companies for allowing it to offer insurance to their customers.

Quartz Launches Paid Cryptocurrency Newsletter; Newsletter will cost $15 per month, or $150 annually

By Benjamin Mullin 
614 words
28 August 2018
15:15
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Quartz is the latest media company attempting to cash in on the growing enthusiasm for cryptocurrency.

The online business-news startup is launching "https://qz.com/private-key/", a newsletter that aims to guide its subscribers through the often turbulent markets for virtual currencies such as bitcoin and Ethereum.

For $15 a month or $150 a year, readers will have access to a twice-weekly newsletter that summarizes and analyzes the rapidly shifting world of cryptocurrencies and the decentralized accounting technology that supports them. The newsletter is the first paid product that Quartz has released since it was "https://www.wsj.com/articles/quartz-to-be-bought-by-japans-uzabase-for-as-much-as-110-million-1530512254" by the Japanese financial intelligence and media firm business intelligence firm Uzabase.

A paid cryptocurrency newsletter hits multiple sweet spots for Quartz, which was an early adopter of both free newsletters and coverage of bitcoin, said Kevin Delaney, the co-president and editor in chief of Quartz. The company gauged reader interest in the product before it launched and managed to sign up about 50 paying customers weeks in advance, Mr. Delaney said.

In an interview, Mr. Delaney said the heightened interest in virtual currency opened up a niche for Quartz because many potential consumers don't have the information they need to make informed decisions.

"Despite surging interest in them, cryptocurrencies remain opaque to most potential investors. It's surprisingly difficult to find clear and useful information on the web," Mr. Delaney said. "Quartz Private Key is stepping into that void with analysis of new coins, regulations, emerging uses of blockchain, and other information that's essential to being a smart cryptocurrency investor."

Quartz Private Key will face competition from other newsletters that focus on cryptocurrency. Anthony Pompliano, a founding partner at the asset management firm Morgan Creek Digital, writes "https://offthechain.substack.com/" called "Off the Chain" that provides daily analysis of cryptocurrency markets.

Mr. Pompliano says the newsletter, which costs $30 a month, has already attracted "thousands" of subscribers. CoinDesk, a news site that specializes in digital currencies, also offers a mix of daily and weekly newsletters. And many major news organizations, from Bloomberg to The Wall Street Journal, write regularly about the world of cryptocurrencies.

Uzabase bought Quartz for between $75 million and $110 million, according to The Wall Street Journal. Uzabase said the acquisition was driven, in part, by the potential of working with Quartz to build a paid subscription business.

The deal was structured with a so-called earn-out, with the final acquisition price determined by Quartz hitting revenue goals. A company spokesperson said Quartz was still on track to meet its projected revenue goal of $35 million to $38 million for 2018. Last year, Quartz booked $28 million in revenue but wasn't profitable.

Like many media companies, Quartz is turning to subscriptions as the market for digital advertising becomes increasingly competitive. As major tech firms, including Alphabet Inc.'s Google and Facebook Inc., tighten their grip on new digital spending, media outlets are looking for alternate revenue sources.

Quartz' U.S. readership has held steady over the past two years, according to data from web analytics firm comScore. In 2017, Quartz attracted about 11.87 million average monthly U.S. visitors; so far this year, Quartz has averaged about 11.67 million monthly U.S. visitors, according to comScore.

For Quartz, paid products have been a long time coming. In 2017, the company acquired Intelligentsia.ai, an artificial intelligence research firm, and explored the creation of a paid research business before shelving the idea. Quartz also ran a high-end paid events business before canceling its flagship conference last year.

Cyber Daily: States Clamp Down on Breached Companies

1,299 words
28 August 2018
12:52
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Colorado Law Tightens Timeframe for Data Breach Reporting

By Adam Janofsky

Businesses seeking to comply with the 50 state data breach laws on the books should take note of a strict Colorado consumer data privacy act that goes into effect September 1.

The new law has the tightest reporting deadline of any state breach notification act, giving companies 30 days to provide notice of a breach involving personal information belonging to Colorado residents, according to David Stauss, a Denver-based partner at the law firm Ballard Spahr LLP. Most states have 45 or 60 day reporting requirements, and many allow for exceptions.

The Colorado law is the latest in a push among state regulators for stronger cybersecurity and privacy laws, attorneys say. Alabama and South Dakota passed data breach notification laws in March, becoming the last two states to do so. States also are expected to consider data privacy legislation to restrict and oversee data-collection practices following the lead of California, which passed a wide-reaching bill in June.

Colorado also expanded the definition of personal information to include biometric data, driver's license numbers, passwords and other items. In other state laws, personal information is often limited to a first name or initial and last name in combination with an identifier, such as a Social Security number.

What companies must do: Given the shorter time frame for reporting a breach, businesses should examine their internal response plans, taking stock of how often they conduct penetration tests and audit their systems for vulnerabilities, said Cynthia Larose, an attorney specializing in privacy matters with Mintz Levin in Boston.

"They're going to need to be able to demonstrate to regulators that they've got a solid incident response plan and that they can implement it--and it's not just something you can grab off the internet," she said.

The recent push for stricter data breach laws, including the ones in Alabama and South Dakota, has largely been motivated by the breach Equifax Inc. reported in September 2017 that compromised the personal information of 147.9 million U.S. consumers, according to Pam Greenberg, a policy researcher with the National Conference of State Legislatures.

In May, Vermont enacted a law that was the first of its kind, specifically regulating data brokers who buy and sell personal information. That law requires data brokers to register annually with the state attorney general and disclose their practices for collection, storage and sale of personal information, said Ms. Larose. The report must also include the number of data breaches during the prior year, and the total number of consumers affected.

The GDPR effect: Although U.S. laws are getting stricter, they're still lenient compared to the European Union's General Data Protection Regulation, which went into effect in May. That law requires firms to disclose breaches within 72 hours, and companies that fail to comply with GDPR could face fines as large as 4% of their global annual revenue or  20 million, whichever is higher.

It's likely that states will start passing stricter data privacy laws in 2019, following the lead of California, which passed a broad data-privacy bill in June that requires businesses to delete certain data upon request, among other mandates, said Ms. Larose. Technology firms, fearful of this push, are working with policy makers to shape potential federal regulation that would pre-empt state laws, The Wall Street Journal reported.

"Tech companies believe that if other states follow the California model... it's going to be a complete disaster," said Mr. Stauss. "I think what you'll see is tech companies changing their tune and preferring federal regulation, because one law is better than 50."

*******************************

China Has an Advantage with Big Data

China's "more totalitarian regime" gives the government freedom to access citizens' data with less regard for privacy compared to other countries, Tadashi Maeda, the governor of the state-owned Japan Bank for International Cooperation told WSJ's Peter Landers in an interview. Data protection laws make it difficult for other governments including Japan's to collect and analyze their citizens' data.

International data rules? Mr. Maeda said in the interview that the U.S., European countries and Japan should agree on joint rules for data use to counter China's growing strength in the area. The national strategy "Made in China 2025" may give Chinese companies an advantage over competitors as they use their access to troves of personal data like online chats to develop artificial intelligence.

China's tech stocks take a hit. Last week, China and the U.S. raised new tariffs totaling $16 billion on one another's goods, in the latest development in the two countries' ongoing trade disagreements. The Trump administration has argued that China uses its industrial strategy to fund high-tech companies and give them an unfair advantage over competitors. The Journal's Akane Otani and Steven Russolillo report that trade negotiations, possible regulation and slowed down growth have caused Chinese technology stocks to fall this year.

The percentage of consumers who say most companies handle personal data responsibly, according to a PricewaterhouseCoopers LLC survey of 2,000 U.S. adults.

Facebook Inc., Alphabet Inc.'s Google, Microsoft Corp. and other technology companies have lobbied the Trump administration for a federal law to give companies wide leeway over how firms collect and share user data, according to the New York Times. Such a law would overrule the new California state law that gives people the ability to know what information companies collect about them, the Times reported. David Redl, head of a Commerce Department division that oversees privacy efforts, said in July the Trump administration's "commitment to prosperity will be our guide," the Times reported.

The Federal Energy Regulatory Commission until last week had declined to identify the utility it fined $2.7 million this year for a 2016 breach, WSJ's Ms. Smith reports. Roughly 30,000 records about PG&E's cyber assets were exposed to the web without password protection at a time when authorities have said Russian hackers were trying to breach U.S. energy firms.

Russian hackers targeted clergy. The decision, which could come as soon as next month, could drastically erode the power of church leaders in Moscow, the AP said.
