10:22
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

TOKYO A top Japanese government banker said China's industrial strategy is "very dangerous" for democracies because totalitarian nations can ignore their citizens' privacy and get an edge in new technologies.

"A country like China, a more totalitarian regime, will perform better than the democratic countries," said Tadashi Maeda, governor of the state-owned Japan Bank for International Cooperation, in an interview. "We have a lot of problems gathering big data because of the privacy issue. China doesn't have such an issue. So obviously they have some advantage."

The Trump administration, sometimes with backing from Japan and Europe, has taken on Beijing over its "Made in China 2025" plan, which seeks to boost Chinese industry in high-tech areas. Washington says the initiative is packed with subsidies for Chinese high-tech firms that would give them an unfair advantage in global competition. It has placed tariffs on $50 billion in Chinese goods, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/china-u-s-fire-off-new-tariffs-in-middle-of-trade-talks-1535018365".

China has said its plan is in line with World Trade Organization rules and "http://www.xinhuanet.com/english/2018-04/04/c_137088566.htm".

Mr. Maeda said he feared Made in China 2025 would allow Chinese companies to get an edge in artificial intelligence and other new technologies that rely on processing large amounts of data such as billions of online chats. Already the Chinese government is building one of the world's most sophisticated security systems based on big data, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/chinas-tech-giants-have-a-second-job-helping-the-government-see-everything-1512056284".

"I think it is very dangerous," Mr. Maeda said. Democracies including the U.S., European nations and Japan need to counter the Chinese effort by establishing common rules for data use, he said.

A representative of China's Ministry of Foreign Affairs, asked about Mr. Maeda's comments, said Tuesday: "China's development achievements in the field of artificial intelligence and big data are obvious to all, and China is willing to share its development fruits with all countries. Others should not wear 'colored glasses.'"

Under Mr. Maeda, who took the top job in June, JBIC is branching out from its traditional business of making loans for big infrastructure projects abroad. His priority in the bank's three-year plan, announced this summer, is to help Japanese companies get better access to overseas technologies for the "fourth industrial revolution," in which traditional manufacturers will use new kinds of data to run their businesses more efficiently.

He said JBIC was ready to take equity stakes in overseas companies doing business with Japan, focusing on data-driven areas such as "smart houses" that monitor the health condition of the elderly.

Pro Bankruptcy
J.C. Penney: Buying and Chasing

By Evan Mann, Gimme Credit 
735 words
27 August 2018
11:00
WSJ Pro Bankruptcy
RSTPROBK
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

J.C. Penney posted disappointing fiscal 2018 second-quarter sales and adjusted Ebitda and lowered its forecast for the year. With no CEO at the helm (former CEO Marvin Ellison abruptly departed in May), the department store retailer appears to have lost its way. Although "hiring a new CEO is a top priority," we expect the company to remain adrift until an experienced merchandiser can be brought in to right the ship and steer it to better execution along with sales and adjusted Ebitda growth. With this year's holiday season not far off, it is unlikely a new CEO can make his mark on the company before the second half of next year at the earliest. Moody's and S&P seem to agree, both lowering JCP's senior unsecured notes rating one notch to Caa2/CCC+.

About the Author

Evan Mann has 28 years of experience as a credit analyst with Gimme Credit High Yield, Delaware Investments, Banc America Securities, Dillon Read and Lord Abbett. He earned his B.A. from Washington University and his M.B.A. from Hofstra University.

Same-store sales for May and July were ahead of the company's fiscal 2018 guidance but offset by a soft June. This quarter, JCP changed its inventory management strategy from "buying to store capacity" to "buying and chasing" based on actual sell-through results. This necessitated taking markdowns to clear excessive inventory beyond seasonal product or fashion misses with further actions to right-size continuing into the second half as inventory liquidation will remain a drag on this year's operating performance.

Total revenue for the second quarter ended Aug. 4 fell 8% to $2.8 billion because of last year's store closings, lower credit card income, and a meager 0.3% increase in same-store sales. The adjusted Ebitda margin decreased 290 basis points from higher markdowns and pricing actions to clear slow-moving and excess inventory as well as selling, general and administrative deleveraging related to lower sales. Reflecting the declines in sales and margin, fiscal second-quarter adjusted Ebitda dropped 48% to $105 million, short of the $193 million consensus estimate. For the last 12 months, adjusted Ebitda of $789 million translated to leverage of 5.3x and rent adjusted leverage of 5.9x, deterioration from 4.4x and 5.0x, respectively, in fiscal 2017.

J.C. Penney ended the quarter with $2.2 billion of liquidity. For the first half, free cash flow (cash flow from operations less net capital spending) swung $245 million to a $235 million shortfall mostly because of lower earnings and increased working capital usage. Inventory was up 1% on a same-store basis in a quarter with flat same-store sales, supporting the company's argument that it needs to reduce inventory further. The retailer looks for third-quarter same-store sales to be slightly negative with gross margin pressure continuing from the decision to right-size inventory, partly offset by sales gains for higher-margin women's apparel, leveraging of private label and sourcing, and better pricing analytics. The consensus estimate calls for third-quarter Ebitda of $40 million, down from $108 million in the year-ago period. For fiscal 2018, J.C. Penney expects same-store sales to be flat (from flat to up 2%), margin declines related to inventory reductions and selling, general and administrative deleveraging, adjusted Ebitda of around $700 million, free cash flow flat to up slightly, and year-end liquidity of at least $2.2 billion.

Based on our updated model, we look for leverage to rise to 5.9x (6.3x rent adjusted) at year end. Since our last report on May 23, the 5.875% senior notes due 2023 have widened 140 basis points to 8.6%. With credit metrics expected to deteriorate over the near term and no replacement CEO, we maintain an underperform rating.

Markets
It Was Meant to Be the Better Bitcoin. It's Down Nearly 90%; With faster transactions and lower fees, cryptocurrency Bitcoin Cash was conceived as a way to make bitcoin a mainstream payment tool

By Alexander Osipovich 
666 words
25 August 2018
00:31
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The bear market in cryptocurrencies has "https://www.wsj.com/articles/cryptocurrency-market-plumbs-new-depths-in-2018-1534241274" who bought bitcoin at the "https://www.wsj.com/articles/for-bitcoin-a-year-like-no-other-1514721601" last year. But losses have been even more brutal for those who invested in once-promising "https://www.wsj.com/articles/bitcoin-cash-litecoin-ether-oh-my-whats-with-all-the-bitcoin-clones-1514037600".

Consider Bitcoin Cash, an "https://www.wsj.com/articles/bitcoins-civil-war-what-you-need-to-know-1501508664" that launched on August 1, 2017, which moves independently of bitcoin itself. While

"https://www.wsj.com/articles/is-bitcoin-back-cryptocurrency-crosses-8-000-for-first-time-in-two-months-1532433493" 67% from its record intraday high of $20,089 in December, Bitcoin Cash is down a crushing 88% from its peak of $4,355.62, according to CoinMarketCap.com. Friday evening, it traded at around $533.

Hundreds of other coins have also "https://www.wsj.com/articles/bitcoins-hype-vanishes-just-like-that-were-in-the-boring-phase-1523534400", but the underperformance of Bitcoin Cash is notable because it touches on a bigger theme: whether virtual currencies can really become a means of payment that would supplant traditional money.

Supporters of Bitcoin Cash conceived it as a way to fix problems that they believed were impeding bitcoin from becoming a mainstream payment tool, akin to PayPal or Visa.

Boosters including Roger Ver, a prominent crypto investor often called Bitcoin Jesus argued that the original bitcoin network had become too slow and that users were paying overly hefty fees for sending each other bitcoins. This faction contended that bitcoin's clunkiness was causing people to treat it as a store of value, like gold, and not as a practical way to buy Big Macs or cappuccinos.

There is still a lively debate over whether bitcoin is any good as a medium of exchange. Many bitcoin holders view it as a hedge against the collapse of government-backed currencies, but don't often use it to pay for things.

After waging an unsuccessful campaign to change bitcoin from within, the rebels broke off and created Bitcoin Cash, in a so-called fork that bitterly split the virtual-currency community. The underlying technology of the new cryptocurrency was almost identical to that of bitcoin, except for tweaks aimed at speeding up transactions and lowering fees.

One year later, some research suggests the breakaway coin hasn't lived up to its promises. A study of activity by major cryptocurrency payment processors which help businesses accept payments in crypto found that just $3.8 million worth of payments were made in Bitcoin Cash in May. That is down from $10.5 million in March, according to Chainalysis, an analytics firm that carried out the study.

Backers of Bitcoin Cash note that payments in bitcoin are also down if not quite as sharply and that their preferred cryptocurrency is cheaper to use.

"Despite the decline in price, all the exciting innovation is happening in [Bitcoin Cash]," Mike Komaransky, an adviser to the Bitcoin Cash Association, a group that promotes the use of the cryptocurrency, said in an email. Mr. Ver, another adviser to the group, didn't respond to a request for comment.

There are many reasons why bitcoin and other cryptocurrencies haven't taken off as a form of payment, including their volatility and uncertain regulatory status, which has kept many businesses from embracing them.

Bitcoin Cash is still the fourth-most valuable cryptocurrency by market capitalization, but its value relative to bitcoin has sagged, suggesting that investors who once bet on it may have shifted back to the original. One unit of Bitcoin Cash is now worth about 8% of one bitcoin, down from 18% in early May, CoinMarketCap.com data shows.

For the average investor, the technical distinctions between the two may not be that important. Instead, the market seems to be favoring the one with the stronger brand, according to Martin Garcia, a managing director at Genesis Global Trading Inc., a digital-currency trading firm.

"Bitcoin is expressing itself as the more dominant token," he said.

Upgrade mostly arranges online loans for consumers to pay down credit card debt

Renaud Laplanche, the fintech pioneer who built Lending Club into America s biggest provider of unsecured consumer loans, has achieved a $500m valuation for his follow-up venture, Upgrade.

Like Lending Club, Upgrade is in the business of arranging loans over the web, bypassing brick-and-mortar banks by connecting needy borrowers with yield-hungry investors. In almost a year and a half of operation the San Francisco-based company has supplied more than $1bn of loans to consumers, mostly to pay down revolving debt held on credit cards.

On Thursday, Upgrade announced that it had secured $62m in a third round of equity funding from existing investors. The cash values the company at about $500m on a pre-money basis, according to a person familiar with the deal.

Mr Laplanche said the new capital would support continued investments in products, analytics and compliance in offices in San Francisco, Phoenix, Montreal and Chicago.

In May 2016 the French-born executive was forced out of Lending Club, the company he founded, after governance glitches that prompted many of the company s big institutional customers to put their buying programmes on hold. Lending Club has since recovered some of its poise but still expects to lose more than $100m this year, as still-elevated costs offset rises in revenues.

At Upgrade, Mr Laplanche is counting on a fixed-rate loan being more appealing to consumers than the variable cost of credit-card debt, at a time when rising base rates are putting a squeeze on habitual borrowers. Total balances on credit cards held by US consumers stood at $829bn at the end of June, according to New York Federal Reserve data, not far from the pre-crisis peak of $870bn.

Upgrade s latest funding round was led by CreditEase FinTech Investment Fund, a Chinese fund which has backed several lending platforms including Funding Circle, Lenda and Figure, the new venture from Mike Cagney, the former chief executive of SoFi. This is the first time CreditEase has led a round in the US.

 We believe Upgrade will grow into a mainstream consumer credit brand that is dedicated to helping consumers better understand their credit and encourages responsible credit behaviour and we are excited to be part of the journey,  said Anju Patwardhan, managing director at CreditEase, who is joining the Upgrade board.

Business
India's Flipkart buys speech recognition software start-up

SIMON MUNDY 
508 words
22 August 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Main
16
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Technology

Indian ecommerce group Flipkart has sought to steal a march over rival Amazon by acquiring a start-up that turns local languages from speech to text, as industry attention turns to lowerincome people not yet shopping online.

Flipkart said yesterday that it had agreed the takeover of Bangalore-based Liv.ai, which has developed artificial intelligence-driven software enabling smartphone users to dictate text in 10 indigenous tongues, from Hindi to Malayalam.

Flipkart, which was itself bought by Walmart this year in a $16bn deal, did not reveal the value of the acquisition, although local reports put it at about $40m.

The purchase reflects a growing consensus among ecommerce executives that strong growth will require a broadening of the market beyond Englishspeaking, relatively prosperous Indians who already do some shopping online.

Kalyan Krishnamurthy, Flipkart chief executive, said that "the next wave of growth of internet users" was coming from beyond India's biggest cities. He added that most of these new users want to access online services in vernacular languages   which most would never have written using a keyboard.

"Given the complexities in typing on vernacular keyboards, voice will become a preferred interface for new shoppers," he said.

A joint report this month by Bain & Company, Google and Omidyar Network found that only 40 per cent of India's 390m internet users made any transactions online, with this group skewed overwhelmingly towards higher-income people.

Analysts say this is partly a reflection of the fact that Indian ecommerce offerings have failed to keep up with a shift in the country's online demographics beyond the fluent English speakers who were first to get online.

KPMG estimates that in 2011, there were 68m Indian web users who were comfortable using English, against 42m who preferred using a local language. By 2016, the latter group outnumbered English speakers 234m to 175m. And in 2021, KPMG forecast, there would be 536m local language speakers online, against 199m English speakers.

Like Amazon India, Flipkart's interface is currently available only in English. It did not reveal when it would announce services in Indian languages.

Other Indian companies, however, have already rolled out local language offerings. Quikr, an online classified advertisement service, is available in seven Indian languages. Dailyhunt's mobile app offers 100,000 articles a day in 14 languages licensed from domestic news outlets, and claims to have had more than 150m app downloads.

The Bain-led report estimated that a push for new internet users could unlock $50bn of sales for the Indian ecommerce industry   more than double the sector's total revenue last year of about $20bn.

Part of this potential growth, it added, would come from winning back customers who abandoned ecommerce after their first transaction. While about 160m Indians were currently shopping online, the report estimated, more than 50m had tried and then abandoned ecommerce, typically because they were not satisfied with the online experience or perceived a wider range of options in offline retail.

Is Kroger s Visa ban a sign of things to come?

Nicholas Megaw 
1,161 words
21 August 2018
10:05
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Monzo to join unicorn club, Amazon insurance ambitions, Netflixification of trading

Visa is fond of boasting  wherever you are, there s a Visa card to suit you . But customers of US supermarket chain Kroger last week discovered there are limits to that claim.

Kroger s Foods Co. subsidiary has made good on its threat to stop accepting Visa credit cards, the culmination of a dispute over the interchange chargesretailers pay to card networks for each transaction they process. The grocer has threatened toexpand the banto its other stores if they can t reach an agreement soon.

For most companies, a fight with America s largest supermarket chain would be a worry. The banknocked sharesin Visa and its peers when it was announced late last month, amid worries that other frustrated companies could follow Kroger s lead in trying to fight high processing charges. But the drops are barely blips in the context of the groups  recent growth.

Shares in Visa have risen almost 40 per cent over the past twelvemonths, while Mastercard has jumped more than 50 per cent. Even as challengers like Alipay, Apple Pay and PayPal encroach on their markets, investors have been drawn to the incumbent payment giants who they still believe will benefit from astructural shiftaway from cash and toward digital payments.

But are investors overly optimistic about the prospects for legacy payments giants? As the Kroger conflict shows, Visa and Mastercard s customers   both the merchants that take payments and the banks that issue cards   are agitating for lower costs.

Regulators, meanwhile, are creating the conditions for challengers to flourish. The EU s Second Payment Services Directive   PSD2   makes it easier for third parties to initiate payments directly from customers  bank accounts, bypassing the card companies  networks.

Mark Nelsen, Visa's senior vice-president and head of risk and authentication products, said change is nothing new.  The way you pay has been changing fairly significantly already,  he told the FT.  In my mind there s been a constant drum of innovation happening and PSD2 is designed to accelerate that. 

But while companies such asSweden s Trustly  which specialises in account to account payments that bypass the card networks   have built a foothold in a few markets, Mr Nelsen said established groups  scalewould make them hard to beat. Visa itself works with 46mmerchants globally.

 That connectivity is hard to replicate,  Mr Nelsensaid, adding that Visa wasn t taking the competition  lightly  and was doing  everything we can  to make sure Visa remains a top payments choice.

Recent experience in China highlights the potential for customer habits to quickly change, with state-backed bank card network China UnionPay losing ground in its home market to mobile-driven services Alipay and WeChat Pay.

Zilvinas Bareisis, an analyst specialising in consumer payments at Celent, the consultancy, said the new Chinese groups offered  a completely unique and really interesting consumer value proposition   they're not payments apps, it s more like a lifestyle app with payments capability ingrained in that   that s when you start shifting behaviours  & that s one thing we have been missing in the western world. 

Still, he was sceptical that Visa and Mastercard s dominance would end any time soon, given the groups  investments in improving services and customers  ingrained behaviours.

Both companies have stepped up their investments in new tech and partnered with other fintech groups, with Mastercard s 700mpurchase of Vocalinkmarking one of the most visible responses to the potential changes in the market. Vocalink powers systems such as the UK s Faster Payments scheme for consumer bank transfers, giving Mastercard a stake even in transactions that bypass its card network.

If all else fails, Mr Bareisis added, the card networks  healthy profit margins give them the option to drop prices to squeeze out competitors if they come under sustained pressure   the two companies both reported operating margins of over 50 per cent in the three months to June 30.

That means that, in the long term, the complaints of companies like Kroger could fade   but for now Visa is still holding most of the cards.

Further fintech fascination

Unicorn Alert

UK digital bank Monzo is set to join the unicorn clubwhen it closes a $150m fundraising that values the firm at $1.5bn. The new valuation   which comfortably meets the unicorn definitionof privately held start-ups worth at least $1bn   is more than four times the $356m Monzo was valued at when it last sold stock in November, according to an exclusive story in the FT. Nice work if you can get it.

Amazon's Ambitions

With its treasure trove of consumer spending and lifestyledata, and a captive audience of hundreds of millions, Amazon's forays into fintech are alwaysworth watching. The tech giant'slatest wheeze, according to Reuters, is aUK insuranceprice comparison website. Amazon has already spoken to several insurers about the project, but talks are at an early stage.

Netflixification of Trading

Yes it's a real thing. Switzerland's UBS is developing a new algorithm that suggests future trades based on past ones, using similar underlying concepts to the ones that Netflix uses to encourage House of Cards watchers to peruse Designated Survivor. A good sales person calls his or her client and goes over what they think will be interesting for the day,  saidGiuseppe Nuti, who heads up data science in UBS s FX, rates and credit Strategic Development Lab. We are trying to automate that. It means the job of a sales person, which in many ways has remained immune to the technology revolution, will probably change. 

Fintechviews in theFTbanking podcast

Bits and bobs

German dream lives onGermany's retail banking market is notoriously difficult to crack, but investors have given open banking start-up Deposit Solutions another $100m to do just that. The Hamburg-based bank has just completed the second-largest financing round of any German fintech, and is now valued at more than $500m.

AI's jobs threatsFew things make finance'sArtificial Intelligence experts more uncomfortable than asking them about the potential for their creations to replace human bank tellers, box tickers, traders and assorted other personnel. Robots are about enhancing jobs, not ending them, the PR spin goes.The Bank of England's chief economist Andy Haldane blew a great big hole in that slogan onMonday,telling Radio 4 that AI could threaten more human jobs than the Industrial Revolution.

Ebury heads southBritish international payments firm Ebury   which has built a $1bn valuation in nine years   issetting up shopin Sydney. Co-founder Juan Lobato told the Australian Financial Review that Sydney's status as a  gateway country for the Asia Pacific  made it a key market in Ebury's expansion strategy.

Battle with Tencent for market share in China has erased profitability

A blockbuster listing of Ant Financial, the fast-growing electronic payment affiliate of China s leading tech group Alibaba, has again been put on the back burner as it continues to burn cash and come under pressure from Beijing s crackdown on non-traditional financial institutions.

Ant was valued at $150bn in its last private fundraising in June, a round that bankers said paved the way for an initial public offering as early as this year.

Since then, however, the battle for China market share with the payments arm of rival Tencent has erased profitability and yet again bumped IPO plans further into the future. Two people familiar with the process said a listing was unlikely before the end of 2019, with one saying it was  years  away.

Ant Financial reported a loss in the first three months of the year, according to Alibaba s last earnings announcement. Alibaba will report second-quarter results on Thursday.

This year has already featured a string of listings by unprofitable but fast-growing Chinese start-ups and more are hoping to follow suit next year   including ride-hailing app Didi Chuxing, the second biggest private tech group after Uber, which it chased out of China in 2016.

Ant is also under pressure from Beijing s efforts to tighten control of non-traditional financial groups, whose rapid rise in recent years has encroached on the turf of state-owned banks.

 There are so many challenges to the business model of Ant and other non-banks,  said one analyst who requested anonymity.  I am not surprised that they once more are postponing their IPO. 

The decision to delay Ant s listing was made several months ago, according to two people with direct knowledge of the matter. Since then, the business environment has continued to deteriorate while liquidity in China has tightened. Bankers also said that Ant, backed by deep-pocketed Alibaba, did not need to raise cash.

Ant said it had never had a timetable for listing, and played down the regulatory threats to its business.  A regulated and orderly market is good for players like us and we view compliance as a prerequisite to doing business,  said a spokesperson.  We have proactively engaged the regulators and they have been supportive of our efforts to promote financial inclusion. 

Despite its instance there was no timeframe for an IPO, investors in Ant s last funding round said they were told a public offering would follow later this year or next.

The crackdown on financial innovation by Chinese regulators has had a big impact on listed internet consumer lenders, whose share prices dropped more than 50 per cent from November to mid-July, according to a report from UBS.

 In the recent credit downgrade, delinquency rates have almost doubled,  UBS added. In the first half of 2018, about 600 peer-to-peer lenders closed, it calculated.

Ant is far from the only non-bank affected by the changing regulatory environment. Non-banks that began life as P2P lenders, such as Lufax and Dianrong, are delaying their plans to list as they scramble to diversify away from that line of business

 It isn t just Ant Financial, it is everyone,  says the head of one Chinese private equity firm.  Beijing is tightening financial regulation because they think the innovation has gone too far. It will take at least all of next year for these companies to recover. 

Ant and Tencent have both argued they differ from most non-banks because their use of big data gives them credit scoring superior to that of the banks. They also argued banks remained unwilling to do consumer lending beyond mortgages.

Ant s listing plans were originally put on ice last year, at which time it was valued at $60bn. Bankers and lawyers blamed the need to secure regulatory approval and to focus on building the business.

In February this year Alibaba restructured its relationship with Ant, jettisoning a profit-sharing arrangement in favour of a direct equity stake of 33 per cent. It followed that up with the $10bn funding round in June, which valued the group at $150bn.

Lu returns home to tap China's technological talent

772 words
18 August 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Main

English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Corporate person in the news Lu Qi China head, Y Combinator

Sam Altman, the founder of Y Combinator, the leading Silicon Valley start-up incubator, first met Lu Qi in 2005 when the Chinese-born technologist was at Yahoo   and Mr Altman was still a teenager.

This week, Mr Altman announced that he had hired Mr Lu to establish a beachhead in China for Y Combinator and help identify start-ups on the mainland to nurture and invest in.

"He will be perfectly aligned with founders of young companies," Mr Altman said, pointing to Mr Lu's approach to negotiation and dealing with young companies.

"He told me that his grandfather always said, 'never wipe the table clean. Always leave something for the other side.' Qi understands you need to put founders first. And he will build a brilliant company in China."

Mr Lu has never founded his own company, but he has had a brilliant career spearheading breakthroughs in artificial intelligence on both sides of the Pacific. After leaving Yahoo, he spent three years at Microsoft in Seattle   where Satya Nadella, chief executive, used to work for him.

His rise was also seen at home as evidence that a Chinese could make it in the best US companies.

When Mr Lu left to become chief operating officer at Baidu, one of China's leading internet companies, and moved to Beijing in 2017, many of his compatriots took it as confirmation of the ascendancy of their tech industry.

"China has a structural advantage in artificial intelligence development and commercialisation. Only China has the US combination of talent, risk capital, technology and the market," Mr Lu said last year.

When he left Baidu this year, it triggered a debate on Chinese social media. His departure seemed to imply shortcomings at one of China's homegrown giants and questions were raised about which other mainland company he might join instead, with Tencent a seemingly strong possibility.

Instead, he has chosen to take advantage of his links between both worlds to tap into the next generation of Chinese entrepreneurialism.

Mr Lu, 56, is one of the first of a generation of technologists who combine the best of both worlds. He dreamt of becoming a philosopher, then after touring a shipbuilding factory as a student, he decided that working there seemed a "most glamorous job. But only 3 per cent of students at the time made it to that type of school. I didn't weigh enough to meet their minimum requirement."

When he asked his parents if he should study maths or computer science instead, they advised him that the former would lead to a teaching career, but that technology might get him a job at a radio factory. The latter was more appealing.

He ended up studying computer science at the elite Fudan University in Shanghai, before going on to earn a PhD at Carnegie Mellon University in Pittsburgh.

Mr Lu joined Yahoo, where he became head of search before joining Microsoft in 2013. His responsibilities there included running Bing, the company's search engine, and overseeing Office.

One of his projects involved working with his engineering team on a "backward-brain bicycle", which worked in the opposite to conventional bikes   if you wanted to turn left, you would have to turn right.

The team believed that the muscle memory required to learn how to adapt to the new bike might generate insights into how algorithms behave. Unfortunately though, Mr Lu broke a hip while on the bike and left Microsoft in part because the injury required several operations to repair.

Neither Mr Lu's arrival at Baidu nor his departure came as a surprise. Robin Li, chief executive of Baidu, is steeped in AI and has a long history of finding the best Chinese talent abroad and luring that talent back home.

But, although Mr Li gave Mr Lu responsibility for the company's initiatives in artificial intelligence and autonomous driving, he never provided the resources he needed to fulfil those responsibilities, according to people with knowledge of the matter both inside Baidu and outside.

At Y Combinator, Mr Lu will continue to bounce between both sides of the Pacific, spending half of his time in China and half in the US.

"I view myself as a piece of software," he said. "Tomorrow's version has to be better than today's. I don't want tomorrow to be the same as today." Henny Sender

The Woman Who Has a Plan for Wall Street to Help Cure Blindness; Karen Petrou spent years trying to hide her blindness. Now she has come up with a plan to get private investors to bankroll a cure.

By Christina Rexrode 
1,005 words
18 August 2018
15:00
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Karen Petrou, an influential adviser to bankers and regulators, has made a career of deciphering complicated financial regulations. Now she's trying to decode another type of puzzle.

The conundrum: Matching medical researchers who need money with investors who have it. A bill outlining her strategy, which would include a government guarantee, was introduced in the House of Representatives last month.

Her goal, to cure blindness, is lofty, and her crusade is personal. Though Ms. Petrou, who runs policy-analysis firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc., consumes dense regulatory tomes faster than perhaps anyone in Washington, it has been years since she could see.

Ms. Petrou, 65, is unsentimental about finance, where the sharp contours of profit trump the squishy metrics of doing good. But she also believes that pension funds, insurers and others will invest in her so-called Eye Bonds if they can make money doing it. The plan, which she designed with her husband and business partner, Basil, envisions a five-year pilot that would finance up to $1 billion in loans for organizations doing early-stage research on blindness.

"In the scheme of Wall Street, it's lunch money anyway," Ms. Petrou said. "And maybe it appeals to what's left of their heartstrings."

Under their proposal, labs and others would get a stamp of approval for projects deemed promising by the National Eye Institute, a designation that could help them line up loans.

The lenders would sell the loans to an Eye Bond Trust administered by financial institutions that would package and sell the loans to investors she envisions 10-year bonds. Investors would be repaid both the principal and interest when the bonds mature, and if a lab's project turned into a commercial success, the investors could get equity. A government guarantee would partially protect investors if the labs couldn't repay.

Ms. Petrou grew up in a liberal-leaning home in Westchester County, to an artist mother and book-publisher father. As a child, she once challenged the rabbi teaching her Sunday school class to "an intellectual disputation," said her mother, Blanche Dolmatch. As a teenager, she donned a Colonial dress and mobcap to give tours of Philipsburg Manor in Sleepy Hollow, N.Y.

"She was always good at making speeches and instructing on things, and blowing off intellectual steam," Mrs. Dolmatch said.

In high school, Ms. Petrou was diagnosed with retinitis pigmentosa, a disorder that causes gradual vision loss. "We just kind of staggered out of the place," Mrs. Dolmatch recalled.

Within a few years, acquaintances complained that Ms. Petrou didn't say hi when she passed them. Her peripheral vision was failing.

At the same time, she was working on a doctorate in political science at the University of California, Berkeley. But she took a job in banking, figuring it would offer more opportunities for women than academia.

For a while, it did, and Ms. Petrou rose through the ranks in political analysis and public affairs at San Francisco-based BankAmerica Corp. When she was 32, the CEO told her he wasn't comfortable promoting a young woman to senior vice president, according to Ms. Petrou. She left soon after.

Since then, she has built out her firm and gained a reputation for knowing the minutiae of esoteric regulations and their big-picture repercussions. Both banks and regulators hire her to provide a nonpartisan analysis of the potential effects of new policies. "I'm not coming up with loopholes," Ms. Petrou said.

Rodgin Cohen, senior chairman of Sullivan & Cromwell LLP, met Ms. Petrou in the early 1990s when they worked on the merger of Mellon Bank Corp. and Dreyfus Corp., a controversial proposal at the time because it combined a bank and a mutual-fund business.

"She gave a very broad-based view of everything that was going on how the regulators would work, how Congress would react, and how the media would react," Mr. Cohen said. "It was typical of her approach and forthrightness."

Along the way, Ms. Petrou's sight continued to deteriorate. She lost her reading vision six months after starting her firm. Wary of being pitied or stereotyped, she would tell people she couldn't see a menu because she had lost a contact lens.

Today, she is almost completely blind, though many of her clients don't know until her German shepherd guide dog, Zuni, trundles into their boardroom. She ingests volumes of ponderous regulatory documents by listening to screen-reading software at 400 words a minute, pausing to type her analysis for clients.

"If you are going to challenge Karen on a fact, you better be right," said Pete Mills, who worked for Ms. Petrou and is now head of residential public policy for the Mortgage Bankers Association. "You can't get anything by her."

In recent years, Ms. Petrou has "https://economicequality.blog/" about the widening gap between rich and poor, including her belief that postcrisis government policy has exacerbated the problem. In a lecture to the Federal Reserve Bank of New York this year, she scolded central bankers for years of ultralow interest rates. Her take: The policy has boosted the stock market, which benefits investors, but harmed families trying to set aside savings.

With Eye Bonds, the Petrous aren't looking to reinvent themselves but to be heard on a different issue. They believe the obstacle to curing blindness isn't a lack of science but a lack of funding. Though it is early days for Eye Bonds, they hope their structure or something similar could be applied to other diseases.

"People always say, 'Why is this for blindness?'" Ms. Petrou said. "And I say, 'Well, I'm blind, so let's start with that.'"

Write to Christina Rexrode at "mailto:christina.rexrode@wsj.com"

DE Shaw taps academic to set up new machine learning  group

Robin Wigglesworth 
347 words
16 August 2018
18:17

FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

DE Shaw, the $50bn quantitative hedge fund founded above a Marxist bookshop, has hired a prominent academic in the field of artificial intelligence to set up a new research group dedicated to machine learning   one of the hottest subjects in finance.

The New York hedge fund group founded by David Shaw in 1988 is one of the best-known and respected members of the rapidly swelling  quant  industry, which uses computers and complex algorithms to trawl through financial markets in search for profitable patterns to mine.

DE Shaw has long used artificial intelligence techniques such as  machine learning  to trade, but the new independent unit led by Pedro Domingos   a professor of computer science and engineering at the University of Washington   will have a wider-ranging research brief.

 This new, independent venture reflects the importance we attach to gaining fresh perspectives and insights as technology evolves,  Eric Wepsic, a DE Shaw managing director who oversees the hedge fund s quantitative trading and investments, said in a statement.  It also provides us with an opportunity to bring in some of the brightest lights in the field and allow them to set their own research agenda. 

Machine learning, which in simple terms consists of algorithms that can learn and develop over time as they are fed new data, is a popular tool to find patterns in enormous digital data sets, often dubbed  Big Data .

These can range from microsecond-level data of stock market trading to the blizzard of daily credit card purchases, satellite images of industrial sites or social media chatter. The world s annual data generation is now roughly doubling annually, and the overall size will reach 44 zettabytes (or trillions of gigabytes) by 2020, according to a study by International Data Corporation.

While only 17 per cent of investors surveyed by Greenwich Associates are currently using some form of AI, 56 per cent plan to integrate it into their investment process, and 40 per cent expect to ramp up spending in the area.

Pro Cyber
Cyber Daily: Fighting for Insurance Payouts; Security Spending on the Rise

1,747 words
16 August 2018
13:29
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Insured Against Cyber Theft? Maybe Not

By Kim S. Nash

First there was cyber crime, then cyber insurance. Now come court battles over which crimes and hacker tactics insurance will cover.

Cyber insurance can be purchased as an add-on to risk and other business coverage or as a standalone policy. Security officers and finance and legal executives who negotiate with insurance companies must understand that cyber-specific coverage is an immature sector compared to other forms of insurance, says Adam Ziffer, a principal in McKool Smith's insurance recovery litigation practice.

Cyber criminals can change methods quickly, leaving insurers and customers uncertain as to what situations are covered, he says. Much of this is getting sorted out in court, he says. "There's a disconnect between what policies cover and how frauds occur."

A 2013 suit filed by seafood company Aqua Star against Travelers Casualty and Surety Company of America is instructive. A hacker used email to trick an Aqua Star employee to wire hundreds of thousands of dollars to foreign bank accounts. Travelers denied the claim saying damages from "authorized" transactions were not covered. That is, the employee had the appropriate credentials to wire funds; it was no matter that the transaction happened because a criminal faked email messages. In 2016, a U.S. district court in Washington ruled for the insurer.

A similar case last year in New York involving fraudulent email sent to an employee at clinical trials firm Medidata Solutions Inc. went the opposite way, with Federal Insurance Co. covering damages.

Disputes are likely to increase because there is no settled law yet, Mr. Ziffer said in an interview with WSJ Pro Cybersecurity. After relevant verdicts, insurers sometimes rewrite policy language to reflect a judge's opinion and tighten holes, he said. Here are edited excerpts of the conversation.

Q: Skeptical people will say that insurance companies deny claims often because they don't want to pay.

A: That may be true but the court is supposed to take an ambiguous policy and identify the reasonable expectations of the policy holder. You hope that common sense plays a role.

Q: What are the standard clauses to watch out for?

A: This industry is somewhat immature. What you're seeing in these disputes is insurance companies have not been able to anticipate all the types of fraud they can expect. Same with policyholders. There's a disconnect between how these frauds occur and what the policies cover.

Q: What can companies do?

A: You can try to have some type of choice among a couple policies and evaluate which is providing you with the broadest coverage. A lot of time, policyholders are focused more on premium than the language.

Q: Cyber insurance is relatively young. Is every case now a test case?

A: That's right. What happens is, when insurance companies get a bad decision, they have the ability to change language in the policy that they sell the next year. If you presume their goal is to restrict coverage, they will try to win cases and when they don't, they can change the language in policies. We've seen some policies become more restrictive in language in response to court decisions.

Q: Is cyber insurance very lucrative for insurance companies, compared to other forms of business insurance?

A: I don't know. I do know that they're selling it like crazy. Everybody's concerned about cyber risk. This is one of the only tools to manage it. The insurance industry typically makes money on the insurance it sells. There's a lot of it being sold. These losses [from cyber crime] are very, very difficult to recover from the perpetrators. So insurance becomes the only way to see relief.

*******************************

Firms Battle Privacy Concerns, Compliance With Double-Digit Increase In Security Budgets

By Adam Janofsky

Privacy concerns, skills shortages and new regulations are pushing companies to spend more on cybersecurity tools and services, according to new research from Gartner Inc.

Global spending on cybersecurity will reach $114 billion in 2018, a 12.4% increase from last year, according to the report released Wednesday by the research firm. Privacy concerns will drive at least 10% of that demand through 2019, the report found.

Companies are expected to increase their spending on privacy tools such as identity and access management and data loss prevention to comply with new laws like the European Union's General Data Protection Regulation, which went into effect in May, according to Gartner research director Siddharth Deshpande.

Data Worries Push Spending Higher: The cost of complying with these laws would be a fraction of the penalties for violations. For example, companies that fail to comply with GDPR could face fines up to  20 million ($22.7 million) or 4% of their annual revenue. About 60% of companies said they would spend at least $1 million on GDPR compliance, and 12% said they would spend more than $10 million, according to a survey conducted by PricewaterhouseCoopers LLP in the fall of 2017.

Spending on data security and privacy tools may also increase as companies including Facebook Inc. and Alphabet Inc. come under increased scrutiny by lawmakers, the U.S. Securities and Exchange Commission and other regulators for how they handle customer data and deal with cyber threats.

In a November earnings call, Facebook chief executive Mark Zuckerberg said his company would double security staff by the end of 2018 and develop artificial intelligence technologies to better detect cyberattacks. Law enforcement officials and cybersecurity specialists have said the platform was used to spread misinformation during the 2016 presidential election.

"I am dead serious about this & I've directed our teams to invest so much in security on top of the other investments we're making that it will significantly impact our profitability going forward," Mr. Zuckerberg said on the earnings call, The Wall Street Journal reported at the time.

Cybersecurity executives at M&T Bank Corp., Fair Isaac Corp. and U.S. Bancorp have said boards and senior management have been willing to pad cybersecurity budgets with the hope that it will help them avoid a major incident like the one Equifax Inc. revealed in September 2017, in which sensitive data about 147.9 million consumers was compromised.

 Something has gone horribly wrong': "The very first time I was introduced to our CEO, he asked me one question," said Jason Witty, executive vice president and chief information security officer at U.S. Bank, a unit of U.S. Bancorp, at a June cybersecurity summit in New York. "He looked me dead in the eye and said,  Are we spending enough on information security?'"

Mr. Witty responded that the bank was spending at an acceptable level, and added that CEOs, CFOs and CIOs should be asking this question regularly to make sure there is an adequate security budget.

"If you're going to the board and asking for funding, something has gone horribly wrong," he said.

White House Scraps Cyberattack Planning Policy

President Trump has reversed an Obama-era memorandum dictating how and when the U.S. government can deploy cyberweapons against its adversaries, the Journal's Dustin Volz reports.

Loosening restrictions. "The change was described as an  offensive step forward' by an administration official briefed on the decision, one intended to help support military operations, deter foreign election influence and thwart intellectual property theft by meeting such threats with more forceful responses." Mr. Volz reports.

What happens next? Former Obama officials said the framework was designed to help the government consider all possible outcomes before launching a cyberattack. "I am sympathetic to trying to make our cyber capabilities more nimble in their use," said Joshua Geltzer, who was senior director of counterterrorism at the National Security Council until March of last year. "On the other hand, there were some very real and hard legal questions associated with cyber about what operations the government would take that still have not been resolved."

Some say the profile photo, email address and phone number associated with their accounts also had been changed. The company now says it is upgrading its authentication settings, Mashable said.

Supply chain security warning. Chinese government-sponsored hacking activity is targeting companies and state agencies in Malaysia, the cybersecurity company FireEye Inc. told Reuters. FireEye has found evidence of new espionage activities aimed at uncovering intelligence about whether Malaysia's new prime minister will renegotiate or cancel the country's investment in Chinese infrastructure projects, according to Reuters. "As Chinese investments continue to be scrutinized, that is going to be a motivator for groups...to gain more intelligence and information on the future of these projects," Sandra Joyce, FireEye's head of global intelligence operations, told Reuters.

Mortgage Probe Gathers Steam

By Cezary Podkul 
1,452 words
16 August 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Owners of an apartment complex near Pittsburgh, who wanted to take out a mortgage on the buildings, allegedly made vacant units look occupied by turning on radios, placing shoes and mats outside doors and in one instance having a woman tell inspectors her boyfriend was asleep inside.

The owners obtained a $45.8 million loan, which was wrapped into mortgage securities and sold to investors.

Practices such as these -- which were alleged in a federal search-warrant application -- have sparked one of the largest mortgage-fraud investigations since the financial crisis. It focuses on whether income from commercial properties was falsified, a move that would enable owners to get larger mortgages and take out cash or expand their businesses faster.

Still in its early stages, the investigation has so far yielded a fraud-conspiracy indictment against four real-estate executives in upstate New York. Loans that some or all of them were involved with totaled about $170 million, the indictment alleges.

Investigators have sought mortgage data on dozens of other apartment buildings, according to documents reviewed by The Wall Street Journal and interviews with people familiar with the probe. Investigators have looked at student housing and self-storage facilities in addition to apartment complexes.

The Federal Bureau of Investigation, the U.S. attorney in the Western District of New York in Buffalo and the Federal Housing Finance Agency's Inspector General are all working on the probe.

Lax and fraudulent lending on single-family homes played a role in the mid-2000s housing bubble. The 2010 Dodd-Frank financial overhaul required home borrowers to document their income, and home lenders to verify it.

The rule doesn't apply to multifamily housing. Lenders in that market generally don't examine every lease to check income. Neither do Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac, the government-sponsored firms that buy and securitize loans. Credit-rating firms generally don't review loans for fraud.

"All the systems will work fine as long as people are being honest," said Sam Berns, senior vice president at NorthMarq Capital LLC, a firm licensed to sell multifamily mortgages to Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac. If borrowers or their mortgage brokers choose, they can easily submit and certify false numbers, he said. "It's a fault and a failure within the system."

One owner of properties investigators reviewed is Robert C. Morgan, the founder of a suburban Rochester, N.Y.-based apartment development company. After a 1991 shooting at his family's seafood store left him wheelchair-bound, he turned his full attention to real estate and built a business of more than 140 properties across 14 states, according to its website.

It was at a Morgan property near Pittsburgh called Rochester Village that a May 22 indictment in federal court in Buffalo alleged the faking of occupancy via radios playing in vacant units.

In addition, an executive anticipating an inspection of one building asked a property manager to have employees park their cars in its garage, according to the search-warrant application.

Deutsche Bank AG had just backed out of a deal to finance the property, suspecting occupancy was misrepresented, according to the document. The owner then obtained a loan via Berkadia Commercial Mortgage LLC, which sold the loan to Freddie Mac.

Deutsche Bank and Berkadia declined to comment.

In another instance, a mortgage broker allegedly created a fake $1.4 million loan on a Morgan complex near Rochester, N.Y., called Avon Commons, the indictment alleged, after which Mr. Morgan's firm refinanced the fake loan with a real one for $6.3 million. The loan was from Arbor Realty Trust, which sold it to Freddie Mac, the indictment said.

Arbor didn't comment on the investigation but said it is "committed to strengthening and improving our policies."

The indictment leveled fraud-conspiracy charges against Todd and Kevin Morgan, a son and a nephew, respectively, of Robert C. Morgan. It also charged their mortgage brokers, Frank Giacobbe and Patrick Ogiony of Aurora Capital Advisors LLC, a Buffalo firm the indictment said Mr. Giacobbe owned.

All pleaded not guilty. Mr. Giacobbe had no comment. Lawyers for Mr. Ogiony and Kevin Morgan didn't respond to requests for comment. Todd Morgan's lawyer said his client maintains he is innocent.

The indictment didn't charge Robert Morgan or his firm, until June called Morgan Management LLC. It has changed its name to Grand Atlas Property Management, and Mr. Morgan has no ownership or employment at the firm, according to a lawyer for Grand Atlas. The lawyer also said Kevin and Todd Morgan are no longer employees.

Robert Morgan and Grand Atlas declined to comment on the investigation.

About $1.2 billion of Morgan loans currently back Freddie Mac-issued mortgage securities and $350 million back Fannie Mae securities, according to a Journal analysis of Thomson Reuters loan data.

Fannie Mae, in a training course on its website, says, "Multifamily mortgage fraud continues to be a problem nationwide." The firm said that statement "does not mean to suggest that these issues routinely appear in Fannie Mae loans" or with its lenders.

Freddie Mac said it doesn't rely just on information submitted by borrowers but also uses third-party data and analytics. It said all Morgan loans packaged into its securities remain current.

Borrowers can stay current even on loans that are large relative to a property's earning power if they need to pay only the interest for several years. Mortgages with full or partial interest-only repayment periods made up about 75% of multifamily loans bought by Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac last year, according to data from Trepp LLC, a commercial-mortgage tracker.

FBI agents have reviewed deals involving several upstate New York developers with ties to Robert Morgan and to Mr. Giacobbe. One of them, according to documents reviewed by the Journal, is Prime Group Holdings, a Saratoga Springs-based owner of self-storage facilities founded by Robert J. Moser. Prime has worked with Mr. Giacobbe. Mr. Moser said that he and Robert Morgan "enjoyed a very successful business relationship for many years."

Until last year, a Moser biography that was on the websites of both Mr. Moser's and Mr. Morgan's companies said the two men had together acquired a $4 billion commercial real-estate portfolio. In an email, Mr. Moser said only some of the deals involved Mr. Morgan. He said he stopped doing business with Mr. Morgan ahead of the FBI probe.

Asked about Mr. Moser's account, Mr. Morgan replied that it wasn't correct, but declined to elaborate.

Mr. Moser hasn't been accused of any wrongdoing. He said his attorneys asked the Justice Department whether his transactions were being reviewed and were "told just the opposite."

Barbara Burns, a spokeswoman for the U.S. attorney pursuing the probe, said that, in general, "that is a question that we would decline to answer."

Investigators also have examined properties owned by Thomas Masaschi of DHD Ventures and Brett Fitzpatrick of Somerset Companies, both of whom have sometimes invested with Mr. Morgan and worked with Mr. Giacobbe, according to documents and people familiar with their deals. Another developer whose properties have been scrutinized, Matthew Cherry of Glendale Development, used to work at Mr. Giacobbe's Aurora Capital Advisors.

Like Mr. Moser, the three men haven't been accused of wrongdoing, and it is possible investigators are interested not in the property owners but in other parties involved.

Mr. Cherry said, "We do not believe Glendale is involved in the matter," and "I have not been associated with Aurora in more than 5 years." Mr. Fitzpatrick declined to comment. Mr. Masaschi didn't respond to requests for comment.

Delinquencies on multifamily-housing loans have been minimal. Still, investors can take a hit if properties produce less than expected. That appears likely in one deal that caught the interest of federal investigators: Monarch 815, a 176-unit student-housing complex near East Tennessee State University, which was developed by Mr. Masaschi's firm.

When the firm obtained a $31.9 million mortgage on it in 2015, financial information provided to lenders suggested the complex could have net income of about $2.7 million annually, according to Trepp. It earned only $804,266 in 2017, Trepp data show.

The loan, packaged into securities, is in foreclosure, according to Trepp. Mr. Masaschi, a managing partner at his firm, didn't respond to written questions.

Two Morgan-linked mortgages totaling $45 million were transferred to a special servicer for handling, according to disclosures the servicer filed last month. It said they face "imminent default."

Once-Hot Lithium Attracts Bears

By Amrith Ramkumar 
437 words
16 August 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
B11
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

One of the hottest investments of 2017 is now a big short.

Investors have reaped gains this year from short bets on companies that produce lithium -- a commodity used in the batteries that power electric vehicles and smartphones.

Lithium prices soared last year on expectations that growth in demand would lead to a shortage in supply.

But so far in 2018, stocks of lithium companies have tumbled after Morgan Stanley raised concerns that producers will ramp up output too quickly.

Investors who had short bets have been rewarded by the slump. Short bets against eight of the most actively traded lithium companies already have generated more than $550 million in profits this year, according to an analysis by financial technology and analytics firm S3 Partners based on stock prices through Monday.

"It's been a home run trade -- there's no question about it," said Chris Berry, founder of House Mountain Partners LLC, a New York-based adviser to battery-metals companies and investors.

Bullish projections for consumption made lithium and cobalt two of the market's darlings last year, but sentiment quickly turned after Morgan Stanley called for oversupply in lithium in February.

Now, analysts say prices of materials for batteries have plunged in China, which dominates the battery market. Worries have intensified that changes to China's electric-vehicle subsidy policies will lead to lower demand.

The Global X Lithium and Battery Tech ETF -- a popular ETF that allows investors to bet on the industry -- is down 19% so far in 2018. That is a reversal from its 59% rally last year.

Some of the largest producers in the world including Chilean firm Sociedad Quimica & Minera De Chile SA and U.S. firm Albemarle have tumbled more than 25%. Small lithium companies have fared even worse, in some cases shedding up to 70%.

Some companies and investors remain bullish on the lithium market: SoftBank Group Corp. and Tesla Inc. are among the large technology and auto companies that have recently done deals to secure lithium supply.

That hasn't dented the bearish outlook of some investors and traders. They are betting losses in the lithium market will continue.

Short positions make up more than 18% of outstanding shares of Albemarle and Australian firms Galaxy Resources Ltd. and Orocobre Ltd., according to data from IHS Markit. That is up from an average of 7.5% at the start of the year.

"There's a disconnect there for sure, and the question now is, 'What changes the sentiment?' " Mr. Berry said.

Letters to the Editor
Banks Will Lose Patrons' Trust Via Facebook

317 words
16 August 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Facebook is asking large U.S. banks for their customers' detailed transaction information ("Facebook Asks Banks for Customer Data," page one, Aug. 7). That may seem like a win-win. But there's a catch. In the financial industry, trust between a client and his or her provider is of primary importance. You can't sell a customer's banking data in the same way you sell his or her internet surfing behavior.

We believe it is now or never to define the principles on the use of customer data. The World Economic Forum, working with the finance industry, believes the principles should be: Customers control their data, not the companies collecting them. If banks want to share them with social media or others, they should attain customer consent. And, to make consent meaningful, companies need to provide clear and accessible information; consent as a "tick-of-the-box" is meaningless. Finally, companies should allow customers to access, download, transfer and permit third parties to manage data about them.

In all of this, the line between customer and company ownership of data is who generates them. Personal info and credit-card transactions should be owned by customers. Insights derived from them by big data and proprietary technology should be owned by the financial-service provider.

The EU's General Data Protection Regulation and the California Consumer Privacy Act are part of a necessary start. It's now time for other legislators, including Congress, to put their chips on the table. If not now, it will soon be too late.

Kai Keller and Peter Vanham

World Economic Forum

New York

---

Count me out on releasing banking data to Facebook or any other social-media site. Why give hackers another shot at draining a bank account?

Charles H. Gessner

Marblehead, Mass.

Heard on the Street
Markets
Nielsen Should Get a Break From the Market; TV ratings firm has struggled as a public company but a breakup won't solve its problems

By Aaron Back 
572 words
15 August 2018
10:30
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

For ratings and data giant Nielsen Holdings , life in the public markets has proven difficult. A return to private-equity ownership makes sense, but the company shouldn't be broken up.

Activist hedge fund Elliott Management"https://www.wsj.com/articles/activist-elliott-management-to-push-nielsen-holdings-to-sell-1534131900" it has taken an 8.4% stake in Nielsen, worth around $660 million, and will encourage the company to look at strategic options including a sale. Private-equity buyers, stuffed with cash and eager to put it to work, have expressed interest, The Wall Street Journal reported Monday.

Nielsen has two segments a near-monopoly media and television rating business, which it calls its "watch" business, and a business that tracks purchases of groceries and other consumer goods, called "buy."

This latter business has been the weak spot. In July Nielsen "https://www.wsj.com/articles/nielsen-ceo-to-leave-at-end-of-year-1532603179" saw revenue decline by 5.4% from a year earlier in the second quarter in constant currency terms. Nielsen also "https://www.wsj.com/articles/nielsen-ceo-to-leave-at-end-of-year-1532603179", and that it is examining options for the "buy" business that might include a sale. In response, shares fell 25% in a single day.

Nielsen blamed disruption in the consumer-goods industry. Makers of packaged food and household products like tissues and cleansers are being hit by rapid shifts in consumer tastes and rising e-commerce alternatives to traditional retail. Elliott however believes Nielsen is also losing market share to smaller data provider IRI , due in part to underinvestment in data automation and analytics, according to a person familiar with the matter.

The truth is likely somewhere in between. Companies from Campbell Soup to Kimberly Clark are under pressure to cut costs, though at the same time they need market data more than ever to make accurate judgments about consumer trends. For many companies, this may mean they are simply ordering fewer categories of data, or opting for less frequent updates.

Nielsen's data is still the gold standard for the industry, and is far more international than IRI 's.

The next question is if it makes sense to hive off the "buy" business. One reason to keep them together is they have the same clients. To plan advertising campaigns, clients like Coca-Cola need to know not only what media is being watched, but also what products are selling and to whom. A breakup of the company would cause these natural synergies to be lost.

In addition, the "buy" business may not fetch a great valuation on its own. Private-equity buyers will be attracted to the steady cash generation of the lucrative ratings business.

Nielsen was owned by a consortium of private-equity firms, including Blackstone, Carlyle and Kohlberg Kravis Roberts , from 2006 to 2011. They had a mixed track record, adding debt and posting no annual operating profits until 2010, but also growing revenue by 31% over four years.

After listing in 2011, shares initially fared well but have fallen nearly 40% over the past 12 months. Until its rally this week on news of the Elliott stake, Nielsen shares were below the initial public offering price of $23.

Putting Nielsen in private hands would give the company some breathing room, but any deal should keep watch and buy together.

Write to Aaron Back at "mailto:aaron.back@wsj.com"

News
China's flourishing biotech start-ups look to be 'true innovators'

Henny Sender 
768 words
15 August 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
12
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

INSIDE BUSINESS ASIA

H an Yusheng, a Chinese entrepreneur with a background in biology, established Burning Rock to promote the early diagnosis and treatment of cancer, especially lung cancer, using next-generation sequencing-based diagnostics in 2014 with the help of venture capital firms including Sequoia Capital China.

Mr Han was an early mover in what has become among the hottest areas in venture capital: biotech. Last year, such start-ups, broadly defined, raised almost $40bn, while their financiers invested almost $12bn in the sector, according to data from McKinsey. While venture capital inflows into other tech sectors have slowed, $32bn has been raised and $7.8bn invested in biotech in the first half of the year, suggesting 2018 will break last year's record, the investment bank China Renaissance in Beijing calculates. International private equity firms such as General Atlantic and Warburg Pincus have also become big investors in the sector.

There are good reasons for the continued enthusiasm for mainland biotech. Many scientists believe the next generation of medical breakthroughs will come from what Kenneth Oye, director of the Program on Emerging technologies at the Massachusetts Institute of Technology, refers to as the blurring of the line between information technology and biotechnology.

Still, "the key question is whether Chinese start-ups can make the transition from being fast followers to true innovators," says Kevin Xie, co-founder of China Renaissance and head of its life science division, which tracks 200 biotech mainland start-ups. Mr Han says: "The gap is large with the US in basic research. But there is so much talent and opportunity in China. In life sciences, we can catch up. The technological gap with the US is disappearing."

Today, it is accepted wisdom that early detection and treatment for diseases such as cancer will come from applying artificial intelligence and machine learning to the personal health data of large numbers of people   and China has more such data with fewer constraints on access to it than any other country.

China does not have the same level of concern China is gradually building an eco-system modelled on the US. Among the prerequisites are government incentives and funding, appropriate regulation, human capital, vibrant venture capital, links between university research labs and the private sector, and adequate reimbursement for innovation, according to Ben Shobert, the founder of Rubicon Strategy Group and a healthcare expert at the National Bureau of Asian Research.

with regards to privacy as the US Moreover, Chinese regulators "are moving in a better direction," says Mr Han. This is in contrast to over a decade ago when the then head of the Chinese equivalent of the Federal Drug Administration, Zheng Xiaoyu, was executed for corruption. China is also improving its protection of intellectual property rights.

There is also a darker side to Chinese life sciences, however. China does not have the same level of concern over privacy as the US. If US regulators consider (as they do) that financial data jeopardises national security, the need to protect medical data on US citizens is even more critical.

In addition, there are far fewer constraints on human trials and the current scandal over vaccines in China is merely the latest in a series of such scandals.

These are among the reasons why the Committee on Foreign Investment in the US (Cfius) is increasingly targeting life sciences in its scrutiny of Chinese money going to the US. Indeed, such initiatives remain one of the biggest clouds on the horizon for innovation in the life sciences.

There are few sectors where the US and China are as closely intertwined as in life sciences. Cross border research and development deals involving Chinese biotech companies have risen by 70 per cent since 2012, according to Mr Shobert. Licensing is one key area of co-operation.

The pipeline was once just one way, with companies such as Pfizer and Celgene licensing drugs to Chinese companies such as Fosun Pharma. But in the past year China was the recipient of drugs in 27 cases, but in four others, mainland companies such as Wuxi Biologics and BeiGene licensed their products to companies including Celgene, data from McKinsey show. Moreover, many US pharma firms, including Eli Lilly, have venture arms in China that provide both funding and scientists to mainland start-ups.

Sadly, Cfius and politics mean more clouds over prevention and early detection of disease.

Pro Cyber
Cyber Daily: Medical Data in Hacker Crosshairs; Automating Security Tests; WHOIS Scared of GDPR?

1,858 words
